Refutation of the Illusion that Universal Substances Need a Subject
The author refutes the illusion that universal substances depend on a subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why universal substances do not depend on a subject, and how the author refutes the two reasons for that illusion.

Core Idea
Universal substances are self-subsistent; predication does not imply metaphysical dependence.

This lesson unpacks a subtle illusion in kalam: that universal substances (like the universal 'human') require a subject to subsist. Some thinkers mistakenly argue that because universals are predicates of individuals, they must inhere in them, or because they exist as mental forms, they need the mind. The author of the Matn refutes this by distinguishing different meanings of 'subject' and clarifying the nature of universals.

Guided Path
1
Understand the illusion
Identify the mistaken claim that universal substances need a subject, and note the two reasons given for it: essential predication and mental existence.
The illusion is based on a confusion between logical and metaphysical relations.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the refutation's structure: first distinguishing 'subject' in the metaphysical sense, then addressing the status of mental forms.
3
Grasp the two key distinctions
The author distinguishes between 'subject' as a metaphysical locus and 'subject' as a logical predicate. Also, mental forms are individuals (accidents), not universal substances.
This is the core of the refutation.
4
Apply the reasoning
Reflect on why the two reasons fail: essential predication is a logical relation, not ontological; mental forms are particular accidents, not universals.
The refutation shows that universals as such are not dependent on a subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وقد توهم) افتقار كلياً الجواهر إلى الموضوع، لكونها محمولات ذاتية للشخص، وصوراً قائمة بالنفس . وردَّ بأن معنى الموضوع ههنا غير موضوع القضية، ومعنى جوهرية الصور أنها إذا وجدت في الأعيان كانت لا في الموضوع، وأما من حيث حلولها في النفس الجزئية، فهي أشخاص أعراض لا كليات جواهر . لما كان معنى الموضوع هو المحل المقوم للحال، ومعنى الجوهر هو ما يقوم بنفسه لا بغيره كان استغناؤه عن الموضوع ظاهراً إلا أنه قد توهم اختصاص ذلك بجزئيات الجواهر دون كلياتها لوجهين : أحدهما أنها مفتقرة في الوجود إلى أشخاصها التي هي موضوعات لها لكونها محمولة عليها بالطبع . وثانيهما: أنها صورة قائمة بالنفس لا قوام لها من حيث هي كليات بدونها .
He said: 'And one might falsely imagine' that universals of substances depend on a subject, because they are essential predicates of the individual, and forms subsisting in the mind. And it is refuted that the meaning of subject here is not the subject of the proposition, and the meaning of the substantiality of forms is that when they exist in external reality, they are not in a subject. But as for their inherence in the particular soul, they are individual accidents, not universal substances. Since the meaning of subject is the locus that constitutes the state, and the meaning of substance is what subsists by itself not by another, its independence from the subject is apparent. However, one might falsely imagine that this is restricted to particular substances, not their universals, for two reasons: first, that they depend in existence on their individuals which are subjects for them, because they are naturally predicated of them. Second, that they are forms subsisting in the mind, having no subsistence as universals without it.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage addresses an illusion (tawahhum) that universal substances (e.g., the universal 'human') require a subject. The illusion arises from two observations: (1) universals are essential predicates of individuals, so individuals are subjects for them; (2) universals exist as mental forms, depending on the mind. The refutation first distinguishes the metaphysical subject (locus that constitutes a state) from the logical subject of a proposition. When universal forms exist in extramental reality, they do not inhere in a subject; they are the individuals themselves. When they exist in the mind, they are individual accidents (mental entities), not universal substances. Therefore, universal substances do not need a metaphysical subject. The author then reinforces that substance by definition is self-subsistent, so the independence of particular substances is clear; the illusion mistakenly restricts this to particulars, ignoring that universals, when considered as universals, are not in a subject.

Key Terms

موضوع
جزئيات
صورة
نفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that universals are substances that inhere in something, or that predication implies metaphysical dependence.
Correction: Universals as substances are self-subsistent; predication is a logical relation, not an ontological one. Mental forms are accidents, not universal substances.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why might one falsely imagine that universal substances need a subject?
A) Because universals are eternal
B) Because they are essential predicates of individuals
C) Because they are material objects
D) Because they are independent of the mind

Key Takeaway
Universal substances do not require a subject; predication is a logical relation, not an ontological inherence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Universal substances are self-subsistent, yet you may feel that your value depends on external validations. If something is truly a substance, it stands on its own. Where in your life have you made your worth dependent on a 'subject' other than Allah? What step will you take to affirm your independence in Him?

Why Universal Substances Don't Need a Subject
Refutes the misconception that universal substances need a subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that universal substances are independent of a metaphysical subject because 'subject' in logical predication differs from 'subject' as a locus, and that mental existence does not make them accidental.

Core Idea
Universal substances need no subject; the confusion is between logic and ontology.

This passage tackles a sophisticated objection: if universals are predicated of individuals and exist in the mind, don't they require a subject like accidents? The author clarifies this confusion by exposing two equivocations. By the end, you'll see why universal substances remain independent in the metaphysical sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two objections
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two reasons given for thinking universals need a subject: (1) they are predicated of individuals, (2) they subsist in the soul as forms.
These are the two pillars of the misconception.
2
Understand the first refutation: two meanings of 'subject'
The author says the first objection confuses the logical subject of a proposition with the metaphysical locus (mahall). Individuals are subjects only in the logical sense.
This distinction is central to kalam ontology.
3
Analyze the second refutation: substantiality vs. mental existence
The second objection is refuted by noting that forms are substances by nature when external; their mental existence is accidental, not making them dependent on a subject.
Mental existence does not change their essential definition.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the passage defends the independence of universal substances by clarifying terms and distinguishing levels of existence.
This reinforces the definition of substance as self-subsistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وقد توهم) افتقار كليات الجواهر إلى الموضوع، لكونها محمولات ذاتية للشخص، وصوراً قائمة بالنفس. وردَّ بأن معنى الموضوع ههنا غير موضوع القضية، ومعنى جوهرية الصور أنها إذا وجدت في الأعيان كانت لا في الموضوع، وأما من حيث حلولها في النفس الجزئية، فهي أشخاص أعراض لا كليات جواهر. لما كان معنى الموضوع هو المحل المقوم للحال، ومعنى الجوهر هو ما يقوم بنفسه لا بغيره كان استغناؤه عن الموضوع ظاهراً إلا أنه قد توهم اختصاص ذلك بجزئيات الجواهر دون كلياتها لوجهين: أحدهما أنها مفتقرة في الوجود إلى أشخاصها التي هي موضوعات لها لكونها محمولة عليها بالطبع. وثانيهما: أنها صورة قائمة بالنفس لا قوام لها من حيث هي كليات بدونها. ورد الأول بأنه غلط من جهة اشتراك لفظ الموضوع بين المحكوم عليه في القضية وبين المحل المقوم للحال، والشخص إنما يكون موضوعاً للكلي بالمعنى الأول دون الثاني. ورد الثاني بأن معنى كون الصور جواهر أنها في ذاتها طبايع إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت لا في موضوع، وأما من حيث حلولها في النفس الجزئية، وقيامها فهي من قبيل الأعراض الجزئية لا الجواهر الكلية.
He said: And it was imagined that universals of substances are in need of a subject, because they are essential predicates of the individual, and forms subsisting in the soul. And it was replied that the meaning of subject here is other than the subject of the proposition, and the meaning of the substantiality of forms is that when they exist in external entities they are not in a subject, but in terms of their occurrence in the particular soul, they are individual accidents, not universal substances. Since the meaning of subject is the locus that constitutes the state, and the meaning of substance is that which subsists by itself not by another, its independence from a subject is apparent, except that it was imagined that this is specific to particular substances not their universals for two reasons: first, that they are in existence dependent on their individuals which are subjects for them, because they are predicated of them by nature; second, that they are forms subsisting in the soul and have no subsistence as universals without it. The first was refuted by saying it is mistaken due to equivocation in the term 'subject' between that which is predicated in a proposition and the constitutive locus, and the individual is a subject for the universal only in the first sense, not the second. The second was refuted by saying that the meaning of forms being substances is that they in themselves are natures which, when they exist externally, are not in a subject, but in terms of their occurrence in the particular soul and their subsistence, they are of the category of particular accidents, not universal substances.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The author addresses a misconception that universal substances (kulliyat al-jawahir) are in need of a subject like accidents. The objection is raised in two parts, each answered. The first answer clarifies that 'subject' (mawdu') has two meanings: the logical subject of a proposition and the constitutive locus (al-mahall al-muqawwim). Individuals are subjects for universals only in the logical sense, not in the metaphysical sense of a substratum. The second answer distinguishes between the essential nature of substantial forms (their being substances in themselves, independent of a subject when externally existent) and their mental existence in the particular soul, which is accidental. Thus, the dependence of universals on individuals in the mind does not make them need a subject in the ontological sense. The two objections are respectively based on the universal's dependence on individuals for existence and its mental subsistence, but both are shown to rely on an equivocation.

عرض ('arad)
موضوع (mawdu')
كلي (kulli)
جزئي (juz'i)
محمول (mahmul)
محل (mahall)

Correction: The passage shows that 'subject' has two meanings—logical and ontological—and that mental existence does not undermine the substantial nature of universals.

Key Takeaway
Universal substances do not need a metaphysical subject; the apparent dependence is merely logical or accidental, not ontological.

The universal in the soul is an accident, not the true nature. Your thoughts about Allah are mental forms—accidents in your soul. Definition of Body and the Nature of Composition
Defines body and disputes al-Qadi on composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's definition of body as a substance receptive to division and the key dispute with al-Qadi regarding composition.

Core Idea
Body is defined by receptivity to division, not three dimensions.

This passage launches the discussion of bodies in kalam by establishing the fundamental definition of a body. The author presents the view of the mutakallimun that body is a substance receptive to division, then contrasts it with al-Qadi's position that each part of a composite is a body, not the whole. Understanding this definition and the dispute is essential for grasping later arguments about atoms, composition, and the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the flow and key terms.
The passage is dense and technical; take your time.
2
Identify the core definition
Locate the mutakallimun's definition of body: 'the substance receptive to division without restriction to the three dimensions'. Note how it differs from the ordinary language sense.
3
Understand al-Qadi's counter-position
Identify al-Qadi's claim: each individual atom is a body, not the composite. Note the argument that composition subsists in each part.
This is a key point of contention.
4
Follow the beginning of the rebuttal
The author starts to answer al-Qadi by stating that composition is a relation between two things, attributed to the sum. The passage cuts off, but you can see the direction.
The full answer is on subsequent pages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما المقالة الأولى) ففيما يتعلق بالأجسام وفيه فصلان: الفصل الأول: فيما يتعلق بها على الإجمال وفيه مباحث: البحث الأول: الجسم عندنا القابل للانقسام فيتناول المؤلف من الجزئين فصاعداً، لا كل واحد منهما، على ما زعم القاضي تمسكاً بأنه الذي قام به التأليف، فيكون مؤلفاً، وكل مؤلف جسم للفرق الظاهر بين المؤلف من الشيء ومع الشيء. لا خفاء ولا نزاع في أن لفظ الجسم في لغة العرب وكذا ما يرادفه في سائر اللغات موضوع بازاء معنى واحد واضح عند العقل من حيث الامتياز عما عداه، لكن الخفاء حقيقته، وتكثر لوازمه كثر النزاع في تحقيق ماهيته، واختلفت العبارات في تعريفه، وأدى ذلك إلى اختلاف في بعض الأشياء إنه هل يكون جسماً أم لا؟ فعند المحققين من المتكلمين هو الجوهر القابل للانقسام من غير تقييد بالأقطار الثلاثة. فلو فرضنا مؤلفاً من جوهرين فردين كان الجسم هو المجموع لا كل واحد منهما كما زعم القاضي تمسكاً بأنه جوهر مؤلف، وكل جوهر مؤلف وفاقاً، ومبني الصغري على امتناع قيام التأليف بالجزأين لامتناع قيام العرض الواحد بمحلين، بل لكل جزء تأليف قائم به وهو معنى المؤلف. والجواب: أن التأليف معنى بين الشيئين يعتبر استناده إلى المجموع من حيث
He said: As for the first article, it concerns bodies, and it contains two sections. The first section concerns them in general, and it contains investigations. The first investigation: The body according to us is that which is receptive to division, so it comprises the composite of two parts or more, not each one of them, contrary to what al-Qadi claimed, arguing that it is that which composition subsists in, so it is composed, and every composed thing is a body, due to the apparent difference between the composite from something and with something. There is no obscurity or dispute that the word 'body' in the language of the Arabs and likewise its equivalents in other languages is used for a single meaning evident to the intellect in terms of distinctness from others, but its reality is obscure and its concomitants are numerous, so there is much dispute in investigating its quiddity, and expressions differ in defining it, and that led to disagreement about some things whether they are bodies or not. According to the verified scholars among the mutakallimun, it is the substance receptive to division without restriction to the three dimensions. So if we suppose a composite of two individual atoms, the body is the sum, not each one of them, as al-Qadi claimed, arguing that it is a composed substance, and every composed substance agrees, and the minor premise is based upon the impossibility of composition subsisting in the two parts because a single accident cannot subsist in two loci; rather, each part has composition subsisting in it, which is the meaning of the 'composed'. And the answer: that composition is a meaning between two things, its attribution is considered to the sum in terms of
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on bodies (ajsam). It opens with the first investigation concerning the definition of body. The author states that body is 'that which is receptive to division' (al-qabil li al-inqisam), which encompasses the composite of two or more parts, but not each part individually—contradicting al-Qadi ('the Judge'), who held that the composite itself is not a body but each part is, because body is what has composition (ta'lif) subsisting in it. The author notes that although the word 'body' is clear in ordinary language, its precise reality is obscure. The mutakallimun define it as a substance receptive to division without restricting it to three dimensions. The passage then presents a hypothetical: if two individual atoms (jawharayn fardayn) are composed, the body is the whole sum, not each atom—again opposing al-Qadi. The argument for al-Qadi's view rests on the impossibility of a single accident (composition) subsisting in two separate loci. The rebuttal begins but is cut off: 'the answer: that composition is a meaning between two things, its attribution is considered to the sum in terms of...'. The lesson thus provides the definition and outlines the dispute, but the full response is incomplete on this page.

Checkpoint
According to the verified mutakallimun, what is the definition of a body?
A) A substance with three dimensions.
B) That which is composed of two or more atoms.
C) A substance receptive to division without restriction to three dimensions.
D) That which has composition (ta'lif) subsisting in it.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun define body as a substance receptive to division, rejecting the necessity of three dimensions and the view that each atom is a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The scholars disagreed on what a 'body' is, despite its apparent clarity. How many of your own beliefs about Allah and the unseen are based on assumptions you have never examined? Will you commit to investigating your creed with the same intellectual rigor as the mutakallimun examined bodies?
?
Al-Qadi argued that each atom is a body because composition subsists in it. But the mutakallimun corrected him, showing that composition is a relation between parts, not in each part. In your life, are there relationships (with family, community) that you mistakenly think exist solely within yourself, rather than between you and others? Where do you need to shift your perspective from individual to relational, in order to see the truth?
?
If even the definition of something as basic as a physical body is disputed among scholars, how much more careful must you be when forming opinions about Allah, the Hereafter, and the soul? Will you now seek knowledge with humility, recognizing that you may be wrong about things you take for granted?

Refuting Two Objections on Subject and Universals
Refutes two objections about 'subject' and 'forms as substances'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes two objections concerning the terms 'subject' and 'universals' by disambiguating their meanings.

Core Idea
Ambiguity in 'subject' and 'form' is key to refuting objections.

The passage addresses two objections that might arise when studying kalam: one about the term 'subject' (mawḍūʿ) and one about whether forms (ṣuwar) are substances. The author clarifies that these terms have multiple meanings, and the objections only apply to one sense. Let's read the refutation carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: two distinct refutations, each beginning with 'He refuted the first...' and 'And he refuted the second...'.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the key terms.
2
Identify the two objections
The first objection concerns the word 'subject' (al-mawḍūʿ) being ambiguous. The second objection questions whether forms (al-ṣuwar) can be called substances (jawāhir). Underline these key terms.
3
Understand the refutation strategies
For the first, the author shows that 'subject' has two meanings: the subject of a proposition and the substrate underlying a state. The individual is a subject only in the first sense. For the second, he distinguishes between the essence of forms (which are substances) and their existence in the soul (which makes them accidents).
Both refutations rely on disambiguation.
4
Apply to kalam
Reflect: how does this connect to defining the subject of kalam? The clarification prevents misunderstandings about what a 'subject' or 'universal' means in theological discussion.
This is a common logical technique in Islamic theology: resolving disputes by clarifying terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد الأول بأنه غلط من جهة اشتراك لفظ الموضوع بين المحكوم عليه في القضية وبين المحل المقوم للحال، والشخص إنما يكون موضوعاً للكلي بالمعنى الأول دون الثاني. ورد الثاني بأن معنى كون الصور جواهر أنها في ذاتها طبايع إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت لا في موضوع، وأما من حيث حلولها في النفس الجزئية، وقيامها فهي من قبيل الأعراض الجزئية لا الجواهر الكلية.
He refuted the first by saying it is a mistake on account of the homonymy of the word 'subject' between the thing predicated in the proposition and the substrate underlying the state, and the individual is a subject for the universal only in the first sense, not the second. And he refuted the second by saying that the meaning of 'forms are substances' is that they are in themselves natures which, when they exist in the external world, are not in a subject; but in terms of their occurrence in the particular soul and their subsistence there, they are of the category of particular accidents, not universal substances.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage presents two refutations of objections that were presumably raised earlier. The first objection involved the term 'subject' (al-mawḍūʿ), which is ambiguous: it can mean the subject of a proposition (mawḍūʿ ʿalayhi fī l-qaḍiyya) or the substrate that underlies a state (al-maḥall al-muqawwim lil-ḥāl). The refutation points out that the individual is a subject for the universal only in the first sense, not the second, so the objection fails. The second objection concerned whether forms (al-ṣuwar) are substances (jawāhir). The refutation explains that they are substances from the perspective of their essence (dhāt) as natures existing outside the mind, but when they reside in the particular soul they are particular accidents (aʿrāḍ juzʾiyya), not universal substances. Thus the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawḍūʿ)
كلي (kullī)
صورة (ṣūra)
جوهر (jawhar)
عرض (ʿaraḍ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might mistakenly think that the subject of a universal is always a substrate.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'subject' has two meanings; the individual is a subject for the universal only in the logical sense (as subject of a proposition), not as a substrate.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the first objection regarding the subject?
A) By stating that the word 'subject' is unambiguous.
B) By differentiating between the subject in a proposition and the substrate of a state.
C) By denying that the individual is a subject for the universal.
D) By claiming the objection is based on a grammatical error.

Key Takeaway
The refutations rely on disambiguating key terms like 'subject' and 'form' to show that the objections only apply to a secondary meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read or hear an argument, do you ever assume a word has only one meaning and thereby miss the real point? Recall a specific time that happened. How could you apply the lesson of disambiguating 'subject' in your future reading?
?
The passage shows that what is true of a thing in one context (external existence) may not be true in another context (mental existence). Where in your own life do you confuse the way things are in your mind with the way they are in reality? What would it mean to let that distinction change how you think?
?
These refutations protect the coherence of a larger truth. Is there an objection you have held against a core belief of yours that might dissolve once you clarify the terms involved? Will you take the time to examine that today?

The Definition of Body (Jism) and the Grammatical Analogy
Al-Taftazani defines jism and part using a grammatical analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani defines 'jism' (body) as a composite in a specific sense, using a grammatical analogy to clarify the relationship between body and part.

Core Idea
The definition of 'jism' depends on distinguishing two senses of 'composition'.

Before diving into the controversy over composition, al-Taftazani first establishes what is meant by 'body' and 'part' through a clear grammatical analogy. This preliminary definition is not merely semantic—it sets the stage for the theological dispute that follows. In this lesson, we will unpack that analogy and the two senses of composition it introduces.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal English translation. Note how the author defines 'jism' immediately after presenting a grammatical example.
The passage begins with 'هو المجموع' which refers back to the definition of body.
2
Understand the grammatical analogy
Identify the analogy with Arabic grammar: speech is a composite with predication, and inflected words are composites that do not resemble base forms. Notice how the author applies this to body and part.
3
Distinguish the two senses of composition
Recognize that 'jism' is composite in the first sense (the whole composed of parts), while 'part' is composite in the second sense (the constituent that composes with others). Reflect on why the author says 'the middle is not repeated.'
The two senses correspond to 'composed of' and 'composed with'.
4
Connect to the broader dispute
Think about how this clear-cut definition sets up the objection about ta'lif that follows. Why might someone object that 'composition' is not just a linguistic notion but a real accident?
This step builds a bridge to the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو المجموع فيكون مؤلفاً من الشيء وإلى كل واحد فيكون مؤلفاً مع الشيء كما يقال في النحو الكلام هو المركب الذي فيه الإسناد والمعرب المركب الذي لم (۱) يشبه مبني الأصل، فالجسم هو المؤلف بالمعنى الأول والجزء بالمعنى الثاني فلا تكرر للوسط . فإن قيل : المراد بالتأليف عرض خاص مغاير لمعناه اللغوي المشعر بالانضمام المقتضى للتعدد وهو السبب عند المعتزلة لصعوبة الانفكاك. فالجواب : حينئذ منع الكبرى وجعل الآمدي (٢) النزاع لفظياً عائداً إلى أن لفظ الجسم بإزاء أي معنى وضع ، وصاحب المواقف معنوياً عائداً إلى أنه هل يوجد عند اجتماع الأجزاء وحصول الجسم عرض خاص هو التأليف والاتصال، والسبب الصعوبة الانفكاك على ما يراه المعتزلة أم لا بل الجسم هو نفس الأجزاء المتجمعة، فالقاضي يحكم بوجوده ، لكن يزعم أنه ليس قائماً بالجزئين كما هو(۳) رأى المعتزلة، بل لكل جزء تأليف يقوم به فيكون جسماً لما سيجيء من أن الجزء بمنزلة المادة، والتأليف بمنزلة الصورة، وفيه نظر لأن جمهور الأصحاب أيضاً قائلون به وبعدم قيامه بجزئين، وإن جعل النزاع بينه وبين المعتزلة بمعنى أنهم قائلون بالتأليف دونه ففساده أكثر لأن القاضي يقول بالتأليف وهم لا (٤) يقولون بجسمية الجوهرين .
It is the aggregate, so it is composed of the thing and to each one, so it is composed with the thing, as is said in grammar: 'speech is the composite that contains predication, and the inflected is the composite that does not resemble the base of the original.' So the body (jism) is the composite in the first sense, and the part is in the second sense, so the middle is not repeated. Then an objection: 'What is meant by composition (ta'lif) is a specific accident (arad) different from its linguistic meaning implying conjunction which necessitates multiplicity, and it is the cause according to the Mu'tazila for the difficulty of separation.' The answer: Then the major premise is denied, and al-Amidi made the dispute linguistic, returning to what meaning the word 'body' is placed; and the author of al-Mawaqif made it real, returning to whether when parts come together and the body is obtained, there is a specific accident of composition and connection, and the cause is the difficulty of separation as the Mu'tazila see it, or not, but the body is the same aggregated parts. So al-Qadi rules its existence, but he claims it does not subsist in the two parts as is the view of the Mu'tazila, but rather each part has a composition subsisting in it, so it becomes a body according to what will come: that the part is like matter and composition is like form. And there is an objection: because the majority of the companions also say it and that it does not subsist in two parts; and if the dispute between him and the Mu'tazila is taken to mean that they assert composition and he does not, then its corruption is greater because al-Qadi says there is composition and they do not say that two substances are a body.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer discussion about the definition of body (jism) and the nature of composition (ta'lif). It first gives a definition of jism as 'the composite' with an analogy from Arabic grammar: speech is a composite with predication, and inflected words are composites that don't resemble uninflected bases. Body is composite in the first sense (the whole), part in the second (the constituent). The objection argues that 'composition' (ta'lif) is a specific accident distinct from its linguistic sense of 'joining', and the Mu'tazila use it to explain why separation is difficult. The answer denies the major premise (that ta'lif is a specific accident) and notes that al-Amidi considered the dispute merely linguistic—over what the word 'jism' means—while the author of al-Mawaqif considered it real: whether an extra attribute of composition exists when parts join. Al-Qadi holds that composition exists but does not subsist in two parts jointly; rather each part has its own composition. This makes the part matter and composition form. The passage ends with a criticism that most Ash'aris also hold this view, so the intended dispute between al-Qadi and the Mu'tazila is mischaracterized.

Key Terms
جسم
تأليف
مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the grammatical analogy in the passage, what does 'speech' (kalam) correspond to?
A) The part (juz')
B) The body (jism)
C) The composition (ta'lif)
D) The inflected word (mu'rab)

Key Takeaway
The definition of 'jism' hinges on understanding two distinct senses of composition: the whole composed of parts and the part that composes with others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the composition of your own body as a whole made of parts, do you also reflect on how each part must cooperate with others to function? How does this awareness affect your gratitude to Allah for His perfect design?
?
This lesson distinguishes two senses of composition—one for the whole and one for the part. In your understanding of Tawhid, are you careful not to confuse the attributes of Allah with His essence (like confusing a part with the whole)? What step will you take today to refine your understanding?
?
The grammatical analogy used here relies on precise technical terms. Are you willing to invest the time to learn the precise definitions of Islamic creedal terms, even when they require careful thought? What is one term you will study more deeply this week?

The Dispute over Composition (Ta'lif): Linguistic or Real?
Al-Taftazani presents an objection and answer about the nature of ta'lif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that ta'lif is a specific accident, the responses of al-Amidi and the author of al-Mawaqif, and how al-Qadi's view fits into the broader Ash'ari consensus.

Core Idea
The dispute over composition is not just about definitions—it involves whether an extra attribute exists.

Now that the basic definition of 'jism' is established, al-Taftazani presents a significant objection: perhaps 'composition' (ta'lif) is not a linguistic concept but a real accident that causes cohesion. This leads to a deeper debate about whether the dispute is merely verbal or concerns an actual attribute. In this lesson, we follow the objection, the answers, and the final criticism that clarifies the positions of key figures.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection starting with 'فإن قيل'. Understand the Mu'tazilite claim that ta'lif is an accident distinct from the linguistic meaning and that it explains the difficulty of separation.
2
Analyze the answer and the two interpretations
The answer denies the major premise and then presents two views: al-Amidi considers the dispute linguistic (about the meaning of the word 'jism'), while the author of al-Mawaqif considers it real (about whether an extra accident exists).
Notice the difference between linguistic and real disputes.
3
Examine al-Qadi's position
Al-Qadi holds that composition exists but not as subsisting in two parts; rather each part has its own composition. This makes part like matter and composition like form.
4
Understand the criticism
The passage ends with a criticism: most Ash'aris also hold al-Qadi's view, so the intended disagreement with the Mu'tazila is mischaracterized. Reflect on why this matters.
This shows the importance of precise attribution in theological debates.

It is the aggregate, so it is composed of the thing and to each one, so it is composed with the thing, as is said in grammar: 'speech is the composite that contains predication, and the inflected is the composite that does not resemble the base of the original.' So the body (jism) is the composite in the first sense, and the part is in the second sense, so the middle is not repeated. Then an objection: 'What is meant by composition (ta'lif) is a specific accident (arad) different from its linguistic meaning implying conjunction which necessitates multiplicity, and it is the cause according to the Mu'tazila for the difficulty of separation.' The answer: Then the major premise is denied, and al-Amidi made the dispute linguistic, returning to what meaning the word 'body' is placed; and the author of al-Mawaqif made it real, returning to whether when parts come together and the body is obtained, there is a specific accident of composition and connection, and the cause is the difficulty of separation as the Mu'tazila see it, or not, but the body is the same aggregated parts. Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer discussion about the definition of body (jism) and the nature of composition (ta'lif). ?

The Theologians' Definition of Body: Why Width and Depth Are Necessary
Al-Taftazani explains the theologians' definition of body and the necessity of width and depth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why theologians insist that the definition of body must include not just length but also width and depth, and how this excludes lines and surfaces.

Core Idea
A body is defined by its ability to receive three dimensions, not just one.

The definition of body may seem straightforward, but theologians (mutakallimūn) debated its precise requirements. In this passage, al-Taftazani explains that body is defined by its acceptance of three dimensions—length, width, and depth—and why width and depth are essential to avoid confusing bodies with lines or surfaces. He also addresses an objection and reviews various theological positions on the minimum number of parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without interruption, then read the English translation. Let the author's argument unfold before you analyze it.
The passage is dense with theological terminology and sectarian views.
2
Identify the definition of body
Notice that the passage defines body by its acceptance of three dimensions (length, width, depth). Understand what 'acceptance' means in this context.
The dimensions are not necessarily fixed; the key is the possibility of receiving them.
3
Trace the objection and response
Find where the text raises the objection about the need for width and depth, and follow the author's response showing why they are necessary to exclude lines and surfaces.
The response involves different theological views on the minimal composition of a body.
4
Examine the sectarian views on parts
List the different minimum numbers of parts according to al-Naẓẓām, al-Jubbāʾī, Abū al-Hudhayl, and the fourth view. Understand why the triangular arrangement is rejected.
This shows the diversity of opinion and the rationale behind requiring all three dimensions.
5
Summarize the key requirement
Conclude that width and depth are necessary conditions because a compound atom with fewer dimensions (line or surface) is not a true body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالطول والعرض والعمق . بأن المراد قبول تلك الأبعاد وإمكانها، وهذه خاصة شاملة لازمة على أن ما ذكر من زوال مقدار وحدوث آخر مما لا يثبت له عند المتكلمين، بل الجواهر الفردة هي التي تنتقل من طول إلى عرض . ولو سلّم فالمراد مطلق الأبعاد وهي لازمة، وإنما الزوال للخصوصيات . فإن قيل : على تقدير نفي المقادير، فالطويل خاصة للجسم، وعلى تقدير إثباتها فالجوهر الطويل، فأي حاجة إلى ذكر العرض والعموم. قلنا : إنما يصح ذلك لو كان منقسم جسماً حتى المؤلف من جزءين، وهم لا يقولون بذلك بل عند النظام أجزاء كل جسم غير متناهية، وعند الجبائي(1) أقلها ثمانية، بأن يوضع أربعة بحيث يحصل مربع ثم فوقها أربعة كذلك، وعند أبي الهزيل (۲) ستة ، بأن يوضع ثلاثة ثم فوقها ثلاثة، وقيل أربعة بأن يوضع جزءان، وبجنب أحدهما في سمت آخر فرد واحد (۳) ، وفوق أحد الثلاثة جزء آخر، وإنما لم يفرض بالثلاثة على وضع المثلث والثالث على () ملتقاهما بحيث يحصل مكعب لأن جواز ذلك عندهم في حيز المنع لاستلزامه الانقسام على ما سيجيء : وبالجملة فالجوهر المركب الذي يكون عدد أجزائه أقل من أدنى ما يصح تركب الجسم منه أو يكون تركب أجزائه على سمت واحد فقط المسمى عندهم بالخط وفي سمتين فقط وهو المسمى عندهم (۱) بالسطح يكون واسطة بين الجسم والجوهر الفرد ويجب الاحتراز عنه بقيد العرض والعمق .
By length, width, and depth. That what is meant is the acceptance of those dimensions and their possibility, and this is a comprehensive, necessary property, given that what was mentioned of the passing away of one magnitude and the occurrence of another is not established according to the theologians; rather, individual atoms are what transfer from length to width. And even if it were granted, what is meant is absolute dimensions and they are necessary, and the passing away is only of particularities. For if it is said: On the supposition of negating magnitudes, then length is a property of the body; and on the supposition of affirming them, then the long atom (jawhar ṭawīl) [is the subject]; so what need is there to mention width and depth? We say: That would only be valid if it were divisible into a body, even if composed of two parts; but they do not say that. Rather, according to al-Naẓẓām, the parts of every body are infinite; according to al-Jubbāʾī (1), the minimum is eight—by placing four to form a square, then four above them likewise; according to Abū al-Hudhayl (2), six—by placing three, then three above them; and it is said four—by placing two parts, and beside one of them in another direction a single individual (3), and above one of the three another part. And the reason it was not supposed with three in a triangular arrangement and the third at () their meeting point so as to form a cube is that the permissibility of that according to them is in the realm of prohibition because it entails division, as will come. In summary: the compound atom (jawhar murakkab) whose number of parts is less than the minimum from which the composition of a body is valid, or whose parts are arranged in only one direction—called by them a line—and in only two directions—called by them a surface—is an intermediate between body and the individual atom; and it is necessary to guard against it by the condition of width and depth.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is an extended discussion about the definition of body (jism) according to the theologians (mutakallimūn). It begins by explaining that the properties of length, width, and depth are meant as the acceptance and possibility of those dimensions, not as actual fixed extensions. The author clarifies that for theologians, these dimensions are necessary and comprehensive properties of body, and that the passing away of one magnitude and occurrence of another is not something they affirm; rather, individual atoms (jawāhir fard) transfer from length to width. The text then raises an objection: if magnitudes are negated, then length is a property of body; if affirmed, then the subject is the 'long atom'—so why mention width and depth? The answer explains that this objection would only hold if a body were divisible even from two parts, but theologians hold different views on the minimal parts constituting a body. Al-Naẓẓām holds parts are infinite; al-Jubbāʾī says the minimum is eight arranged as a cube; Abū al-Hudhayl says six; and another view says four. The arrangement of three (triangular) is rejected because it would require division. The summary concludes that a compound atom with fewer than the minimal number of parts, or arranged only in one direction (line) or two directions (surface), is intermediate between body and individual atom, and must be excluded from the definition by requiring width and depth. Thus, the definition of body must include all three dimensions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Body can be defined solely by length, and width and depth are redundant.
Correction: Width and depth are necessary to exclude entities that have only one dimension (line) or two dimensions (surface); otherwise, those would be considered bodies.

Checkpoint
Why do the theologians require the condition of width and depth in the definition of body?
A) Because length alone is insufficient to differentiate body from line or surface.
B) Because width and depth are actual dimensions present in all bodies.
C) Because atoms cannot have length.
D) Because the philosophers defined body without depth.

Key Takeaway
The true definition of body requires not just length but also width and depth, as these comprehensive dimensions distinguish bodies from lines and surfaces.

?
The passage shows that a surface (two dimensions) is not a body. Are there areas of your spiritual life where you have settled for a 'surface-level' understanding, avoiding the depth that a fuller pursuit would require? What would it mean to commit to that depth starting now?
?
Theologians debated the minimum number of atoms needed to form a body, rejecting easy answers. Is there a truth you have been avoiding because it requires more complexity than you want? Are you willing to do the hard work of examining it, even if it challenges your current views?

The Philosophers' Definition of Body
Al-Taftazani presents the philosophers' definition of body as substance with three imaginary dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' definition of body as substance with three dimensions and the nuances regarding the meaning of dimensions.

Core Idea
A body, for philosophers, is a substance in which three imaginary dimensions can be imagined.

This lesson explores the precise definition of body according to the philosophers (falāsifa). Al-Taftazani presents their view that a body is a substance that can receive three dimensions. He then clarifies what is meant by 'dimensions' and addresses potential objections. Understanding this definition is essential for grasping later theological debates about the nature of the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its restriction
Read the passage carefully. Note the core definition: body is a substance with possible three dimensions. Also note the optional restriction of intersection at right angles.
The restriction is for verification, not essential.
2
Understand the clarification of dimensions
The author specifies that dimensions mean imaginary lines inside the body, not actual extensions. Compare with celestial and elemental bodies.
This prevents misunderstanding about the nature of dimensions.
3
Examine the objection and final point
The passage addresses an objection about the soul and imagination, then concludes that the subject is body, not hyle.
This ensures the definition applies only to body.
4
Review and test
Use the checkpoint to confirm your grasp of the definition.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وعند الفلاسفة) [ هو الجوهر الذي يمكن أن يفرض فيه أبعاد ثلاثة وقد يقيد بالتقاطع على زوايا قوائم وهو للتحقيق، ودفع الوهم دون الاحتراز. (۲) والمراد بالأبعاد ههنا الخطوط المتوهمة في داخل الجسم لا امتدادات حاصلة بالفعل لازمة كما في الفلكيات ، أو غير لازمة بخصوصياتها كما في العنصريات ولا النهايات المنفية من الجسم الغير المتناهي. والمراد قبول أعيانها لا صورها العقلية أو الوهمية، فلا يرد النفس ولا الوهم على أن الجوهر لا يشمله، ثم لاخفاء في أن المتصف بها هو الجسم لا الهيولي]. التعريف السابق هو الذي ذكره قدماء الفلاسفة.
He said: (And according to the philosophers) [It is the substance in which three dimensions can be imagined; and it is sometimes restricted by intersection at right angles, and that is for verification and repelling illusion, not for avoidance. And what is meant by dimensions here are the imaginary lines inside the body, not actual necessary extensions as in celestial bodies, nor non-necessary in their particularities as in elemental bodies, nor the negated ends of the infinite body. And what is meant is the acceptance of their essences, not their mental or imaginary forms; so neither the soul nor the imagination is relevant to the objection that substance does not encompass them. Then it is no secret that what is qualified by them is the body, not the hyle]. The previous definition is that mentioned by the ancient philosophers.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' (falāsifa) definition of body. It begins by quoting their definition: 'It is the substance (jawhar) in which three dimensions can be imagined.' The author notes that the definition is sometimes restricted by adding 'intersecting at right angles,' but this is for precision and to avoid illusion, not as an essential condition. Next, the meaning of 'dimensions' is clarified: they are the imaginary lines within the body, not actual extensions that are necessary (as in celestial bodies) or non-necessary in their particularities (as in elemental bodies), nor the negated ends of an infinite body. The acceptance intended is of the essences of these dimensions themselves, not their mental or imaginary forms; thus the objection that substance does not encompass soul or imagination does not apply. Finally, it is clear that the subject qualified by these dimensions is the body, not the hyle (prime matter). The passage ends by noting that this is the definition given by the ancient philosophers.

The Philosophical Definition of Body
Definition of body with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core philosophical definition of body as a substance capable of three dimensions and the role of the right-angle condition.

Core Idea
A body is defined by its capacity for three mutually perpendicular dimensions.

The philosophers define body not by what it actually is (e.g., having length, width, depth at every moment) but by what it can have: three dimensions. This definition distinguishes body from a line (one dimension), a surface (two dimensions), and the 'teaching body' (a mathematical abstraction). The passage presents the definition and explains why the condition of intersection at right angles is added—for verification and to repel the illusion that a surface could be considered a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage from 'قال وعند الفلاسفة هو الجوهر الذي يمكن أن يفرض فيه أبعاد ثلاثة' up to 'وهو للتحقيق، ودفع الوهم دون الاحتراز'. Notice the key terms: الجوهر (substance), يمكن أن يفرض (can be supposed), أبعاد ثلاثة (three dimensions), and التقاطع على زوايا قائمة (intersection at right angles).
The definition uses 'possibility' (imkān) and 'supposition' (farḍ), not actual existence.
2
Identify the two components of the definition
First, body is a substance (jawhar) – this excludes the 'teaching body' (jism taʿlīmī) which is not a substance. Second, it can have three dimensions. This capacity, not actuality, is what matters.
The condition of intersection at right angles is added by some philosophers for further precision.
3
Understand the purpose of the right-angle condition
The passage says this condition is 'for verification and repelling illusion, not for avoidance'. It ensures that a surface (which can only accept two dimensions) is not mistakenly considered a body, because a surface cannot have three mutually perpendicular dimensions.
The condition also helps in defining body uniquely as distinct from other extended entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجسم منه أو يكون تركب أجزائه على سمت واحد فقط المسمى عندهم بالخط وفي سمتين فقط وهو المسمى عندهم بالسطح يكون واسطة بين الجسم والجوهر الفرد ويجب الاحتراز عنه بقيد العرض والعمق. قال وعند الفلاسفة هو الجوهر الذي يمكن أن يفرض فيه أبعاد ثلاثة وقد يقيد بالتقاطع على زوايا قوائم وهو للتحقيق، ودفع الوهم دون الاحتراز. والمراد بالأبعاد ههنا الخطوط المتوهمة في داخل الجسم لا امتدادات حاصلة بالفعل لازمة كما في الفلكيات، أو غير لازمة بخصوصياتها كما في العنصريات ولا النهايات المنفية من الجسم الغير المتناهي. والمراد قبول أعيانها لا صورها العقلية أو الوهمية، فلا يرد النفس ولا الوهم على أن الجوهر لا يشمله، ثم لاخفاء في أن المتصف بها هو الجسم لا الهيولي. التعريف السابق هو الذي ذكره قدماء الفلاسفة. وحين ورد على ظاهره أنه لا بد من ذكر الجوهر احترازاً عن الجسم التعليمي، وأنه لاعبرة بوجود الأبعاد بالفعل، صرح أرسطو وشيعته بالمقصود، فقالوا : هو الجوهر القابل للأبعاد الثلاثة، أي الذي يمكن أن يفرض فيه أبعاد ثلاثة وزاد بعضهم قيد التقاطع على زوايا قائمة . ومعنى ذلك أنه إذا قام خط على آخر فإن كان قائماً عليه أي غير مائل إلى أحد جانبيه . فالزاويتان الحادثتان تكونان متساويتين ، وتسميان قائمتين، وإن كان مائلاً فلا محالة تكون إحدى الزاويتين أصغر وتسمى حادة، والأخرى أعظم وتسمى منفرجة. فإذا فرضنا في الجسم بعداً كيف اتفق ، ثم آخر يقاطعه في أي جهة شئنا بحيث تحصل أربع قوائم ثم ثالثاً يقاطعهما بحيث تحصل منه بالنسبة إلى كل من الأولين أربع قوائم. وهذا الثالث متعين لا يتصور فيه التعدد، فهذا معنى تقاطع الأبعاد على زوايا قائمة وهذا القيد لتحقيق أن المعتبر في الجسم قبول الأبعاد على هذا الوجه وإن كان هو قابلاً لأبعاد كثيرة لا على هذا الوجه، فما ذكر في المواقف أن الجوهر القابل للأبعاد لا يكون إلا كذلك . والذي يقبل أبعاداً لا على هذا الوجه إنما هو السطح ينبغي أن يكون إشارة إلى صحة التقاطع على زوايا قائمة لا إلى التقاطع، ولدفع وهم من يتوهم التعريف بالجوهر القابل للأبعاد شاملاً للسطح بناء على تركيبه من الجواهر الفردة وكان هذا مراد من قال إنه احتراز من السطح أي على توهم كونه جوهراً ، ولا يرد الجسم التعليمي لأنهم لا يتوهمونه، بل يجعلون الحاصل من تراكم السطوح هو الجسم الطبيعي لا غير. وقد يقال إن معنى الاحتراز عن السطح أن لا يبقى القابل للأبعاد شاملاً له فيصير خاصة للجسم صالحاً في معرض الفصل لصيرورته أخفى من الجوهر مطلقاً لا من وجه . وهذا إنما يتم لو لم يبق مع هذا القيد شاملاً للجسم التعليمي، وإنما اعتبر الفرض لأن جسمية الجسم ليست باعتبار ما لها من الأبعاد بالفعل لأنها مع بقاء الجسمية بحالها قد تتبدل كما في الشمعة وقد تزيد وقد تنقص بالتخلخل والتكاثف، ولأنه قد ينفك الجسم في ماهيته عن السطح، والخط كما في تصور جسم غير متناه بل وعن الخط في الوجود أيضاً كما في الكرة المصمتة ، والأسطوانة ، وذكر الإمكان لأن فعل
The body, from it, or its parts are composed in only one direction, which they call a line, and in only two directions, which they call a surface, being intermediate between body and the individual substance, and it must be guarded against by the condition of width and depth. He said: And according to the philosophers: it is the substance in which three dimensions can be supposed, and it is sometimes qualified by intersection at right angles, and this is for verification and repelling illusion, not for avoidance. What is meant by dimensions here is the imaginary lines inside the body, not actual necessary extensions as in celestial bodies, nor non-necessary in their particularities as in elemental bodies, nor the negated endpoints of the infinite body. What is meant is the acceptance of their essences, not their mental or imaginary forms, so it is not refuted by the soul or the illusion that substance does not include it, then there is no concealment that the one characterized by them is the body, not the matter. The previous definition is what the ancient philosophers mentioned. When it was objected that its apparent meaning requires mentioning substance to avoid the teaching body, and that there is no consideration of the actual existence of dimensions, Aristotle and his followers clarified the intended meaning, saying: It is the substance capable of the three dimensions, i.e., that in which three dimensions can be supposed, and some added the condition of intersection at right angles. The meaning of that is: if a line stands upon another, if it is perpendicular upon it, i.e., not inclined to either side, then the two resulting angles are equal and are called right angles; but if it is inclined, inevitably one of the two angles is smaller, called acute, and the other greater, called obtuse. So if we suppose in the body a dimension arbitrarily, then another intersecting it in whatever direction we wish such that four right angles are obtained, then a third intersecting both such that from it with respect to each of the first two four right angles are obtained—and this third is determined, multiplicity cannot be conceived in it—then this is the meaning of the intersection of dimensions at right angles. This condition is for verification that the considered aspect in body is the acceptance of dimensions in this manner, even though it is capable of many dimensions not in this manner; so what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif—that the substance capable of dimensions is only such—should be an indication of the correctness of intersection at right angles, not of intersection, and to repel the illusion of one who imagines that the definition by substance capable of dimensions includes surface, based on its composition from individual substances; and this was the intent of one who said it is a safeguard against surface, i.e., based on the illusion that it is a substance. But the teaching body is not refuted because they do not imagine it; rather, they make the result of the accumulation of surfaces as natural body and nothing else. It may be said that the meaning of safeguarding against surface is that the capable of dimensions should not remain inclusive of it, so it becomes a property of body suitable for being a differentia, because it becomes more hidden than substance absolutely, not from a certain aspect. This is only completed if with this condition it does not remain inclusive of the teaching body. The supposition is considered only because the corporeality of a body is not by virtue of its actual dimensions, because with the corporeality remaining as it is, they may change, as in a candle, and may increase or decrease by rarefaction and condensation; and because a body may be separated in its quiddity from surface and line, as in the concept of an infinite body, and even from line in existence also, as in a solid sphere and cylinder; and the mention of possibility is because the act...
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage defines the philosophical concept of body (al-jism) and explains its conditions. It begins by noting that a body's parts may be arranged along one dimension (a line) or two (a surface), but a proper body requires three dimensions. The author then presents the definition of the ancient philosophers: a substance in which three dimensions can be supposed, sometimes qualified by intersection at right angles. This qualification is for verification and to dispel the illusion that a surface could be considered a body. The dimensions intended here are the imagined lines inside the body, not actual extensions (as in celestial bodies) or non-necessary ones (as in elemental bodies), nor the negated endpoints of an infinite body. The acceptance is of the essences of these dimensions, not their mental images. Next, Aristotle and his followers clarified that the definition must include 'substance' to exclude the 'teaching body' (a mathematical body) and must use 'possible' because actual dimensions can change (e.g., a candle changes shape). Some added the condition that the three dimensions intersect at right angles. The author then explains the meaning of right-angle intersection: a line standing perpendicular on another produces equal angles; an inclined line produces acute and obtuse angles. For a body, we suppose one dimension arbitrarily, then a second perpendicular to it, and a third perpendicular to both—this third is uniquely determined. This condition ensures that the body can have dimensions in this specific manner, distinguishing it from a surface, which can only accept two dimensions. The passage further discusses that surface, if composed of individual substances, might be mistaken for a body, but the condition of right-angle intersection prevents this. The condition is not for avoiding the teaching body, as that is not imagined to be a substance. Finally, the author justifies using 'supposition' (farḍ) and 'possibility' (imkān) because the actual dimensions of a body are not essential to its corporeality—they can change, increase, decrease, or even be absent (as in a conceptual infinite body or a solid sphere without a line). Thus the definition focuses on the capacity, not actuality.

Key Terms
الجسم (al-jism)
الجوهر (al-jawhar)
الأبعاد الثلاثة (al-abʿād al-thalātha)
التقاطع على زوايا قائمة (al-taqāṭuʿ ʿalā zawāyā qāʾima)
الجسم التعليمي (al-jism al-taʿlīmī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a body requires actual length, width, and depth at every moment, so a changing entity (like a candle) would not be a body.
Correction: The definition uses 'can be supposed' (imkān al-farḍ) to emphasize capacity, not actuality. A body is defined by its potential for three dimensions, not by constant actual possession.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers as presented in this passage, what is the definition of a body (al-jism)?
A) That which has length, width, and depth in actuality at all times.
B) A substance that can have three dimensions, often qualified by intersection at right angles.
C) Any object composed of atoms (jawahir fard).
D) A line or surface with added thickness.

Key Takeaway
The philosophical definition of body focuses on the capacity for three mutually perpendicular dimensions, not on actual extensions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The definition says a body is defined by its capacity for three dimensions, not by what it actually has. In your spiritual life, are you measuring yourself by your actual achievements or by your God-given capacity for growth in faith? What would change if you focused on capacity instead of current state?
?
The philosophers added the condition of right angles to 'repel illusion' – to prevent mistaking a surface (two dimensions) for a body (three). What 'illusory surfaces' in your life – things that seem meaningful but lack depth – have you mistaken for true substance? How will you verify that your priorities have the 'third dimension' of sincere devotion to Allah?
?
If your body is merely a substance with dimensions, yet it will be resurrected and held accountable, how does this definition challenge you to treat your physical existence as more than just a temporary container? What concrete action will you take today to honor the potential your body has for obedience?

Dimensions, Right Angles, and Possibility
Clarification of dimensions, right-angle intersection, and justification of possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specifics of the three dimensions (as imagined lines, not actual extensions), their required intersection at right angles, and why the definition uses 'possible supposition' rather than actuality.

Core Idea
The three dimensions are imagined lines that must intersect perpendicularly, and the definition relies on possibility because actual dimensions are changeable.

The passage now dives deeper: it clarifies that 'dimensions' here refer to imaginary lines inside the body, not actual extensions (like those in celestial or elemental bodies). Then it explains the meaning of right-angle intersection in geometric detail and shows why the third dimension is uniquely determined. Finally, it justifies the use of 'possible' and 'supposed' in the definition, because actual dimensions can change, increase, decrease, or even be absent (as in a conceptual infinite body or a solid sphere without a line).

Guided Path
1
Grasp what is meant by 'dimensions'
Read the Arabic from 'والمراد بالأبعاد ههنا الخطوط المتوهمة في داخل الجسم' up to 'فلا يرد النفس ولا الوهم'. The dimensions are imagined (mutawahhima) lines inside the body, not actual extensions (imtidadāt bil-fiʿl) that are necessary (as in celestial spheres) or non-necessary in particular (as in elemental bodies). The body accepts the essences (aʿyān) of these dimensions, not their mental images.
This avoids objections that the soul or intellect cannot conceive of a body without dimensions.
2
Understand the right-angle intersection condition in detail
Continue reading from 'ومعنى ذلك أنه إذا قام خط على آخر' to 'فهذا معنى تقاطع الأبعاد على زوايا قائمة'. This explains geometry: if a line stands perpendicular on another, the angles are equal (right). For a body, suppose one dimension, then a second intersecting it to form four right angles, then a third intersecting both to form four right angles with each. This third dimension is unique (mutāʿayyin, no alternative).
This condition ensures the body is truly three-dimensional and not a surface (which can only have two dimensions).
3
Why 'possible supposition' and not actuality
Read the final part from 'وإنما اعتبر الفرض لأن جسمية الجسم ليست باعتبار ما لها من الأبعاد بالفعل' to the end. The reason is that actual dimensions can change (e.g., a candle), increase/decrease (rarefaction/condensation), and a body can be conceived without surface/line (infinite body, solid sphere). So the definition uses 'possible' (imkān) because the capacity is essential, not the actual dimensions.
The word 'فعل' at the end is incomplete; it likely indicates that the act of supposing is sufficient.

الجسم منه أو يكون تركب أجزائه على سمت واحد فقط المسمى عندهم بالخط وفي سمتين فقط وهو المسمى عندهم بالسطح يكون واسطة بين الجسم والجوهر الفرد ويجب الاحتراز عنه بقيد العرض والعمق. قال وعند الفلاسفة هو الجوهر الذي يمكن أن يفرض فيه أبعاد ثلاثة وقد يقيد بالتقاطع على زوايا قوائم وهو للتحقيق، ودفع الوهم دون الاحتراز. والمراد بالأبعاد ههنا الخطوط المتوهمة في داخل الجسم لا امتدادات حاصلة بالفعل لازمة كما أرسطو وشيعته بالمقصود، فقالوا : هو الجوهر القابل للأبعاد الثلاثة، أي الذي يمكن أن يفرض فيه أبعاد ثلاثة وزاد بعضهم قيد التقاطع على زوايا قائمة . ومعنى ذلك أنه إذا قام خط على آخر فإن كان قائماً عليه أي زوايا قائمة وهذا القيد لتحقيق أن المعتبر في الجسم قبول الأبعاد على هذا الوجه وإن كان هو قابلاً لأبعاد كثيرة لا على هذا الوجه، فما ذكر في المواقف أن الجوهر القابل للأبعاد لا يكون إلا كذلك . والذي يقبل أبعاداً لا على هذا الوجه إنما هو السطح ينبغي أن يكون إشارة إلى صحة التقاطع على زوايا قائمة لا إلى التقاطع، ولدفع وهم من يتوهم التعريف بالجوهر القابل للأبعاد شاملاً للسطح بناء على تركيبه من الجواهر الفردة وكان هذا مراد من قال إنه احتراز من السطح أي على توهم كونه جوهراً ، ولا يرد الجسم التعليمي لأنهم لا يتوهمونه، بل يجعلون الحاصل من تراكم السطوح هو الجسم الطبيعي لا غير. وقد يقال إن معنى الاحتراز الفرض لأن جسمية الجسم ليست باعتبار ما لها من الأبعاد بالفعل لأنها مع بقاء الجسمية بحالها قد تتبدل كما في الشمعة وقد تزيد وقد تنقص بالتخلخل والتكاثف، ولأنه قد ينفك الجسم في ماهيته عن السطح، والخط كما في تصور جسم غير متناه بل وعن الخط في الوجود أيضاً كما في الكرة المصمتة ، والأسطوانة ، وذكر الإمكان لأن فعل
The body, from it, or its parts are composed in only one direction, which they call a line, and in only two directions, which they call a surface, being intermediate between body and the individual substance, and it must be guarded against by the condition of width and depth. He said: And according to the philosophers: it is the substance in which three dimensions can be supposed, and it is sometimes qualified by intersection at right angles, and this is for verification and repelling illusion, not for avoidance. What is meant by dimensions here is the imaginary lines inside the body, not actual necessary extensions as in celestial bodies, nor non-necessary in their particularities as in elemental bodies, nor the negated endpoints of the infinite body. What is meant is the acceptance of their essences, not their mental or imaginary forms, so it is not refuted by the soul or the illusion that substance does not include it, then there is no concealment that the one characterized by them is the body, not the matter. The previous definition is what the ancient philosophers mentioned. When it was objected that its apparent meaning requires mentioning substance to avoid the teaching body, and that there is no consideration of the actual existence of dimensions, Aristotle and his followers clarified the intended meaning, saying: It is the substance capable of the three dimensions, i.e., that in which three dimensions can be supposed, and some added the condition of intersection at right angles. The meaning of that is: if a line stands upon another, if it is perpendicular upon it, i.e., not inclined to either side, then the two resulting angles are equal and are called right angles; but if it is inclined, inevitably one of the two angles is smaller, called acute, and the other greater, called obtuse. So if we suppose in the body a dimension arbitrarily, then another intersecting it in whatever direction we wish such that four right angles are obtained, then a third intersecting both such that from it with respect to each of the first two four right angles are obtained—and this third is determined, multiplicity cannot be conceived in it—then this is the meaning of the intersection of dimensions at right angles. This condition is for verification that the considered aspect in body is the acceptance of dimensions in this manner, even though it is capable of many dimensions not in this manner; so what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif—that the substance capable of dimensions is only such—should be an indication of the correctness of intersection at right angles, not of intersection, and to repel the illusion of one who imagines that the definition by substance capable of dimensions includes surface, based on its composition from individual substances; and this was the intent of one who said it is a safeguard against surface, i.e., based on the illusion that it is a substance. But the teaching body is not refuted because they do not imagine it; rather, they make the result of the accumulation of surfaces as natural body and nothing else. It may be said that the meaning of safeguarding against surface is that the capable of dimensions should not remain inclusive of it, so it becomes a property of body suitable for being a differentia, because it becomes more hidden than substance absolutely, not from a certain aspect. This is only completed if with this condition it does not remain inclusive of the teaching body. The supposition is considered only because the corporeality of a body is not by virtue of its actual dimensions, because with the corporeality remaining as it is, they may change, as in a candle, and may increase or decrease by rarefaction and condensation; and because a body may be separated in its quiddity from surface and line, as in the concept of an infinite body, and even from line in existence also, as in a solid sphere and cylinder; and the mention of possibility is because the act...
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage defines the philosophical concept of body (al-jism) and explains its conditions. It begins by noting that a body's parts may be arranged along one dimension (a line) or two (a surface), but a proper body requires three dimensions. The author then presents the definition of the ancient philosophers: a substance in which three dimensions can be supposed, sometimes qualified by intersection at right angles. This qualification is for verification and to dispel the illusion that a surface could be considered a body. The dimensions intended here are the imagined lines inside the body, not actual extensions (as in celestial bodies) or non-necessary ones (as in elemental bodies), nor the negated endpoints of an infinite body. The acceptance is of the essences of these dimensions, not their mental images. Next, Aristotle and his followers clarified that the definition must include 'substance' to exclude the 'teaching body' (a mathematical body) and must use 'possible' because actual dimensions can change (e.g., a candle changes shape). Some added the condition that the three dimensions intersect at right angles. The author then explains the meaning of right-angle intersection: a line standing perpendicular on another produces equal angles; an inclined line produces acute and obtuse angles. For a body, we suppose one dimension arbitrarily, then a second perpendicular to it, and a third perpendicular to both—this third is uniquely determined. This condition ensures that the body can have dimensions in this specific manner, distinguishing it from a surface, which can only accept two dimensions. The passage further discusses that surface, if composed of individual substances, might be mistaken for a body, but the condition of right-angle intersection prevents this. The condition is not for avoiding the teaching body, as that is not imagined to be a substance. Finally, the author justifies using 'supposition' (farḍ) and 'possibility' (imkān) because the actual dimensions of a body are not essential to its corporeality—they can change, increase, decrease, or even be absent (as in a conceptual infinite body or a solid sphere without a line). Thus the definition focuses on the capacity, not actuality.

Key Terms
الأبعاد (al-abʿād)
الخطوط المتوهمة (al-khuṭūṭ al-mutawahhima)
الامتدادات الحاصلة بالفعل (al-imtidādāt al-ḥāṣila bil-fiʿl)
التقاطع على زوايا قائمة (al-taqāṭuʿ ʿalā zawāyā qāʾima)
الإمكان (al-imkān)
الفرض (al-farḍ)

Checkpoint
Why does the passage say that the definition uses 'possible supposition' (imkān al-farḍ) rather than actual dimensions?
A) Because actual dimensions are impossible for bodies.
B) Because the body's corporeality remains even when actual dimensions change or are absent, so only the capacity matters.
C) Because the philosophers wanted to make the definition harder to understand.
D) Because the body cannot have three dimensions at the same time.

Key Takeaway
A body's essence lies in its capacity for three mutually perpendicular imaginary dimensions, not in any actual extensions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that a body is defined by its capacity for three dimensions, not by its actual state. What 'actual state' have you been using to define yourself, and how might you shift to see the capacity Allah has placed within you?
?
The condition of right angles excludes surfaces from being bodies. In your spiritual life, what 'surfaces'—areas where you are content with only two dimensions like outer actions without inner sincerity—are preventing you from being a complete 'body' of faith?
?
Dimensions can change while body remains the same (e.g., melting candle). What changeable aspects of your life (health, wealth, relationships) do you cling to as if they define you? How can you ground your identity in what is essential and unchanging before Allah?
