The Four Doctrines on the Simple Body's Divisibility
Classifies four doctrines on the simple body's divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the four classical positions on the nature of the simple body are derived from two key dichotomies: actual vs. potential division and finite vs. infinite.

Core Idea
Four positions come from just two questions: Is division actual? Is it finite?

Before we can argue about whether the body is composed of atoms or continuous, we need to map the logical possibilities. The author presents a precise classification using two distinctions: whether the divisions are actual or potential, and whether they are finite or infinite. This yields four positions, each associated with a specific school or thinker.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two distinctions
The passage uses two criteria: actual vs. potential division and finite vs. infinite. Grasp these concepts.
These are independent dichotomies that combine to form four cases.
2
Learn the four combinations
Combine the criteria to get four logical possibilities. Identify which school corresponds to each.
Use a 2x2 grid to visualize.
3
Memorize the four positions
First: mutakallimun (actual, finite). Second: al-Nazzam (actual, infinite). Third: majority of philosophers (potential, infinite). Fourth: al-Shahrastani (potential, finite).
These are the foundational positions for further debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكروا في ضبط مذاهب القوم في تحقيق حقيقة الجسم . أن الجسم البسيط أعني الذي لا يتألف من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع - إما أن تكون انقساماته الممكنة حاصلة بالفعل أو لا، وعلى التقديرين فإما أن تكون متناهية أو لا فالأول : مذهب المتكلمين والثاني: مذهب النظام. • والثالث: مذهب جمهور الفلاسفة. والرابع : مذهب محمد الشهرستاني. لكن لا خفاء في أن ما لا يكون جميع انقساماته بالفعل يحتمل أن يكون بعضها كذلك على ما ذهب إليه ديمقراطيس من أن الجسم متألف من أجزاء صغار صلبة قابلة للقسمة الوهمية، دون الفعلية. فلذا جعلنا الأقسام خمسة . وأما القول بتألف الجسم من السطوح المتألفة من الخطوط المتألفة من التي هي جواهر فردة ، فهو قول المتكلمين، مع اشتراط الانقسام في الأقطار الثلاثة ، بحيث لا يتألف من أقل من ثمانية أجزاء.
They mentioned in recording the doctrines of the people in investigating the reality of the body: that the simple body — meaning that which is not composed of bodies of different natures — either its possible divisions are actual or not, and in both cases, either they are finite or not. The first: the doctrine of the mutakallimun. The second: the doctrine of al-Nazzam. The third: the doctrine of the majority of philosophers. The fourth: the doctrine of Muhammad al-Shahrastani. But it is not hidden that that whose divisions are not all actual may have some of them actual, as Democritus held, that the body is composed of small solid parts divisible in imagination, not actually. Therefore we made the divisions five. As for the statement that the body is composed of surfaces composed of lines composed of what are indivisible atoms, it is the statement of the mutakallimun, with the condition of division in the three dimensions, such that it is not composed of less than eight parts.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage provides a more precise classification of the doctrines on the nature of the simple body (al-jism al-basit). It defines the simple body as not composed of bodies of different natures. Then it applies two distinctions: actual vs. potential division, and finite vs. infinite. Combining these yields four positions: (1) actual and finite (mutakallimun), (2) actual and infinite (al-Nazzam), (3) potential and infinite (majority of philosophers), (4) potential and finite (al-Shahrastani). The author then notes that a fifth position exists: some actual, some potential (Democritus). The passage concludes with the mutakallimun's specific view that the body is composed of surfaces, lines, and indivisible atoms (jawhar fard), requiring at least eight parts in three dimensions.

The Fifth Position and the Atomist Doctrine
Presents the fifth position and the atomist view of the mutakallimun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth position (Democritus) and the mutakallimun's specific atomist view requiring eight parts in three dimensions.

Core Idea
A fifth position allows mixed actual-potential divisions, while mutakallimun require eight atoms in three dimensions.

After the four logical positions, the author introduces a fifth: some divisions actual, some potential, attributed to Democritus. This leads to a detailed exposition of the mutakallimun's atomism, where the body is composed of surfaces, lines, and indivisible atoms (jawhar fard), with the condition that at least eight parts are needed to form a three-dimensional body.

Guided Path
1
Appreciate the fifth position
The author notes that a fifth position exists: some divisions actual, some potential. This is Democritus's view.
This breaks the strict either-or of the first two dichotomies.
2
Understand Democritus's atomism
Democritus held the body is composed of small solid parts that are divisible in imagination, not actually.
These parts are atoms, indivisible in reality.
3
Learn the mutakallimun's detailed atomism
The mutakallimun say the body is composed of surfaces, lines, and indivisible atoms (jawhar fard), with division in three dimensions requiring at least eight parts.
This is more specific than simple atomism.

ذكروا في ضبط مذاهب القوم في تحقيق حقيقة الجسم . أن الجسم البسيط أعني الذي لا يتألف من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع - إما أن تكون انقساماته الممكنة حاصلة بالفعل أو لا، وعلى التقديرين فإما أن تكون متناهية أو لا فالأول : مذهب المتكلمين والثاني: مذهب النظام. • والثالث: مذهب جمهور الفلاسفة. والرابع : مذهب محمد الشهرستاني. لكن لا خفاء في أن ما لا يكون جميع انقساماته بالفعل يحتمل أن يكون بعضها كذلك على ما ذهب إليه ديمقراطيس من أن الجسم متألف من أجزاء صغار صلبة قابلة للقسمة الوهمية، دون الفعلية. فلذا جعلنا الأقسام خمسة . وأما القول بتألف الجسم من السطوح المتألفة من الخطوط المتألفة من التي هي جواهر فردة ، فهو قول المتكلمين، مع اشتراط الانقسام في الأقطار الثلاثة ، بحيث لا يتألف من أقل من ثمانية أجزاء.
They mentioned in recording the doctrines of the people in investigating the reality of the body: that the simple body — meaning that which is not composed of bodies of different natures — either its possible divisions are actual or not, and in both cases, either they are finite or not. The first: the doctrine of the mutakallimun. The second: the doctrine of al-Nazzam. The third: the doctrine of the majority of philosophers. The fourth: the doctrine of Muhammad al-Shahrastani. But it is not hidden that that whose divisions are not all actual may have some of them actual, as Democritus held, that the body is composed of small solid parts divisible in imagination, not actually. Therefore we made the divisions five. As for the statement that the body is composed of surfaces composed of lines composed of what are indivisible atoms, it is the statement of the mutakallimun, with the condition of division in the three dimensions, such that it is not composed of less than eight parts.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage provides a more precise classification of the doctrines on the nature of the simple body (al-jism al-basit). It defines the simple body as not composed of bodies of different natures. Then it applies two distinctions: actual vs. potential division, and finite vs. infinite. Combining these yields four positions: (1) actual and finite (mutakallimun), (2) actual and infinite (al-Nazzam), (3) potential and infinite (majority of philosophers), (4) potential and finite (al-Shahrastani). The author then notes that a fifth position exists: some actual, some potential (Democritus). The passage concludes with the mutakallimun's specific view that the body is composed of surfaces, lines, and indivisible atoms (jawhar fard), requiring at least eight parts in three dimensions.

Key Terms
ديمقراطيس
الأقسام خمسة
الجواهر الفردة
الأقطار الثلاثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun's atoms are completely indivisible, even in imagination.
Correction: The text says they are divisible in imagination (al-qismah al-wahmiyyah), but not actually.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the mutakallimun hold that the body cannot be composed of less than how many parts?
A) Four
B) Six
C) Eight
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun's atomism requires at least eight indivisible parts in three dimensions, and Democritus's view offers a fifth mixed position.

What specific step will you take this week to engage in deeper tafakkur?
?
The passage shows that ancient thinkers differed on the nature of matter. How does the precision of Allah's creation—down to the atom—humble your own sense of knowledge? Are you willing to admit how little you truly understand, and turn to the Qur'an and sunnah with greater humility?
?
If even the smallest particle is under Allah's command and cannot be divided beyond a certain limit, how does that reshape your trust in His control over your life's most broken or tangled situations? What problem are you facing today that you need to surrender to His absolute power over atoms and hearts?

Three Schools on the Essence of the Body
Dismisses accidental view, lists three schools

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main positions on whether the body is composed of atoms, matter-form, or purely simple, and why the accidental view is rejected.

Core Idea
The body is not accidental; three schools offer the only plausible accounts.

The author first dismisses the view that the body is merely a collection of accidents as impossible. He then presents the three relied-upon schools in Islamic theology and philosophy: atomism (mutakallimun), hylemorphism (philosophers), and a third view of pure simplicity. This lesson will clarify these positions and the common ground among them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author moves from rejection to enumeration.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three schools
Find the three positions: atomic composition, hyle+form, pure simplicity. Note that the author says there is agreement on a substrate (hayula), with dispute on its identity.
The third school may be less familiar.
3
Understand the common ground
The passage notes that all agree on a 'hayula' (substrate). The dispute is whether this substrate is the body itself, matter, or atoms. See how the atomic view is close to hylemorphism.
This prepares for the classification in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجسم من محض الأعراض فضروري البطلان. والمعول عليه من ثلاثة . الأول : أن الجسم مركب من أجزاء لا تتجزأ متناهية . الثاني : أنه مركب من الهيولي والصورة. والثالث : أنه بسيط محض ، وكأنه وقع الاتفاق على أن هناك «هيولي» يتوارد عليها الصور والأعراض. وإنما النزاع في أنه الجسم نفسه، أو المادة التي تحل فيها الصورة، أو الجواهر الفردة التي يقوم بها التأليف، وإذا تحققت فالقول بكون الجسم من الجواهر الفردة والتأليف قريب من القول بكونه من الهيولي والصورة. ذكروا في ضبط مذاهب القوم في تحقيق حقيقة الجسم . أن الجسم البسيط أعني الذي لا يتألف من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع - إما أن تكون انقساماته الممكنة حاصلة بالفعل أو لا، وعلى التقديرين فإما أن تكون متناهية أو لا فالأول : مذهب المتكلمين والثاني: مذهب النظام. والثالث: مذهب جمهور الفلاسفة. والرابع : مذهب محمد الشهرستاني. لكن لا خفاء في أن ما لا يكون جميع انقساماته بالفعل يحتمل أن يكون بعضها كذلك على ما ذهب إليه ديمقراطيس من أن الجسم متألف من أجزاء صغار صلبة قابلة للقسمة الوهمية، دون الفعلية. فلذا جعلنا الأقسام خمسة . وأما القول بتألف الجسم من السطوح المتألفة من الخطوط المتألفة من التي هي جواهر فردة ، فهو قول المتكلمين، مع اشتراط الانقسام في الأقطار الثلاثة ، بحيث لا يتألف من أقل من ثمانية أجزاء.
The body [being composed] of mere accidents is necessarily false. The relied-upon [views] are three: First: that the body is composed of indivisible finite parts. Second: that it is composed of hyle and form. Third: that it is purely simple, and it is as if there is agreement that there is a 'hyle' upon which forms and accidents succeed. The dispute is only whether it is the body itself, or the matter in which form inheres, or the individual atoms from which composition arises, and if verified, the statement that the body is from individual atoms and composition is close to the statement that it is from hyle and form. They mentioned in regulating the schools of the people in verifying the essence of the body: that the simple body – meaning that which is not composed of bodies of different natures – either its possible divisions are actual or not, and in both cases either they are finite or not. So first: the school of the mutakallimun; second: the school of an-Nazzam; third: the school of the majority of philosophers; fourth: the school of Muhammad ash-Shahrastani. But it is not hidden that that whose divisions are not all actual may have some of them actual as Democritus held, that the body is composed of small, solid parts susceptible to imaginary division, not actual. Therefore we made the divisions five. As for the statement that the body is composed of surfaces composed of lines composed of which are individual atoms, that is the statement of the mutakallimun, with the condition of division in the three dimensions, such that it is not composed of less than eight parts.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage first declares false the view that body is composed solely of accidents. It then presents three reliable positions: (1) atomic composition (indivisible finite parts) – the view of the mutakallimun; (2) composition from hyle (prime matter) and form – the view of the Peripatetic philosophers; (3) pure simplicity – likely a reference to others. The author notes that there is agreement among all on the existence of a substrate (hayula) that receives forms and accidents; the disagreement is on its identity: is it the body itself, the material in which form inheres, or the individual atoms. The text then moves to a precise classification of positions on the simple body (i.e., homogeneous body). The classification is based on two binary distinctions: whether its possible divisions are actual or not, and whether they are finite or infinite. This yields four positions: (a) actual and finite – mutakallimun; (b) actual and infinite – an-Nazzam; (c) not actual and infinite – majority of philosophers; (d) not actual and finite – Muhammad ash-Shahrastani. The author then adds a fifth position from Democritus, who posits that some divisions are actual (small solid parts) but not all, as they can only be divided mentally. Finally, the specific view of the mutakallimun is explained: the body is composed of surfaces, which are composed of lines, which are composed of individual atoms, requiring eight atoms for a minimum body (three dimensions with two ends each).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the body is merely a collection of accidents.
Correction: The author explicitly says this is necessarily false (ضروري البطلان).

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three reliable (mu'tawwal 'alayhi) schools on the essence of the body?
A) Composition from indivisible finite parts

Key Takeaway
Three schools provide the only reliable accounts of the body's essence: atomism, hylemorphism, and pure simplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You spend every moment inside a body. Which of these three views—atomism, hylemorphism, or pure simplicity—best describes your intuitive sense of what your body is? What would change in your life if you discovered you were wrong?
?
The passage says there is agreement on a 'substrate' that underlies change. How do you deal with the fact that, on a fundamental level, the true nature of your body is debated? Does that uncertainty drive you to rely more on revelation or to dismiss the question entirely?
?
If the essence of your own body is a matter of scholarly dispute, how can you be certain about anything related to your existence? What does this teach you about the limits of human reason and the necessity of divine guidance?

Four or Five Positions on the Simple Body
Classifies simple body into four/five positions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of Islamic schools into four positions based on whether the simple body's possible divisions are actual and finite, and how a fifth position was added from Democritus.

Core Idea
The simple body's possible divisions yield four schools, with Democritus adding a fifth.

The passage now moves to a precise classification of positions regarding the simple (homogeneous) body. Using two binary criteria—potential vs actual division and finite vs infinite—the author presents four schools: mutakallimun, an-Nazzam, majority philosophers, and ash-Shahrastani. He then adds a fifth from Democritus, who held that some divisions are actual but not all. This lesson unpacks that classification and explains the mutakallimun's specific view of composition from surfaces, lines, and atoms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: two binary criteria leading to four positions, then a fifth, then a clarification.
The classification is systematic; try to map it in your mind.
2
Identify the two binary criteria
The classification uses two questions: Are the body's possible divisions actual or not? Are they finite or infinite? Combine these to get four possibilities.
These criteria are about the simple body (homogeneous).
3
Map the four positions
Match each combination to a school: actual+finite = mutakallimun; actual+infinite = an-Nazzam; not actual+infinite = majority philosophers; not actual+finite = ash-Shahrastani.
Note that 'not actual' means potential division.
4
Understand the fifth position
Democritus held that some divisions are actual (small solid parts) but not all, so his view doesn't fit the four pure categories. The author thus makes the divisions five.
This shows the classification is open to refinement.

Source Text

?

How does that awareness change your sense of dependence on Him in this very moment?
?

How does this idea challenge your complacent view that the world just 'is'? Are you living as if every instant is a fresh act of creation from Allah, requiring your constant gratitude and obedience?
?

Two Definitions of Body: Philosophers vs. Theologians
Defines body from two schools and contrasts their components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two classical definitions of 'body' (jism) from the Peripatetic philosophers and the mutakallimun, and the key difference between their underlying metaphysical frameworks.

Core Idea
Body is defined differently based on whether you believe in substance-form or atomism.

This passage introduces two competing definitions of the term 'body' (jism) that were central to classical Islamic cosmology. The Peripatetic philosophers define body as a substance composed of hyle and form, while the mutakallimun define it as a collection of indivisible atoms held together by an accident called composition. Understanding this difference is crucial for grasping later theological debates about creation, change, and the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the key terms: jism, jawhar, sura, hayula, jawahir farda, ta'lif, 'arad.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find where the author presents the Peripatetic definition (beginning with المشائين) and the mutakallimun definition (وعند المتكلمين). Underline or note the key phrases.
3
Compare the role of form vs. composition
Notice the comparison at the end: ta'lif is like sura but one is an accident and the other is a substance. Why does this difference matter?
This highlights a fundamental metaphysical disagreement.
4
Apply to your understanding
Ask yourself: Would these two schools agree on what makes something a body? What would each say about a piece of bread, for example?
Think about explanatory depth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشائين جوهر يتقوم بجوهر آخر حال فيه سمي صورة يتحصل بتركبهما فيه جوهر قابل للمقادير وسائر الأعراض هو الجسم، وعند المتكلمين هو الجواهر الفردة، التي يقوم بها التأليف، فيتحصل الجسم، فالتأليف عندهم بمنزلة الصورة عند المشائين، إلا أنه عرض لا يقوم بذاته بل بمحله والصورة جوهر يقوم بذاته، ويتقوم به محله الذي هو الهيولي.
The Peripatetics: a substance subsisting through another substance, and that state in it is called form, and by their composition a substance receptive of dimensions and all accidents is obtained, which is the body. And according to the mutakallimun, it is the indivisible atoms by which composition occurs, and thus the body is obtained. So composition for them is like form for the Peripatetics, except that it is an accident that does not subsist by itself but by its locus, while form is a substance that subsists by itself, and its locus, which is hyle, subsists by it.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage provides the technical definitions of 'body' (jism) from two major schools: the Peripatetic philosophers (mashsha'iyun) and the mutakallimun (Islamic theologians). The author begins with the Peripatetic view: a body is a substance that is constituted by another substance. That other substance is called 'form' (sura), and when the two combine, they produce a substance that is receptive of dimensions and all accidents—that is the body. Then he gives the mutakallimun's definition: the body is composed of indivisible atoms (jawahir farda). Composition (ta'lif) among these atoms results in the body. He notes that composition for the mutakallimun is analogous to form for the Peripatetics, but with a crucial difference: composition is an accident that does not subsist independently; it requires a locus (the atoms). In contrast, the Peripatetic form is a substance that subsists by itself, and its locus (hyle) subsists through it. This highlights a fundamental divergence in their metaphysical frameworks.

Key Terms
الجسم
الجوهر
الصورة
الهيولي
الجواهر الفردة
التأليف
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the two schools' definitions of 'body' are essentially the same, just worded differently.
Correction: The passage clarifies that they differ fundamentally: the philosophers posit a substance (form) as a constituent, while the theologians treat the binding element (composition) as a mere accident, not a substance. This reflects different metaphysical frameworks.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the fundamental difference between the composition (ta'lif) of the mutakallimun and the form (sura) of the Peripatetics?
A) Composition is a substance, while form is an accident.
B) Composition is an accident that subsists by itself, while form needs a locus.
C) Composition is an accident that does not subsist by itself, while form is a substance that subsists by itself.
D) Both are substances that subsist by themselves.

Key Takeaway
The definition of 'body' differs between philosophers and theologians because they disagree on whether its principle of unity is a substance (form) or an accident (composition).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You usually think of your own body as a single, unified thing. But this lesson shows that how you define 'body' depends on deeper assumptions about reality. Are you willing to question your assumptions about what you are? What if your identity is not a substance but an accident held together by Allah's will?
?
The mutakallimun insisted that composition is an accident, not a substance. Look at your relationships, your health, your possessions—are you treating them as substances (permanent, self-subsisting) when they are really accidents (dependent on Allah’s constant preservation)? How would your gratitude change if you remembered they could vanish in an instant?
?
Both schools have different principles of unity: form vs composition. What is the principle of unity in your own life—what holds everything together? Is it a job, a family, a belief? Could it be that like composition, it is an accident that does not subsist by itself? How does that thought make you rely more on Allah?

The Mutakallimun's First Way: Three Arguments for Indivisible Parts and Their Refutations
Mutakallimun's first way with three arguments and their refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's three arguments for indivisible parts and how their opponents (philosophers) responded to each.

Core Idea
If a body were truly one, division would annihilate its identity, so it must be composed of actual parts.

The mutakallimun start from the evident fact that bodies are divisible. They argue that this potential for division must already imply actual parts; otherwise, absurd consequences would follow. This lesson presents three such arguments, each followed by a philosophical response that challenges the assumptions about unity, identity, and distinctness.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage in Arabic once, then read the literal translation to get the gist. Notice the repetition at the end.
The text repeats the phrase 'للمتكلمين في كون الجسم من أجزاء لا تتجزأ طريقان أحدهما...' possibly as a structural marker.
2
Identify the three arguments
Find the three 'وجوه' (aspects) in the passage. Each argument is a conditional: if the divisible were one, then something absurd would follow.
The first argument concerns the division of unity itself; the second uses the sea example; the third involves distinct parts.
3
Study the structure of each argument
For each argument, note its premise and the claimed absurd consequence. See how the mutakallimun use 'ضرورة' (necessarily) to assert the conclusion.
4
Examine the responses
After the arguments, the passage gives responses. Note how each response undercuts a key assumption (e.g., unity as a mental consideration, distinction between water and sea, timing of properties).
The responses are introduced by 'وقد يجاب عن الأول...' etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طريقة المتكلمين في كون الجسم يتركب من أجزاء لا تتجزأ. المبحث الثالث: لا تتجزأ. في احتجاج الفريقين، أما المتكلمون فلهم طريقان: أحدهما إثبات أن قبول الانقسام مستلزم لحصوله وفيه وجوه. الأول: أنه لو كان القابل للقسمة واحداً لزم قبول الوحدة الانقسام، ضرورة انقسام الحال بانقسام المحل. الثاني: أنه لو كان واحداً لكان التفريق إعداماً، ضرورة زوال الهوية الواحدة بحدوث الهويتين، فيكون شق البحر بالإبرة إعداماً له، وإحداثاً لبحرين. الثالث: إن مقاطع الأجزاء من النصف، والثلث والربع، وغير ذلك متمايزة ضرورة، ولولا تمايز الأجزاء لما اختلفت خواصها. وقد يجاب عن الأول بأن الوحدة اعتبار عقلي لا ينقسم بانقسام المحل. وعن الثاني: بأنه إن أريد بالبحر ذلك الماء مع ماله من الاتصال، فلا خفاء في انعدامه بعارض الانفصال، وإن أريد الماء بعينه فليس هناك حدوث أو زوال. وعن الثالث: فإن اختلاف الخواص إنما لزم بعد فرض الانقسام. للمتكلمين في كون الجسم من أجزاء لا تتجزأ طريقان أحدهما: إثبات أن قبول الانقسام يستلزم لحصول الأقسام وتقرير الكلام أن كل جسم فهو قابل للانقسام وفاقاً، وكل ما هو كذلك فأقسامه حاصلة بالفعل لوجوه:
The method of the mutakallimun regarding the body being composed of indivisible parts. Third Investigation: Indivisible parts. On the argument of the two parties [philosophers and mutakallimun]: as for the mutakallimun, they have two ways. One of them is to prove that the acceptance of division necessitates its occurrence, and in it are three aspects. First: if that which is divisible were one, then the unity would be subject to division, necessarily because the state divides with the division of its locus. Second: if it were one, then separation would be annihilation, necessarily because the single identity vanishes with the occurrence of two identities, so splitting a sea with a needle would be its annihilation and the creation of two seas. Third: the sections of parts—half, third, quarter, etc.—are necessarily distinct, and if the parts were not distinct, their properties would not differ. And it is answered regarding the first that unity is a mental consideration that does not divide with the division of its locus. Regarding the second: if by 'sea' is meant that water with its continuity, there is no doubt about its annihilation by the accident of separation; if the water itself is meant, there is no occurrence or vanishing. Regarding the third: the difference in properties only arises after assuming division. For the mutakallimun regarding the body being composed of indivisible parts there are two ways. One of them: proving that the acceptance of division necessitates the occurrence of the parts; and the exposition is that every body is divisible by agreement, and everything that is so, its parts are actual, due to several aspects:
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage belongs to the third investigation (al-mabhas al-thalith) of the mutakallimun's physics. The author presents the first way (tariq) by which the mutakallimun argue for the existence of indivisible parts (ajza' la tatajazza'). This way is based on proving that the potential for division implies that parts are already actual. Three arguments (wujuh) are given. The first argument: if the divisible thing were one, then its oneness would itself be subject to division, because the state of a thing divides when its locus divides. The implication is absurd, so the thing must actually be composed of parts. The second argument: if the divisible were genuinely one, then splitting it would annihilate that one identity and generate two new identities, which is absurd; for example, cutting the sea with a needle would annihilate the sea and create two seas. The third argument: the boundaries of parts (half, third, etc.) are necessarily distinct; if the parts were not distinct, their properties would not differ. The author then gives the answers of the opponents (likely the philosophers). To the first, they reply that unity is a mental concept and does not really divide with the division of its locus. To the second, they distinguish: if by 'sea' is meant the water with its continuity, then indeed it is annihilated by separation; but if the water itself is meant, the water remains numerically one, so no annihilation. To the third, they reply that the difference in properties only arises after division is assumed, not before. The passage then repeats that the mutakallimun have two ways, and the first way (the one just described) is that acceptance of division necessitates actual parts, and that every body is divisible by agreement, so parts must be actual.

قبول الانقسام
الوحدة
الإعدام
مقاطع الأجزاء
الاعتبار العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun deny any kind of division in bodies.
Correction: They affirm division in potential; they only deny that division can proceed infinitely in actuality. They argue for actual indivisible parts.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the second argument of the mutakallimun (the sea example)?
A) To prove that the sea is one continuous body.
B) To show that if a body were truly one, then any separation would be a creation of a new body, not a mere division.
C) To demonstrate that water is indivisible.
D) To argue that the sea can be split with a needle.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun's first way argues that if a body were truly one, division would lead to absurdities, so it must be composed of actual indivisible parts; but opponents challenge the assumptions about unity and identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The mutakallimun argue that if your body were truly one, splitting it would annihilate you. But you are split every day—cells die, you grow, you change. Are you still the same 'you'? What part of you survives all splitting?
?
The opponents say unity is just a mental concept, not real in the world. If that is true, what does it mean for your conviction that your soul is a single, undivided entity created by Allah? Is your faith in your oneness just a mental habit?
?
The third argument says properties differ because parts are distinct. Reflect on your own character: are the 'parts' of your personality—your kindness, your anger, your faith—distinct or one? If you try to divide them, does something real get annihilated? How will you protect the unity of your heart for Allah?

First Proof and Response on Unity and Division
The first proof and its response for the mutakallimun's first way.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof of the mutakallimun's first way regarding the division of unity and the response that unity is a mental consideration.

Core Idea
Unity is a mental label, not a real accident that can divide.

This lesson presents the opening argument in a key kalam proof for indivisible parts. The proof highlights a contradiction if unity were a real accident, and the response shifts its status from real to mental. Understanding this dialectic is foundational for subsequent discussions on atomism.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Understand the conditional: if a divisible body is one, then the accident 'unity' would have to divide.
The key term is 'ḥāl' (accident).
2
Identify the objection
Note the response: unity is only a mental consideration (iʿtibār ʿaqliyy), so it does not really divide.
3
Reflect on the significance
This establishes the mutakallimun's strategy of using mental considerations to resolve apparent contradictions.
The distinction between real and mental attributes is crucial in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: أنه لو كان القابل للقسمة واحداً لزم قبول الوحدة الانقسام، ضرورة انقسام الحال بانقسام المحل. وقد يجاب عن الأول بأن الوحدة اعتبار عقلي لا ينقسم بانقسام المحل.
First: If the acceptor of division were one, the acceptance of unity would entail division, necessarily because the accident (al-ḥāl) divides with the division of the subject (al-maḥall). And it is answered concerning the first that unity is a mental consideration (iʿtibār ʿaqliyy) that does not divide with the division of the subject.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the mutakallimun's first way to prove that bodies are composed of indivisible parts (al-juzʾ alladhī lā yatajazzā). The proof argues: if a divisible body were actually one and undivided, then the attribute 'unity' inhering in it would have to divide when the body divides, because accidents divide with their subjects. But unity is inherently indivisible, so the premise is false. The response denies that unity is an accident that divides; rather it is a mental consideration that does not truly inhere.

Key Terms
القابل للقسمة
الوحدة
الحال
المحل
اعتبار عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity is a real accident inhering in bodies.
Correction: The response teaches that unity is a mental consideration, not a real accident that might be subject to division.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why does the acceptance of division by a unitary body lead to a contradiction?
A) Because unity is a mental consideration that cannot divide.
B) Because the accident (ḥāl) must divide with the subject, yet unity is indivisible.
C) Because the body cannot be both divisible and one.
D) Because division requires a plurality of substances.

Key Takeaway
The first proof argues that if a divisible body were one, unity would have to divide, but the response reinterprets unity as a mental concept that does not truly inhere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often treat abstract ideas like 'unity' or 'identity' as solid facts about yourself. But what if they are only mental constructs (iʿtibār ʿaqliyy) that you use to make sense of your fragmented experiences? Can you point to one such construct in your spiritual life that you have mistaken for a reality—and what would it mean to stop treating it as such?
?
This lesson shows that even in logic, dividing something does not necessarily divide its conception. When you feel torn between good and bad inclinations, do you assume that your 'self' is divided into parts? What if your core identity—the you that answers to Allah—remains undivided despite your internal struggles? How would that change the way you repent or strive?

Second Proof: Division as Annihilation and Its Response
Sanusi presents the second proof and its response about division as annihilation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that division implies annihilation and the response distinguishing between the continuous whole and the substance.

Core Idea
Division destroys the whole, not the substance.

This lesson presents a key argument in the debate over the reality of bodies: if a body is truly one, does any division completely annihilate it? Sanusi offers a second proof to show the absurdity of this view, then provides a nuanced response that saves the phenomenon.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compressed style of the proof and response.
2
Identify the proof's logical structure
The proof says: if a body is one, any division destroys that one identity and creates two new identities. This leads to absurd consequences like a needle splitting the sea annihilating it. Identify the conditional and the absurd implication.
3
Grasp the response's key distinction
The response distinguishes two meanings of 'sea': the continuous body of water (which is indeed annihilated by separation) and the water substance itself (which remains unchanged). Understand how this distinction answers the proof.
This distinction shows the proof only applies to the accidental continuity, not the substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أنه لو كان واحداً لكان التفريق إعداماً، ضرورة زوال الهوية الواحدة بحدوث الهويتين، فيكون شق البحر بالإبرة إعداماً له، وإحداثاً لبحرين. وعن الثاني: بأنه إن أريد بالبحر ذلك الماء مع ماله من الاتصال، فلا خفاء في انعدامه بعارض الانفصال، وإن أريد الماء بعينه فليس هناك حدوث أو زوال.
Second: If it were one, then division would be annihilation, necessarily because the one identity disappears with the arising of two identities; thus splitting the sea with a needle would be its annihilation and the generation of two seas. And it is answered concerning the second: that if by 'sea' is meant that water with its continuity, then there is no concealment of its non-existence upon the occurrence of separation; and if the water itself is meant, then there is no generation or disappearance.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This is the second proof of the first way. It argues that if a body is truly one, then any division destroys that one entity and creates two new entities. This would imply that splitting a sea with a needle is the annihilation of that sea and the creation of two new seas, which is absurd. The response distinguishes two senses: 'sea' can mean the continuous body of water, which is indeed destroyed when separated, or it can mean the water substance itself, which remains unchanged. Only the former is annihilated, which is acceptable.

Key Terms
إعدام
هوية
اتصال
انفصال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think any division destroys the substance itself.
Correction: The response shows that only the continuous whole is destroyed; the substance remains.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction in the response to the second proof?
A) Between real and mental existence.
B) Between the sea as continuous water and as water substance.
C) Between essential and accidental properties.
D) Between macroscopic and microscopic division.

Key Takeaway
The second proof is answered by distinguishing between the continuous whole (which is annihilated by division) and the substance itself (which remains).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the things in your life you identify as 'one' – your identity, your family, your community – are you attached to the accidental continuity or the substance? What happens when that continuity is broken? Are you able to see that the substance remains? How does this lesson challenge your fear of change or loss?
?
If someone asked you to prove that their individual self remains the same person despite changes in their body and circumstances, how would you use this lesson's distinction to answer? What does that say about your own understanding of your existence before Allah?
?
The proof's absurd consequence (splitting a sea with a needle) is used to show a logical flaw. Can you identify a similar absurdity in your own thinking about change and identity? What false assumption about unity do you need to correct today?

Third Proof: Distinct Limits of Parts and Its Response
Sanusi presents the third proof and its response about distinct limits of parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi's third proof argues for actual parts based on distinct limits of potential parts, and how the response counters that the difference in properties only arises after assuming division.

Core Idea
Distinct boundaries of potential parts do not prove actual division.

Sanusi's third proof attempts to show that parts must be actual because their limits (half, third, quarter) are necessarily distinct. But the response denies this, arguing that the difference in properties only appears after we assume the division. In this lesson, we'll examine the proof and its refutation closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and response aloud
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Let the argument's rhythm come through.
Notice the concise style of proof and response.
2
Identify key terms
Find the terms for 'divides of parts', 'half', 'third', 'quarter', and 'properties'. Understand their role in the argument.
3
Analyze the logical structure
The proof claims necessary distinctness of limits; the response says difference only after division. See how each step connects.
This is a classic objection-and-answer pattern.
4
Reflect on the implication
Why does this matter for the doctrine of atomism? The proof supports actual parts; the response preserves potentiality.
Consider how this fits into the broader debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: إن مقاطع الأجزاء من النصف، والثلث والربع، وغير ذلك متمايزة ضرورة، ولولا تمايز الأجزاء لما اختلفت خواصها. وعن الثالث: فإن اختلاف الخواص إنما لزم بعد فرض الانقسام.
Third: The divides (maqāṭiʿ) of the parts, such as the half, the third, the quarter, and others, are necessarily distinct; and if the parts were not distinct, their properties would not differ. And it is answered concerning the third: that the difference of properties only follows after assuming division.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This third proof argues that the boundaries of potential parts (half, third, quarter) are inherently distinct even before actual division, as shown by differing properties (e.g., a half has different location than a third). This implies that the parts themselves are distinct entities. The response counters that the difference in properties only becomes relevant after we assume the division has taken place; potentiality does not entail actual distinctness.

Key Terms

النصف
الثلث

خواص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The indivisibility of the atom.
B) The distinct limits of potential parts like half and third.
C) The impossibility of infinite regress.
D) The necessity of a creator.

Key Takeaway
The distinctness of potential boundaries does not prove actual parts; it only follows after assuming division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof assumes that because boundaries are distinct in thought, they must be distinct in reality. Where in your own thinking do you treat a mental distinction as an actual separation? Examine one belief you hold today: is it based on what is truly actual, or only on a possibility you've assumed is real? Commit to verifying one such belief this week.
?
The response teaches that properties only emerge after division. What 'properties' or judgments have you assigned to something that only exist because you have already assumed a particular outcome? How does this affect your decisions? Today, catch yourself treating a potential future as if it already has fixed characteristics, and deliberately remind yourself of its uncertainty.
?
You often divide your time and attention into 'parts' (work, family, worship). Are these divisions actually real, or have you assumed they are distinct when in reality they are interconnected? What would change in your life if you stopped treating them as separate entities and saw them as a single whole?

First Proof: Unity as Accident and Its Response
Sanusi presents the first proof and response of the mutakallimun's second way.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's first proof that a divisible body must be actually divided, and the response that unity is a mental consideration, not a real accident.

Core Idea
Unity is a mental consideration, not a real accident that divides with its subject.

Sanusi presents the first argument of the second way. The mutakallimun argue that if a body is divisible yet considered one, unity would have to be an accident that divides, which is impossible. The response shifts the ground by asserting that unity is a mental concept, not a real attribute that spreads across parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical flow: the conditional statement, the claim of entailment, and the justification.
2
Analyze the entailment reasoning
The proof claims that if the subject is one, its unity would be an accident inhering in it. If the subject divides, the accident would have to divide because the accident in each part would be different. Understand why this leads to the conclusion that the body must be actually divided.
The key point: the accident (unity) cannot be the same in divided parts.
3
Understand the response
The response denies that unity is a real accident; it is a mental consideration. Even if conceded, it is not an extensional accident (ʿaraḍ sārī) that divides with its subject.
This reframes the debate: unity is not a physical attribute but a concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: إن القابل للانقسام لو لم يكن منقسماً بالفعل، بل واحداً في نفسه كما هو عند الحس لزم قبول الوحدة الانقسام، واللازم باطل إذ لا معنى لها سوى عدم الانقسام. وجه اللزوم أن الوحدة حينئذ تكون عارضة لذلك القابل حالة فيه، سواء جعلت لازمة له أو غير لازمة ضرورة أنها ليست نفسه، ولا جزءاً منه، وانقسام المحل يستلزم انقسام الحال ضرورة أن الحال في كل جزء غير الحال في الجزء الآخر. وأجيب بأن الوحدة من الاعتبارات العقلية .. ولو سلّم فليست من الأعراض السارية التي تنقسم بانقسام المحل.
First: If the acceptor of division were not actually divided, but one in itself as it is according to sense perception, then acceptance of unity would entail division, and the necessary consequence is false, for it has no meaning other than non-division. The reason for the entailment is that unity then would be an accident (ʿāriḍ) of that acceptor, inhering in it, whether it is considered necessary or not necessary, necessarily because it is not its essence nor a part of it, and the division of the subject necessitates the division of the accident because the accident in each part is other than the accident in the other part. And it is answered that unity is from mental considerations... and even if conceded, it is not among the extensional accidents that divide with division of the subject.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This is the first proof of the second way. It argues that a body perceived as one but divisible must actually be divided, because the attribute 'unity' would have to be in each part differently if the subject divides. Since unity is indivisible, the subject cannot be truly one if divisible. The response repeats that unity is a mental consideration, not a real accident that spreads across parts.

Key Terms
قابل للانقسام
منقسم بالفعل
عارضة
حال
أعراض سارية
الوحدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity is a real accident that must divide with its subject.
Correction: Unity is a mental consideration, not a real accident. Even if it were, it is not among the extensional accidents that divide with the subject.

Checkpoint
What does the first proof of the second way conclude about a body that is one but divisible?
A) It must be actually divided.
B) It cannot be one.
C) Its unity is a mental consideration.
D) It is impossible for a body to be both one and divisible.

Key Takeaway
The first proof of the second way attempts to show that divisible bodies must be actually divided, but the response counters that unity is a mental consideration, not a real accident that divides.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate the unity of your own self—your body and soul—do you feel a real, indivisible oneness, or do you sense a composite that your mind unifies? How does Sanusi's argument challenge you to see yourself more honestly before Allah?
?
If unity is a mental consideration, then what does it mean for you to say 'Allah is One'? Is your belief in Tawhid rooted in a conceptual understanding or a direct certainty that transcends accidents? Let this question stir you to examine the depth of your faith.
?
The response distinguishes between real accidents and mental considerations. In your daily life, where might you be treating mental constructs (like status, wealth, or opinions) as if they were real, indivisible entities? How can you reorient your heart toward what truly matters in the sight of Allah?

The Second Proof of Oneness and the Sea Analogy
Sanusi presents the second proof and its initial response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational proof for God's oneness based on division and annihilation, and the response distinguishing two senses of 'sea'.

Core Idea
Dividing a body would annihilate its identity—unless we distinguish between object and its continuity.

Sanusi continues presenting proofs for God's oneness. The second proof argues that if God were one, any division of a body would annihilate it, which is absurd. The response refines the notion of identity by considering two meanings of 'sea': the water with continuity and the water itself. This lesson unpacks that argument and its answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Sanusi sets up the proof and then immediately answers it.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the proof's logical structure
The proof is a conditional: if God were one, then dividing a body would annihilate it. Why? Because it removes one identity and creates two. Then he says the consequent is false because a mosquito splitting the sea does not annihilate it.
3
Understand the response's two senses
The answer distinguishes two meanings of 'sea': (1) the water with its continuity, (2) the water itself without considering continuity. The first is annihilated upon separation, the second persists.
This is crucial for resolving the apparent contradiction.
4
Connect to the principle of the acceptor
Sanusi says this response aligns with the philosophers' principle that the acceptor must remain with the accepted. This prepares for the next lesson on matter.
Notice how he cites the principle here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أنه لو كان واحداً لكان تقسيم الجسم وتفريق أجزائه إعداماً له، ضرورة أنه إزالة لهويته الواحدة، وإحداث لهويتين أخريين، واللازم باطل، للقطع بأن شق البعوض البحر بابرته ليس إعداماً له، وإحداثاً لبحرين أخريين. وأجيب بأنه إن أريد بالبحر ذلك الماء ماله مع من الاتصال، فلاخفاء في انعدامه عند عروض الانفصال، وإن أريد نفس ذلك الماء من غير اعتبار بالاتصال، فليس في الشق زوال بحر، ولا حدوث بحرين، وهذا أنسب بقواعدهم، حيث يقولون: إن القابل للشيء يجب أن يكون باقياً عنده، مجتمعاً معه، فإن نقل الكلام إلى المادة بأنها إن كانت متعددة فهو إلزام، وإن كانت واحدة فإن بقيت بعد الانقسام كذلك، فظاهر البطلان للقطع بأن ما هو محل لهذه الصورة غير ما هو محل للصورة الأخرى، وإن صارت متعددة، فقد انعدمت الأولى ضرورة، ولزم انعدام الجسم بمادته وصورته جميعاً، وبطل قاعدة اجتماع القابل مع المقبول فلا محيص إلا بأن يقال المادة استعداد محض ليست في...
Second: If it were one, then dividing the body and separating its parts would be its annihilation, necessarily because it is a removal of its one identity and a generation of two other identities, and the necessary consequence is false, since it is certain that a mosquito splitting the sea with its proboscis is not its annihilation nor the generation of two other seas. And it is answered that if by 'sea' is meant that water with its continuity, then there is no concealment of its non-existence upon the occurrence of separation; and if the water itself is meant without consideration of continuity, then in the splitting there is no disappearance of a sea nor occurrence of two seas. And this is more appropriate to their principles, where they say: The acceptor of a thing must remain with it, united with it. Then if the discussion is transferred to matter (al-māddah) – if it is multiple, then it is a requirement; if it is one and remains as such after division, then its falsity is obvious, because it is certain that what is a subject for this form is not the subject for the other form; and if it becomes multiple, then the first is necessarily annihilated, and the annihilation of the body in its matter and form necessarily follows, and the principle of the coexistence of the acceptor and the accepted is invalidated. There is no escape except to say that matter is pure potentiality (istiʿdād maḥḍ), not in...
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This is the second proof of the second way, similar to the annihilation argument earlier. The response again distinguishes the two senses of 'sea'. It then expands the discussion to matter (hayūlā), arguing from the principles of the philosophers: the acceptor must remain with the accepted. For physical change, matter must persist across transformations. If matter were one and remained one after division, it would be the same subject for different forms, which is impossible. If matter became multiple, the original matter is annihilated, causing the body to be annihilated, violating the principle. Thus, matter must be a pure potentiality, not a substance. The passage ends before completing the argument.

Key Terms
إعدام
هوية
اتصال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Division of a body always annihilates it.
Correction: Only the identity defined by continuity is annihilated; the underlying matter persists.

Checkpoint
In the response to the second proof, what does 'sea' refer to when it is said that splitting does not annihilate it?
A) The water with its continuity.
B) The water itself without considering continuity.
C) The entire body of water.
D) The identity of the sea.

Key Takeaway
The second proof is answered by distinguishing between the object with its continuity and the object itself, allowing the body to persist through division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there an area of your life where you insist on maintaining a certain 'continuity'—a label, a routine, a self-image—such that any disruption feels like annihilation? What would it mean to see yourself as the water, not the sea?
?
When you experience failure or change, do you feel your identity is destroyed? How might the distinction between substance and continuity help you stand firm in who you truly are, even when life divides what you thought was one?
?
You affirm God's oneness with your tongue, but does your heart cling to other 'ones'—people, possessions, plans—as if losing them would be your undoing? What would it take to trust that your real self remains secure in the One who never divides?

Matter as Pure Potentiality: The Philosophers' Dilemma
Sanusi analyzes the nature of matter in light of the philosophers' principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why matter must be pure potentiality according to the philosophers' own principles of change.

Core Idea
Matter cannot persist as one substance through change; it must be pure potentiality.

Sanusi extends the discussion from the sea analogy to the nature of matter itself. Applying the philosophers' principle that the acceptor must remain with the accepted, he examines three possibilities: matter is multiple, remains one, or becomes multiple. Each leads to absurdities, forcing the conclusion that matter is pure potentiality—an incomplete yet foundational idea.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on matter
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice how Sanusi moves from the sea analogy to matter (al-māddah) and enumerates three scenarios.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the argument is incomplete.
2
Identify the three options for matter
Sanusi considers: (1) matter is multiple from the start, (2) matter is one and remains one after division, (3) matter becomes multiple. Each is examined against the principle that the acceptor must coexist with the accepted.
3
Understand why options 1 and 2 fail
Option 1 is dismissed as an 'ilzām' (a forced requirement). Option 2 fails because the same matter cannot be subject to two different forms. Option 3 annihilates the original matter and violates the principle.
4
Grasp the conclusion as far as given
The only escape is to say matter is pure potentiality (istiʿdād maḥḍ), not an actual substance. The passage ends before fully explaining this concept.
This sets up further discussion in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How often do you thank the Creator for the specific 'form' you have right now? Three Arguments for the Indivisible Atom
Al-Maqasid presents three arguments for an indivisible atom based on the implication of divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three rational arguments used by the mutakallimun to prove the existence of an indivisible atom (جوهر لا يقبل الانقسام) based on the principle that the acceptance of division necessitates actual division.

Core Idea
If division were possible everywhere, a mountain and a mustard seed would be equal in size—yet they are not.

This passage presents the first class of arguments from the mutakallimun for the existence of an indivisible atom. The arguments are based on a logical principle: if a body can be divided, then it must actually be divisible into atoms. The author gives three examples, including a theological premise about God's power, a comparison of a mountain and a mustard seed, and an argument about infinite regress of extensions. Let's examine each argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three distinct arguments introduced by 'كقولهم'.
2
Identify the three arguments
The passage presents three separate arguments for the same conclusion. Find each one and note how they differ in their premises.
The first uses God's power, the second uses size comparison, the third uses infinite regress.
3
Understand the objection and answer
The second argument includes an objection and a response. Notice how the mutakallimun defend their reasoning.
The objection challenges the basis of equality in magnitude, but the answer ties magnitude to number of parts.
4
Reflect on the logical principle
All three arguments rely on the principle that acceptance of division implies actual division (استلزام قبول الانقسام حصول الانقسام). Ask yourself why this principle is crucial.
This principle prevents infinite regress and establishes a limit to division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وثانيهما) [ إثبات جوهر في الجسم لا يقبل الانقسام أصلاً وفيه وجوه : منها ما يبتنى على استلزام قبول الانقسام حصول الانقسام، كقولهم إن الله قادر على أن يخلق في أجزاء الجسم الافتراق بدل الاجتماع . فثبت الجزء إذ لو بقي قبول التجزيء بقي الاجتماع، وكقولهم لولا الجزء لما كان الجبل أعظم من الخردلة، لاستواء أجزائهما، لكونهما غير متناهيين واعترض بأن الاستواء في عدد الأجزاء لا في مقاديرها . وأجيب بأن تفاوت المقادير بتفاوت الأجزاء قطعاً. وقد يدعى أن الاستواء في الأجزاء الممكنة أيضاً محال، وكقولهم لو لم ينته انقسام الجسم إلى ما لا امتداد له أصلاً لزم عدم تناهي امتداده، لتألفه من امتدادات غير متناهية،
He said (and the second of them): [Establishing an indivisible atom in the body, and it has aspects: from them is what is based on the implication of the acceptance of division necessitating the actual occurrence of division, such as their statement: God Almighty is able to create in the parts of the body separation instead of conjunction. So the atom is established, for if the acceptance of partitioning remained, conjunction would remain. And their statement: If not for the atom, the mountain would not be greater than the mustard seed, because their parts would be equal, as they are infinite. And it was objected that equality is in the number of parts, not in their magnitudes. And it was answered that the variation of magnitudes is due to the variation of parts decisively. And it may be claimed that equality in possible parts is also impossible. And their statement: If the division of the body did not end at something with no extension at all, then its extension would be infinite, because it would be composed of infinite extensions.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents the first class of proofs for the existence of an indivisible atom (جوهر لا يقبل الانقسام) in a body. The author begins by stating that this is the second way for the mutakallimun. The argument is based on the principle that the acceptance of division implies the actual attainment of division. Three examples are given: first, God's power to create separation instead of conjunction in the body's parts proves that there must be an atom that remains indivisible. Second, if there were no atom, a mountain and a mustard seed would be equal in size because both would have an infinite number of parts, which is absurd. An objection is raised that equality is in number, not magnitude, but the answer is that magnitude variation necessarily comes from variation in parts. Third, if division never ends at a part with no extension, then the body's extension would be composed of infinite extensions, leading to infinite extension, which is impossible. Thus the atom is established.

Key Terms
جوهر لا يقبل الانقسام
انقسام
جزء
اجتماع
افتراق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the arguments are purely physical; they are actually based on theological premises about divine power and logical absurdities of infinite regress.
Correction: The arguments rely on God's omnipotence as a premise and use logical contradictions (like infinite size) to prove the necessity of an indivisible atom.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument in this passage, what does God's ability to create separation in the body's parts prove?
A) That bodies are always divisible
B) That there is an indivisible atom
C) That division is impossible
D) That parts are infinite

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun used logical absurdities, such as the equality of a mountain and a mustard seed, to prove that bodies must contain indivisible atoms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage argues that if not for atoms, a mountain and a mustard seed would be equal in size. How often do you treat small and large sins as equal in your heart, ignoring that Allah has set limits and degrees? Will you today repent genuinely, starting with the smallest recognized sin?
?
The mutakallimun used logical absurdities to prove the existence of atoms. What absurdity in your own life—a contradiction between your beliefs and actions—have you been ignoring? What step will you take to resolve it this week?
?
God's ability to create separation proves atoms. If you truly believed that Allah can separate what is joined and join what is separated, how would your trust in Him change when facing a situation that seems impossible to fix? What situation will you entrust to Him tonight?

The Argument from Motion and Time for the Atom
Al-Maqasid presents the argument based on the nature of motion and time as discrete instants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun used the nature of motion and time to argue for the existence of indivisible atoms.

Core Idea
Motion and time consist of indivisible moments, implying space is also atomic.

This lesson presents a key kalam argument for atomism. The mutakallimun argue that motion and time are composed of discrete instants, and that only the present moment truly exists. From this, they deduce that the present moment of motion is indivisible, leading to indivisible spatial distances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own reasoning come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is a single connected argument.
2
Identify key premises
Identify the premises: motion has successive attainments, time has consecutive moments, and only the present moment of motion and time actually exists.
The terms 'حصولات' (attainments) and 'آنات' (moments) are crucial.
3
Trace the conclusion
Follow the reasoning: the present moment of a non-fixed essence (like motion) is indivisible, so the distance covered in that moment must also be indivisible. This implies an atomic structure of space.
This argument is metaphysical, not empirical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها ما يبتني على أن للحركة حصولات متعاقبة، والزمان آنات متتالية، كقولهم: الموجود من الحركة والزمان هو الحاضر ، لأن الماضي إنما وجد حين حضر والمستقبل إنما يوجد حين يحضر، والحاضر من غير قار الذات لا ينقسم، فكذا ما ينطبق هو عليه من المسافة(١)،
And from them is what is based on the fact that motion has successive attainments, and time has consecutive moments, such as their statement: The existent of motion and time is the present, for the past only existed when it was present, and the future only exists when it becomes present, and the present of a non-fixed essence is indivisible, so likewise that which corresponds to it from distance (is indivisible).
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is the second argument for the indivisible atom, based on the nature of motion and time. The mutakallimun argue that motion consists of discrete 'attainments' (حصولات) and time consists of consecutive 'moments' (آنات). Only the present moment of motion and time actually exists; the past existed only when it was present, and the future will exist only when it becomes present. The present moment of a non-fixed entity (like motion) is indivisible because it has no duration. Therefore, the distance covered in that indivisible moment must also be indivisible. This implies that space is composed of indivisible parts, which is the atom.

Key Terms
حركة
زمان
آنات
حاضر
قار الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is based on empirical observation of motion and time.
Correction: The argument is a metaphysical analysis of the nature of existence and time, not empirical observation.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why is the present moment of motion indivisible?
A) Because it has a fixed essence
B) Because it is not fixed in essence
C) Because it is eternal
D) Because it is divisible

Key Takeaway
The indivisibility of the present moment of motion leads to the conclusion that space is composed of indivisible atoms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If only the present moment of your life truly exists, and your past and future deeds only have reality in their respective present moments, how does that reshape the urgency of your next action for the sake of Allah? What change will you make today because of this?
?
The mutakallimun argued that the present moment of motion is indivisible — you cannot split it. In what ways do you split your present moment between distraction and obedience? Commit to one specific withdrawal this week from a habit that fragments your presence before Allah.
?
Each instant of your deeds is recorded, and this lesson argues that each instant covers an indivisible span of motion. If every act in that instant carries full weight, what does that demand of you regarding the purity of your intentions in the current moment? What do you need to repent for or correct right now?

Argument from the Point
Argument from the point's indivisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the geometric argument that the existence of a point implies an indivisible substance.

Core Idea
The point cannot be divided, so its locus must be an indivisible atom.

Sanusi opens with an argument based on the nature of the point. The point exists as the extremity of a line and as the means by which lines touch. If it is not itself a substance, it must be an accident in an indivisible substance, otherwise the point would be divisible. This argument uses basic geometry to support atomism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the argument's structure.
2
Identify the key claim
Notice that the argument assumes points exist and are indivisible. The conclusion is that their locus is an indivisible atom.
3
Consider the alternative
The argument considers two possibilities: the point could be a substance or an accident. Both lead to an indivisible atom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها ما يبتنى على أن محل النقطة جوهر لا يقبل الانقسام لقولهم النقطة موجودة، لأنها طرف الخط الموجود، وبها تماس الخطوط. فإن كان جوهراً فذاك، وإن كان عرضاً كان بالذات، أو بالواسطة حالاً في جوهر لا ينقسم، لئلا يلزم انقسام النقطة. وكقولهم : إذا وضعنا كرة حقيقية على سطح مستو إن قام خط على خط كانت المماسة بما لا ينقسم ثم إذا أديرت الكرة بتمامها على السطح، ومر الخط إلى آخر الخط ظهر عدم انقسام الأجزاء بأسرها، وثبت المطلوب، وكقولهم : قد ثبت أن الزاوية الحاصلة من مماسة الخط المستقيم لمحيط الدائرة أصغر الزوايا، فلا يقبل الانقسام فثبت الجزء.
And from them is what is based on the fact that the locus of a point is an indivisible substance, because they say: The point exists, for it is the extremity of the existing line, and by it lines touch. If it is a substance, then that is it; but if it is an accident, it would be either directly or mediately inherent in an indivisible substance, to avoid the necessity of the division of the point. And their statement: If we place a real sphere on a flat surface, when a line stands upon a line, the tangency is by what is indivisible; then if the sphere is rotated completely on the surface, and the line passes to the end of the line, the indivisibility of all the parts becomes apparent, and the desired is established. And their statement: It has been established that the angle formed by the tangency of a straight line to the circumference of a circle is the smallest of angles, so it does not accept division, thus the atom is established.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents three geometric arguments for the indivisible atom, all based on the nature of the point. First, the point exists as the extremity of a line and the means of tangency of lines; if it were an accident, it must inhere in an indivisible substance to avoid its own division. Second, when a true sphere contacts a plane surface, the contact is at a single indivisible point; rotating the sphere shows that the entire surface is composed of such indivisible points. Third, the angle formed by the tangent line and the circumference of a circle is the smallest possible angle and is indivisible, proving that its vertex is an indivisible atom. These arguments use geometry to support atomism.

Key Terms
نقطة
خط
جوهر
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The point argument is an empirical observation.
Correction: It is a logical argument from the definition of a point, not an empirical measurement.

Checkpoint
What does the argument from the point conclude about the locus of a point?
A) It is divisible
B) It is an indivisible substance
C) It is an accident
D) It does not exist

Key Takeaway
The point argument shows that the existence of an indivisible point necessitates an indivisible atom as its locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that even a tiny point demands an indivisible reality to exist. If such a seemingly trivial thing has profound implications, how much more should your own existence lead you to contemplate its ultimate ground? Will you take five minutes today to reflect on the fact that your very being points to an indivisible Creator?
?
The point argument relies on premises about lines and tangency that you may never have questioned. Are there assumptions in your own life—about success, happiness, or faith—that you have accepted without examination? What would happen if you followed their implications to their logical end?
?
The point is indivisible, yet your heart is divided among many loyalties and distractions. If the atom cannot be split, should not your devotion to Allah be equally indivisible? What is one practical step you can take today to unify your heart's attention upon Him?
