Fourth Proof: The Sunlit Surface
Taftazani's fourth proof from sunlight on a surface

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the phenomenon of sunlight on a surface demonstrates that atoms must have distinct parts and are therefore divisible.

Core Idea
Illumination exposes hidden division: a single atom cannot be both lit and dark.

Taftazani continues his series of arguments for the divisibility of atoms. This fourth proof draws on a simple observation: when sunlight falls on a surface, one side is illuminated while the other remains dark. This shows that even the smallest unit, if it has extension, must have multiple faces and thus is divisible. The argument uses a hypothetical surface made of indivisible atoms to expose a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice how Taftazani moves from a supposition to a necessary conclusion.
The passage is short but dense. Pay attention to the phrase 'فبالضرورة' (necessarily).
2
Identify the supposition
The argument begins by supposing a surface (صفحة) composed of indivisible atoms (أجزاء لا تتجزأ). This surface has length and width. Note that this is a hypothetical construct.
The atoms are assumed indivisible, but the argument will show they must be divisible.
3
Understand the condition and observation
When the sun shines on the surface, the face opposite the sun becomes illuminated, while the other face remains dark. That is an observable fact.
The illumination is not uniform across the surface; there is a distinction.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the same atom cannot be both illuminated and dark, the atom must have at least two distinct faces (or sides). This implies that the atom has parts and is therefore divisible.
The argument works even if the atom is a point with extension; the idea of facing and opposite gives two sides.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: أنا نفرض صفحة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ بحيث له الطول والعرض فإذا أشرقت عليها الشمس فبالضرورة يكون وجهها المقابل للشمس المضيء بها غير الوجه الآخر فينقسم .
The fourth: we suppose a surface of indivisible parts having length and width. When the sun shines on it, necessarily its face opposite the sun, illuminated by it, is other than the other face, so it divides.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage gives the fourth argument. It imagines a two-dimensional surface made of indivisible parts (atoms) that has length and width. If the sun shines on it from one direction, the face (or side) that faces the sun becomes illuminated, while the opposite face remains dark. Since the same atom cannot be both illuminated and dark simultaneously, the surface must have two distinct faces, which implies it has parts (i.e., it is divisible). Therefore, the supposedly indivisible atoms must actually be divisible.

Key Terms
صفحة
أجزاء لا تتجزأ
الطول والعرض
الانقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The atom itself is not the surface; the argument only applies to a collection of atoms, not a single atom.
Correction: The argument works at the level of a single atom because the surface is composed of atoms; each atom must have a face towards the sun and a face away from the sun, implying the atom itself has distinct parts.

Checkpoint
What does the sun's illumination of a surface of atoms prove according to the fourth argument?
A) The atoms are transparent
B) The atoms have two different sides and are divisible
C) The sun is the cause of division
D) The surface has no parts

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument uses the simple observation of sunlight to demonstrate that even the smallest atom must have parts, otherwise it could not be both illuminated and dark.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that even something as seemingly unified as a sunlit atom contains internal division. In what areas of your own life have you assumed you are 'indivisible'—whole and without internal contradiction—when in fact you harbor contradictions (e.g., between belief and action)? What would it take to honestly acknowledge those divisions?
?
Taftazani uses a physical observation to force a logical conclusion about what must be true. Where in your spiritual life do you avoid following a simple observation to its necessary conclusion because it would require you to change your view of yourself or your Creator? What are you protecting by not following the evidence?
?
The proof relies on the impossibility of a thing being both illuminated and dark simultaneously. Think of a habit or attitude you claim to have left (the 'dark' face) but still entertain in secret (the 'illuminated' face). Will you today choose to expose that contradiction to the light of honest reflection and seek to remove it entirely?

The Fifth Argument: The Implication of Division
Taftazani states the fifth argument and explains its implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical core of Taftazani's fifth argument: why an indivisible atom falling on the meeting point of two others necessarily implies division.

Core Idea
If an atom contacts two others, it must have parts.

Taftazani opens his fifth argument with a conditional claim: if an indivisible part lands exactly on the meeting point of two other parts, then all three must be divisible. The key is the nature of contact: the falling atom cannot touch both wholly — it must use different segments to touch each neighbor. This lesson examines that implication.

Guided Path
1
Read the main claim and translation
Read the Arabic passage from 'الخامس' to 'على الملتقى' and its translation. Notice the structure: a conditional statement followed by its justification.
The term 'ملتقى' means meeting point.
2
Focus on the implication (mulazama)
Taftazani explains that contact must be partial. Trace the reasoning: if the atom touched one neighbor entirely, it would be on that neighbor, not on the meeting point.
This step is the heart of the argument.
3
Reflect on the necessity of partial contact
Consider why the atom cannot touch both with the same part. Visualize two adjacent atoms and a third landing on their junction. How must contact occur?
This clarifies why division follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس: أنه إذا وقع جزء لا يتجزأ على ملتقى جزءين آخرين لزم انقسام الثلاثة . أما الملازمة فلأن التماس بينه وبين كل منهما إنما يكون بالبعض أي يكون شيء منه مماساً لشيء من هذا، وشيء آخر مماساً لشيء من ذاك، إذ لو ماس أحدهما بالكلية لكان عليه لا على الملتقى، وأما بيان حقيقة اللزوم فبوجوه (1) أن نفرض الجزء على الملتقى وفيه مناقشة لا تخفى . (۲) أن يتحرك من جزء إلى جزء فاتصافه بالحركة إنما يكون عند كونه على الملتقى لا على الأول، إذ لم تبتدأ الحركة ولا على الثاني إذ قد انقطعت . (۳) أن نفرض خطاً من أربعة أجزاء فوق الأول جزء وتحت الرابع جزء ثم نفرض مرور الفوقاني والتحتاني على الخط بحركة على السواء مع اتفاق في الابتداء ، أي تكون الحركتان على حد واحد من السرعة والبطء ويكون ابتداؤهما معاً، فبالضرورة تتحاذيان على ملتقى الثاني والثالث. أي حيث يكون الفوقاني فوق الملتقى والتحتاني تحته . (٤) أن نفرض خطاً من خمسة أجزاء فوق الأول جزء، وفوق الخامس جزء، ثم أخذا معاً في حركة على السواء إلى حد الالتقاء، فبالضرورة يكون ذلك في وسط الخط أعني الجزء الثالث، فيكون هو على ملتقاهما من تحت، ولا يخفى أن هذه البيانات إنما تتم على من يجوز وجود الجوهر الفرد على الانفراد، ثم حركته على الإطلاق، ثم حركته على الأنحاء المخصوصة المؤدية إلى المحال .
The fifth: that if an indivisible part falls on the meeting point of two other parts, the division of the three is necessary. As for the implication, it is because the contact between it and each of them is only by part, i.e., something of it is in contact with something of this, and another thing is in contact with something of that, for if it were in contact with one of them wholly, it would be upon it, not upon the meeting point. As for the demonstration of the necessity, it is by several ways: (1) we suppose the part on the meeting point, and there is a discussion not hidden. (2) It moves from one part to another, and its description with motion only occurs when it is on the meeting point, not on the first, since motion did not begin, nor on the second, since it has stopped. (3) We suppose a line of four parts, above the first a part and below the fourth a part, then we suppose the upper and lower pass over the line with equal motion and agreement in beginning, i.e., the two motions are at one degree of speed and slowness and their beginnings are together, so necessarily they align at the meeting point of the second and third, i.e., where the upper is above the meeting point and the lower below it. (4) We suppose a line of five parts, above the first a part and above the fifth a part, then they both move together with equal motion to the point of meeting, so necessarily that is in the middle of the line, namely the third part, so it is upon their meeting point from below. And it is not hidden that these demonstrations are only complete for one who allows the existence of the individual substance (al-jawhar al-fard) in isolation, then its absolute motion, then its motion in the specific ways leading to impossibility.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument against the indivisible part, along with four sub-proofs and a concluding condition. The argument: If an indivisible atom (juz' la yatajzza') falls on the meeting point of two other atoms, all three must divide. The reason for the implication (mulazama) is that the contact between the falling atom and each of the two must be partial: part of it touches one, another part touches the other. If it touched one wholly, it would be on it, not on the meeting point. To demonstrate the necessity, the author gives four sub-proofs. The first simply supposes the part on the meeting point, but notes there is a discussion about it (not elaborated). The second uses motion: as the atom moves from one part to another, it is described as moving only when at the meeting point, implying it has different positions. The third considers two parts moving along a line of four parts at equal speed and starts; they must align at the meeting point of the second and third, showing division. The fourth uses a line of five parts with two parts moving from opposite ends toward each other; they meet at the third part, which becomes their meeting point from below, again implying division. The author then notes that these demonstrations are only valid for someone who grants the existence of the individual atom (jawhar fard) in isolation, its absolute motion, and its motion in the specific ways that lead to impossibility (muhal). This is a condition that limits the force of the arguments.

Key Terms
ملتقى
الملازمة
جزء لا يتجزأ
التماس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani's fifth argument, why must the falling atom divide when it lands on the meeting point of two others?
A) Because it touches one neighbor wholly
B) Because it touches each neighbor partially
C) Because the meeting point is made of two parts
D) Because no atom can exist indivisibly

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument's core is that contact on a meeting point forces partial contact, which entails division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument reveals that what seems simple (indivisible) actually has hidden complexity. Where in your own life have you assumed your intentions or actions are 'simple' and undivided, when in truth they are pulled in different directions? Will you examine one such area today and bring it before Allah?
?
The falling atom cannot fully commit to one neighbor without losing its position. Are you fully committed to truth, or do you try to 'contact' both Allah and your desires at the same meeting point? What would it cost you to be wholeheartedly on the side of Haqq?
?
If logical necessity forces division of the smallest parts, what does that say about the necessity of tawheed in your heart? Can you afford to let any part of your belief be 'indivisible' and unexamined? Ask yourself: Am I willing to let the light of this argument dissect every assumption I have about my faith?

Four Sub-Proofs and the Condition of Their Validity
Taftazani provides four sub-proofs to demonstrate the necessity of division and adds a condition about their validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four sub-proofs Taftazani provides to demonstrate the necessity of division, as well as the condition that these proofs assume specific physical possibilities.

Core Idea
The four proofs show the absurdity of the indivisible atom, but only if motion and isolated atoms are assumed.

After establishing the logical implication, Taftazani offers four concrete ways to demonstrate why the division of the three parts is necessary. These involve geometry and motion. However, he adds a crucial condition: the proofs are only complete for someone who grants the existence of an individual atom, its absolute motion, and its motion in specific ways that lead to impossibility. This lesson walks through each sub-proof and examines this limit.

Guided Path
1
Read all four sub-proofs and translation
Read the Arabic from 'وأما بيان حقيقة اللزوم' to the end, along with the translation. Get a sense of the variety of arguments.
Each sub-proof is numbered (1-4).
2
Work through each sub-proof one by one
Take sub-proof (1): suppose the part on the meeting point. Note Taftazani's remark that there is a discussion. Then (2): motion from part to part. (3): two parts moving along a line. (4): two parts moving toward each other on a line of five. Visualize each scenario.
The first sub-proof is the simplest but Taftazani hints at an objection.
3
Understand the concluding condition
Read the final sentence: these demonstrations are only valid for someone who allows the existence of the individual atom in isolation, its absolute motion, and its motion in specific ways leading to impossibility. This is a limitation the author places on his own arguments.
Taftazani is being cautious; the proofs are not universally absolute.
4
Reflect on the purpose of the condition
Ask: Why does Taftazani include this condition? What does it reveal about his method? It shows he is aware of the presuppositions of the argument and is honest about them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(۲) أن يتحرك من جزء إلى جزء فاتصافه بالحركة إنما يكون عند كونه على الملتقى لا على الأول، إذ لم تبتدأ الحركة ولا على الثاني إذ قد انقطعت . Mu'tazila Objection on Imaginative Division and Taftazani's Response
Taftazani relates the Mu'tazila claim and gives his response, reducing it to Democritus' atomism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila objection that the proofs show only imaginative division and Taftazani's dismissal of it as a revival of Democritean atomism.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila objection of imaginative division is just Democritus' atomism revisited.

In this passage, Taftazani addresses an objection from some Mu'tazila works. They claim that the arguments for indivisible parts only prove conceptual division, not actual. Taftazani counters that this is simply a return to the ancient atomism of Democritus, which has been refuted. Let's examine the objection and response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the English translation. Note the concise structure: objection followed by response.
The Arabic includes a footnote marker (۱) referring to an editorial note on الوهم.
2
Identify the two sides
The Mu'tazila claim that the proofs only show division in imagination (الوهم) and that by 'part' they mean something not actually divided. Taftazani responds that this position is a reversion to the doctrine of Democritus.
Democritus held that atoms are physically indivisible, which the author considers already refuted.
3
Understand the dismissal
Taftazani is not accepting the distinction as valid. He argues that the Mu'tazila are not offering a new argument but merely restating ancient atomism, which the earlier proofs have already rebutted.
This shows the author's method of reducing objections to previously refuted positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما ذكر في بعض كتب المعتزلة من أن الوجوه المذكورة إنما تدل على الانقسام بالوهم (۱) ونحن نعني بالجزء ما لا ينقسم بالفعل فرجوع إلى مذهب ديموقراطيس.
And as for what is mentioned in some Mu'tazila books, that the aforementioned arguments only indicate division in imagination (bi-l-wahm), and we mean by 'part' that which is not actually divided, so it is a reversion to the doctrine of Democritus.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage records an objection from some Mu'tazila works and the author's response. The objection: the arguments presented earlier only demonstrate division in imagination (al-wahm), not actual physical division. The Mu'tazila claim that by 'part' they mean something that does not divide in actuality. The author responds that this position is merely a return to the doctrine of Democritus, who held that atoms are indivisible in act. This response implies that the Mu'tazila are not offering a new argument but simply restating ancient atomism, which has been refuted by the preceding proofs.

Key Terms
المعتزلة

The Meaning of Wahm and Its Types
Definition of wahm as a source of error in perception and judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of wahm (general and specific) and how it leads to false propositions and sophistry.

Core Idea
Wahm is a natural error that, when applied to non-sensory matters, produces sophistry.

This lesson introduces the key term 'wahm' (illusion or estimation), which is a source of error in perception and reasoning. The author distinguishes between a general sense of wahm as any natural error, and a specific sense as a false sensory representation contrary to hallucination. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for identifying fallacies in arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the contrast between general and specific definitions.
2
Identify the two definitions of wahm
Find the general definition (every error in perception/judgment/inference that is natural) and the specific definition (false sensory representation arising from interpretation of perception).
The specific definition is contrasted with hallucination.
3
Examine the examples
The passage gives examples: log in water appearing to drown, small insect appearing as large bird. Consider why these are errors of interpretation, not sensory data.
4
Understand wahmiyyat and sophistry
Wahmiyyat are false propositions about non-sensory matters, like infinite space. A syllogism based on them is called sophistry.
This shows how wahm can lead to false reasoning.
5
Check your understanding
Take the quiz to test your grasp of the key concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يطلق الوهم على كل خطأ في الإدراك أو الحكم أو الاستدلال شريطة أن يظن أنه خطأ طبيعي وإن وقوع المرء فيه ناشيء عن انخداعه بالظواهر تقول : أوهام الحواس. والوهم بوجه خاص مقابل الهالوسة وهو تمثل حسي كاذب ناشيء عن كيفية تأويل الادراك لا عن معطيات الاحساس كمن ينظر الى الخشبة الطافية فوق الماء فيحسبها غريقاً، أو الى الحشرة الصغيرة الطائرة بالقرب من عينيه فيحسبها طيراً كبيراً. والوهميات قضايا كاذبة يحكم بها الوهم في أمور غير محسوسة، كالحكم بأن ما وراء العالم فضاء لا يتناهى، والقياس المركب منها يسمى سفسطة. راجع تعريفات الجرجاني
The term 'wahm' is applied to every error in perception, judgment, or inference, provided that it is thought to be a natural error and that one's falling into it arises from being deceived by appearances. One says: illusions of the senses (awhām al-ḥawāss). And wahm in particular is opposite to hallucination (al-hālūsa). It is a false sensory representation arising from the manner of interpreting perception, not from sensory data, like one who looks at a log floating on water and thinks it is drowning, or at a small insect flying near his eyes and thinks it is a large bird. And wahmiyyāt are false propositions that wahm judges regarding non-sensory matters, such as the judgment that beyond the world is infinite space. And a syllogism composed of them is called sophistry. See al-Jurjānī's Definitions.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the term 'wahm' (illusion or estimation). It first gives a general definition: wahm is any error in perception, judgment, or inference, provided that the error is considered natural (i.e., not deliberate) and arises from being deceived by appearances. An example is 'illusions of the senses'. Then it defines wahm in a specific sense, contrasting it with hallucination (al-hālūsa). In this specific sense, wahm is a false sensory representation resulting from how one interprets perception, not from the raw sensory data. Two examples are given: a log in water appearing to be drowning (actually floating), and a small insect near the eye appearing as a large bird. Next, it mentions wahmiyyāt (pl. of wahmiyya): false propositions that wahm judges about non-sensible matters, e.g., 'what is beyond the world is infinite space'. A syllogism composed of such propositions is called sophistry. The passage ends by referring to al-Jurjānī's Definitions for further reading.

Key Terms
الوهم (al-wahm)
الهالوسة (al-hālūsa)
الوهميات (al-wahmiyyāt)
السفسطة (al-safsata)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Confusing the specific meaning of wahm (false sensory representation) with the philosophical faculty of estimation (al-wahm al-mutaṣarif).
Correction: The passage defines wahm specifically as an error, not as a cognitive faculty. The philosophical faculty is a different concept.

Checkpoint
What does the passage state is the opposite of wahm in its specific sense?
A) Certainty
B) Hallucination (al-hālūsa)
C) Sensation
D) Reasoning

Key Takeaway
Wahm is a natural error that affects perception and reasoning, and when applied beyond senses, leads to false propositions and sophistry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold about the unseen (e.g., the nature of Paradise or the end of time) that you have never thoroughly questioned. Is it possible that this belief is a 'wahmiyya'—a false proposition arising from your own interpretation rather than from revelation? How can you test it today?

The passage says that wahm is a 'natural error' from being deceived by appearances. In your journey of faith, when have you confidently judged something about Allah based on your own reasoning without checking the sources? Will you commit to seeking the original texts before forming judgments about non-sensory matters?
?
Wahm can lead to sophistry—using false premises in arguments. When you debate or share Islam with others, do you ever rely on assumptions that you have not verified? Are you willing to abandon any argument that you cannot trace back to a sound source, even if it feels persuasive?

Experimental Proof Against the Indivisible Part
Al-Taftazani presents a physical experiment to refute atomism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a physical experiment demonstrates the impossibility of atomic pauses in motion, thereby refuting the concept of indivisible parts.

Core Idea
Motion's continuity proves atoms cannot exist.

Al-Taftazani presents a clever experiment to argue against atomism, specifically the idea of an indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza'). By setting up a bucket-and-rope system, he shows that if motion had pauses, the effect would occur without its complete cause, which is rationally impossible. This passage illustrates how physical reasoning is used in theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own description of the experiment come through before you interpret.
Notice the precise distances and the logical conclusion at the end.
2
Identify the experimental setup
Visualize the bucket, hook, ropes, and well. Understand how the hook's movement is the complete cause of the bucket's movement, and that the hook travels half the distance.
The hook is attached to the midpoint of a rope, so it moves 50 cubits while the bucket moves 100.
3
Understand the logical implication
Ask yourself: why does the author conclude that pauses would necessitate the effect without its cause? Reflect on the definition of 'complete cause' (علة تامة) and why the bucke's motion depends entirely on the hook's continuous motion.
This is the core of the refutation of atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلو ثم شددنا كلاً بأعلى طرف حبل آخر طوله خمسون ذراعاً، وأرسلناه في البئر، بحيث وقع الكلاب في الحبل الأول على طرفه المشدود في الخشبة، ثم جررناه من البئر، فيكون ابتداء حركة الكلاب من الوسط والدلو من الأسفل معاً وكذا انتهاؤهما إلى رأس البئر وقد قطع الدلو مائة ذراع، والكلاب خمسين، مع أن حركة الكلاب من تمام علة حركة الدلو، فلو كان له سكنات في خلال حركته لزم وجود المعلول بدون علته التامة.
A bucket, then we tied each to the top end of another rope of length fifty cubits, and we sent it into the well, such that the hook [fell] on the first rope at its end tied to the beam, then we pulled it from the well, so the beginning of the movement of the hook from the middle and the bucket from the bottom is simultaneous, and likewise their ending at the top of the well, and the bucket has traversed one hundred cubits, and the hook fifty, even though the movement of the hook is the complete cause of the movement of the bucket, so if there were pauses for it during its movement, it would necessitate the existence of the effect without its complete cause.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents an experimental argument against the existence of indivisible parts (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza'). The author describes a setup: a bucket is tied to one end of a rope, and a hook (or dog) is attached to the midpoint of another rope. The ropes are arranged so that pulling the bucket moves the hook as well. The hook moves half the distance of the bucket. Since the hook's movement is the complete cause ('illa tamma) of the bucket's movement, if the hook had pauses (sakanat) during its motion, the bucket's movement would occur without its complete cause, which is impossible. Therefore, motion must be continuous, implying that space and time are not composed of indivisible parts.

Key Terms
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
علة تامة
سكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The bucket experiment is a practical demonstration that atoms exist.
Correction: The experiment is a logical proof against atomism, showing that motion cannot be composed of pauses, thus indivisible parts do not exist.

Checkpoint
What does the bucket experiment demonstrate about the indivisible part?
A) That indivisible parts exist in motion
B) That motion requires pauses
C) That movement without a complete cause is impossible, refuting atomism
D) That the bucket and hook move at different speeds

Key Takeaway
The bucket experiment shows that if motion had pauses, the effect would occur without its complete cause, which is impossible, proving that motion is continuous and indivisible parts do not exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you insisted on a belief about the world despite encountering clear evidence against it? What would it take for you to let go of that belief, just as this proof demands letting go of atomism?
?
The hook's motion is the complete cause of the bucket's motion—there are no pauses. In your relationship with Allah, is your obedience continuous, or do you allow 'pauses' (sakanat) of disobedience that break the causal chain of faith? What would it mean to eliminate those pauses?
?
This argument shows that causality demands uninterrupted continuity. In your own life, are there areas where you rely on incomplete causes—excuses or half-hearted intentions—expecting full effects? How can you align your actions to be a 'complete cause' for the spiritual results you seek?

Geometrical Proofs Against the Indivisible Part
Al-Taftazani enumerates seven geometrical proofs and elaborates on the first three.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani uses Euclidean geometry to refute the existence of the indivisible part, and you will be able to identify the key constructions that undermine atomism.

Core Idea
Geometry proves infinite divisibility, refuting the atomist hypothesis.

Al-Taftazani presents a series of geometrical proofs drawn from Euclidean geometry to demonstrate that the assumption of indivisible parts leads to contradictions. These proofs are not empirical but logical, showing that standard geometric constructions—such as bisecting lines and angles, constructing equilateral triangles, and applying the Pythagorean theorem—are impossible if space is composed of discrete indivisible units. In this lesson, we will explore seven such proofs and the detailed elaboration of the first three.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has two clear sections: a summary list of seven proofs, then a detailed elaboration on the first three.
2
Identify the seven proofs in summary
Locate each of the seven brief statements. For each, note the geometric construction used (e.g., line bisection, equilateral triangle, angle bisection, circle/sphere, Pythagorean theorem, etc.).
3
Understand the first detailed proof
The first detailed proof builds on the ability to bisect a line. It shows that in a line of two parts, constructing an equilateral triangle forces a vertex to fall on the meeting point, dividing one of the parts. This contradicts indivisibility.
This proof uses the construction from Euclid's Elements.
4
Understand the second detailed proof
The second detailed proof concerns angle bisection. Every angle can be bisected, meaning the vertex point must be divisible, which is impossible if the vertex is an indivisible part.
Angle bisection is a fundamental Euclidean operation.
5
Understand the third detailed proof
The third detailed proof (partially given) uses a more complex construction: drawing a chord, constructing equilateral triangles on both sides, and connecting vertices to show a line is bisected, again requiring division of a part.
This proof is cut off but the principle is clear: geometric constructions force division of supposedly indivisible units.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدلة هندسية على نفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
قال (ومنها) ما يتعلق بأصول هندسية مبنية على انتفاء الجزء وهي وجوه : الأول : كل خط يمكن تنصيفه ففي المركب من الأجزاء الوتر يلزم تجزيء الوسطاني.
الثاني : كل خط يمكن أن يعمل عليه مثلث متساوي الأضلاع ولا يتصور في المركب من جزئين إلا بوقوع جزء على ملتقى الجزئين.
الثالث : : كل زاوية مستقيمة الخطين تنقسم لا إلى نهاية .
الرابع : إذا ثبت أحد طرفي الخط المستقيم وأدير حتى عاد إلى وضعه الأول حصلت الدائرة، ثم إذا أدير نصفها على قطرها الثابت حصلت الكرة، ووجود الجزء ينفيهما لأنا لو فرضنا محيط الدائرة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، فإما أن يكون ظواهر الأجزاء كبواطنها، فيلزم تساوي ظاهر المحيط وباطنه أو أكثر فيلزم الانقسام، أو بين الظواهر فرج خلاء لا يسع كلها منها جزءاً فيلزم الانقسام، أو يسع فيكون الظاهر ضعف الباطن، ولأن المدار الذي بلاصق المنطقة، إما أن يكون بإزاء كل جزء منها جزء منه فيتساويان، أو أقل فينقسم .
الخامس : مربع وتر القائمة لمجموع مربعى الضلعين المحيطين بها . فإذا فرضنا كل ضلع عشرة أجزاء كان الوتر أكثر من أربعة عشر، وأقل من خمسة عشر، لكونه جذر مائتين . السادس : خط من جزءين فوق أحدهما جزء فهناك قائمة وترها فوق الاثنين، ودون الثلاثة، وإلا لزم كون وتر القائمة مساوية لكل من الضلعين أو لمجموعهما . السابع : مربع من انضمام أربعة خطوط كل منها من أربعة أجزاء، فالقطر إن كان منضم الأجزاء كان أربعة أجزاء مثل الضلع وهو محال . وإن كان مع خلاء بقدر الجزء كانت سبعة أجزاء مثل الضلعين وهو أيضاً محال أو أقل فيلزم الانقسام]. أي ومن تلك الطرق ما يبتنى على أصول هندسية لا سبيل إلى إثباتها، إلا على تقدير انتفاء الجزء كما يظهر للناظر في البراهين المذكورة في كتاب إقليدس، ولهذا هذا الطريق كثيرة جداً، ولنذكر عدة منها : وجوه كانت الأول: أنه يمكن لنا أن نعمل على كل خط شيئاً مثلثاً متساوي الأضلاع، ولا يتصور ذلك في الخط المركب من جزئين إلا بأن يقع جزء على ملتقى الجزءين، وقد عرفت أنه يوجب انقسام الثلاثة .
الثاني : أن كل زاوية فإنه يمكن تنصيفها فيلزم تجزيء الجزء الذي هو ملتقى خطي الزاوية .
الثالث: أن كل خط، فإنه يمكن تنصيفه، ففي المركب من الأجزاء الوتر يلزم انقسام الجزء الذي في الوسط وقد بين ذلك في الهندسة بأن يعمل على ذلك الخط مثلث متساوي الأضلاع، ثم تنصف الزاوية التي يؤثرها ذلك الخط بخط واصل منها إليه ، فتكون على منتصفه وبين منتصف الزاوية بأن يجعل خطاها متساويين ، ثم يوصل بين طرفيهما بخط يكون وتراً لها، ويعمل عليه من الطرف الآخر مثلث متساوي الأضلاع ، ثم يخرج خط من زاوية المثلث الأول إلى زاوية المثلث الثاني
Geometrical proofs for negating the indivisible part. He said: (And among them) what pertains to geometrical principles based on the negation of the part, and they are aspects: First: Every line can be bisected, so in a line composed of parts, the chord necessitates the division of the middle [part]. Second: Every line can have an equilateral triangle constructed upon it, and this is inconceivable in a line composed of two parts except by a part falling on the meeting point of the two parts. Third: Every angle with straight lines can be divided infinitely. Fourth: If one end of a straight line is fixed and it is rotated until it returns to its original position, a circle results; then if its half is rotated on its fixed diameter, a sphere results. The existence of the part negates them, for if we suppose the circumference of the circle to be composed of indivisible parts, then either the exteriors of the parts are like their interiors, necessitating equality of the exterior and interior of the circumference, or more, necessitating division; or between the exteriors there are gaps of void that do not collectively accommodate a part, necessitating division; or they accommodate, so the exterior is double the interior. And because the arc adjacent to the region either has for each part a part from it, so they are equal, or less, so it divides. Fifth: The square of the hypotenuse of a right-angled triangle equals the sum of the squares of the two sides containing it. So if we suppose each side to be ten parts, the hypotenuse is more than fourteen and less than fifteen, because it is the root of two hundred. Sixth: A line of two parts, above one of which is a part, then there is a right-angled triangle whose hypotenuse is above two and below three; otherwise the hypotenuse would be equal to each of the sides or to their sum. Seventh: A square formed by joining four lines each of four parts: if the diagonal is composed of parts, it would be four parts like the side, which is impossible; if it is with a gap the size of a part, it would be seven parts like the two sides, which is also impossible; or less, necessitating division. That is, among these methods are those based on geometrical principles that cannot be proven except on the assumption of the negation of the part, as appears to the observer in the proofs mentioned in Euclid's book, and thus this path is very numerous. Let us mention several of them: There were aspects: First: That we can construct an equilateral triangle on every line, and this is inconceivable in a line composed of two parts except by a part falling on the meeting point of the two parts, and you know that this necessitates the division of the three. Second: That every angle can be bisected, necessitating the division of the part that is the meeting point of the two lines of the angle. Third: That every line can be bisected, so in a line composed of parts, the chord necessitates the division of the part that is in the middle. This has been explained in geometry by constructing an equilateral triangle on that line, then bisecting the angle that that line subtends by a line drawn from it to it, so it falls on its midpoint, and the midpoint of the angle is determined by making its two sides equal, then connecting their two endpoints by a line that is the hypotenuse for it, and constructing on the other side another equilateral triangle, then drawing a line from the angle of the first triangle to the angle of the second triangle.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a collection of geometrical proofs against the existence of the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza'). The author first lists seven brief arguments, then elaborates on the first three. The common theme is that standard Euclidean constructions and properties of lines, angles, and figures entail infinite divisibility, which contradicts atomism. 

First summary: 
1. Any line can be bisected, so if composed of parts, the middle point would require division. 
2. An equilateral triangle can be constructed on any line; on a two-part line, a vertex must fall on the meeting point, dividing it. 
3. An angle can be bisected infinitely. 
4. Circle and sphere generation; if composed of parts, contradictions arise regarding the circumference. 
5. Pythagoras' theorem: for a right triangle with sides of 10 parts, the hypotenuse is irrational. 
6. A specific right triangle where the hypotenuse is between 2 and 3. 
7. A square with side of 4 parts: the diagonal cannot be an integer number of parts. 

The detailed explanations for the first three provide step-by-step constructions from Euclidean geometry, showing that assuming indivisible parts leads to impossibility because certain points or lines would have to coincide or divide the supposed part. The third detailed proof, though cut off, describes constructing an equilateral triangle, bisecting an angle, and drawing a line to demonstrate the bisection of a line, which would require dividing the middle part.

Key Terms
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
خط
زاوية
مثلث متساوي الأضلاع
وتر
مربع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These proofs are based on physical experiments rather than logical deduction from geometric axioms.
Correction: The proofs are a priori logical arguments using Euclidean geometry; they do not rely on empirical observation.

Checkpoint

A) The triangle cannot be constructed
B) A vertex falls on the meeting point, dividing one of the parts

D) The triangle is impossible

Key Takeaway
Geometrical proofs demonstrate that the assumption of indivisible parts leads to contradictions with Euclidean geometry, thus refuting atomism.

Fifth Argument: Pythagorean Theorem Against Atomism
Taftazani's fifth geometric argument refuting atomism using the Pythagorean theorem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Pythagorean theorem demonstrates that indivisible parts cannot account for the length of a hypotenuse in a right triangle.

Core Idea
The hypotenuse's irrational length disproves atomism.

The fifth argument applies the Pythagorean theorem to a right triangle. If legs are ten indivisible parts each, the hypotenuse would be √200, which is not an integer. This contradiction shows that indivisible parts cannot compose continuous lines.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic and translation carefully.
2
Recall the Pythagorean theorem
Understand that a² + b² = c² for a right triangle.
3
Apply the numbers
If each leg is 10 parts, then c² = 10² + 10² = 200.
4
Observe the contradiction
√200 is between 14 and 15, not a whole number, so the hypotenuse cannot be made of whole parts.
This contradicts the idea of indivisible parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : مربع وتر القائمة لمجموع مربعى الضلعين المحيطين بها . فإذا فرضنا كل ضلع عشرة أجزاء كان الوتر أكثر من أربعة عشر، وأقل من خمسة عشر، لكونه جذر مائتين . السادس : خط من جزءين فوق أحدهما جزء فهناك قائمة وترها فوق الاثنين، ودون الثلاثة، وإلا لزم كون وتر القائمة مساوية لكل من الضلعين أو لمجموعهما . السابع : مربع من انضمام أربعة خطوط كل منها من أربعة أجزاء، فالقطر إن كان منضم الأجزاء كان أربعة أجزاء مثل الضلع وهو محال . وإن كان مع خلاء بقدر الجزء كانت سبعة أجزاء مثل الضلعين وهو أيضاً محال أو أقل فيلزم الانقسام
Fifth: the square of the hypotenuse of a right triangle equals the sum of the squares of the two sides surrounding it. If we assume each side is ten parts, the hypotenuse is more than fourteen and less than fifteen, because it is the root of two hundred. Sixth: a line of two parts with a part above one of them, then there is a right triangle whose hypotenuse is above two and below three, otherwise it would require that the hypotenuse be equal to each of the two sides or to their sum. Seventh: a square formed by joining four lines each of four parts: if the diagonal is composed of parts, it would be four parts like the side, which is impossible; if there is a gap of the size of a part, it would be seven parts like the two sides, which is also impossible, or less, necessitating division.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct geometric arguments (the fifth, sixth, and seventh) against the doctrine of indivisible parts (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza'). Each argument shows that if lines and surfaces were composed of indivisible parts, contradictions would arise in basic geometric figures.

1. The fifth argument uses the Pythagorean theorem. In a right triangle, if each leg is ten parts, the hypotenuse would be √200, which is between fourteen and fifteen – a number not composed of whole parts, proving that the hypotenuse cannot be composed of a finite number of indivisible units.

2. The sixth argument considers a line of two parts with a part above it, forming a right triangle. The hypotenuse must be between two and three parts; if it were equal to either leg or their sum, contradictions arise, implying that the hypotenuse cannot be a whole number of parts.

3. The seventh argument examines a square made of four lines each four parts long. The diagonal: if composed of parts without gaps, it would be four parts (like the side), impossible; if with a gap equal to a part, it would be seven parts (like the sum of two sides), also impossible; if less, it requires division of a part. Thus, indivisible parts cannot account for the diagonal's length.

Key Terms
الوتر
القائمة
الجزء
جذر مائتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Pythagorean theorem is irrelevant to atomism.
Correction: The theorem shows that geometric lengths can be irrational, disproving that all lengths are whole multiples of an indivisible unit.

Checkpoint
In the fifth argument, why does the hypotenuse being √200 prove the impossibility of indivisible parts?
A) Because √200 is exactly 14 parts
B) Because √200 is between 14 and 15, not a whole number
C) Because √200 is less than 14
D) Because √200 equals 200

Key Takeaway
The Pythagorean theorem demonstrates that geometric lengths are not always whole numbers, disproving the atomistic claim that lines consist of finite indivisible parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that even a simple geometric truth like the length of a diagonal cannot be neatly defined by whole numbers, how does that challenge your desire for certainty and neat categories in your own understanding of Allah's creation? Are you willing to accept that some truths are beyond simple, finite measurement, and how does that affect your trust in Allah's infinite wisdom?
?
The atomists insisted on indivisible parts to make the world fully explainable. In your own life, what 'indivisible parts' do you cling to—habits, assumptions, or beliefs—that you treat as unbreakable? Will you let the truth of this argument break them open?
?
This proof shows that even a right triangle forces us to accept the irrational. What in your faith requires you to accept what seems irrational to human logic, and how does this strengthen your conviction?

Sixth Argument: Right Triangle with a Vertical Part
Taftazani's sixth geometric argument refuting atomism using a right triangle with a vertical part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a simple right triangle formed by two horizontal parts and one vertical part shows that the hypotenuse cannot be a whole number of indivisible parts.

Core Idea
A right triangle's hypotenuse lies between two and three parts, refuting atomism.

The sixth argument constructs a right triangle using a line of two parts with a third part placed vertically at one end. The hypotenuse must be longer than two but shorter than three parts, otherwise it would equal one of the sides—contradicting geometry. This shows that the hypotenuse contains a fraction of a part.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic and translation carefully.
2
Visualize the triangle
Imagine a horizontal line of two parts and a vertical line of one part at one end, forming a right triangle.
3
Consider possible lengths
The hypotenuse must be greater than two (the longer leg) and less than three (the sum of both legs).
4
Identify contradictions
If it were exactly two, it would equal the horizontal leg; if exactly three, it would equal the sum of legs—both impossible in a right triangle.
Thus the hypotenuse must be fractional, contradicting atomism.

Seventh Argument: Diagonal of a Square
Taftazani's seventh geometric argument refuting atomism using the diagonal of a square.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the diagonal of a square composed of indivisible parts leads to contradictions, refuting atomism.

Core Idea
The diagonal of a square cannot be a whole number of parts without contradiction.

The seventh argument considers a square made of four lines, each four parts long. The diagonal must be either composed of parts without gaps, in which case it would equal the side, or with a gap, making it equal the sum of two sides, both absurd. This demonstrates that indivisible parts cannot form continuous lines.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic and translation carefully.
2
Visualize the square
Imagine a square with sides of four indivisible parts each.
3
Consider the diagonal without gaps
If the diagonal is composed of parts without gaps, its length would be four parts, equal to the side—but the diagonal of a square is longer.
4
Consider the diagonal with a gap
If there is a gap equal to one part, the diagonal could be seven parts (or less), but the actual diagonal is √32 ≈ 5.657, not an integer—so any integer length is impossible.
Thus the diagonal cannot be made of indivisible parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?
What would it mean for your daily perception of size and distance if the physical world were actually discrete? Would you be able to reconcile that with the clear geometric truth demonstrated here?

Geometric Proofs Against Atomism: The First Three Arguments
Taftazani introduces geometric methods and presents three proofs (1-3) against indivisible parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani uses Euclidean geometric constructions to argue against the existence of indivisible parts (atomism).

Core Idea
Geometric operations force division where atomists claim indivisibility.

Taftazani now turns to geometry as a weapon against atomism. He introduces a method based on principles from Euclid's Elements, then presents three arguments showing that basic geometric operations—constructing an equilateral triangle, bisecting an angle, and bisecting a line—lead to contradictions if space is composed of indivisible parts. These arguments aim to demonstrate that the atomist position is geometrically untenable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Note the structure: an introductory remark, then three numbered arguments.
The arguments are labeled 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث'.
2
Understand the geometric method
Notice that Taftazani bases the proofs on Euclidean principles that he claims cannot be established without denying indivisible parts. Ask yourself: why would these operations fail if parts are indivisible?
3
Examine each argument in detail
For each of the three arguments, identify the geometric operation involved (equilateral triangle, angle bisection, line bisection) and how it leads to a contradiction under atomism.
The third argument includes a detailed construction; focus on the logical implication, not the construction steps.
4
Reflect on the overall strategy
Consider why Taftazani groups these three arguments together. They all rely on the same idea: geometric necessity forces division of what atomists claim is indivisible.
These arguments are part of a larger set of geometric proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي ومن تلك الطرق ما يبتنى على أصول هندسية لا سبيل إلى إثباتها، إلا على تقدير انتفاء الجزء كما يظهر للناظر في البراهين المذكورة في كتاب إقليدس، ولهذا هذا الطريق كثيرة جداً، ولنذكر عدة منها : وجوه كانت الأول: أنه يمكن لنا أن نعمل على كل خط شيئاً مثلثاً متساوي الأضلاع، ولا يتصور ذلك في الخط المركب من جزئين إلا بأن يقع جزء على ملتقى الجزءين، وقد عرفت أنه يوجب انقسام الثلاثة . الثاني : أن كل زاوية فإنه يمكن تنصيفها فيلزم تجزيء الجزء الذي هو ملتقى خطي الزاوية . الثالث: أن كل خط، فإنه يمكن تنصيفه، ففي المركب من الأجزاء الوتر يلزم انقسام الجزء الذي في الوسط وقد بين ذلك في الهندسة بأن يعمل على ذلك الخط مثلث متساوي الأضلاع، ثم تنصف الزاوية التي يؤثرها ذلك الخط بخط واصل منها إليه ، فتكون على منتصفه وبين منتصف الزاوية بأن يجعل خطاها متساويين ، ثم يوصل بين طرفيهما بخط يكون وتراً لها، ويعمل عليه من الطرف الآخر مثلث متساوي الأضلاع ، ثم يخرج خط من زاوية المثلث الأول إلى زاوية المثلث الثاني ماراً بالخط الذي هو وتر لهما فينتصف الزاوية، وبين عمل المثلث المتساوي الأضلاع على الخط بأن يرسم ببعده دائرتان يكون كل من طرفي الخط مركز الواحدة منهما، فيتقاطعان لا محالة، فيخرج من كل المركزين خط إلى نقطة تقاطع الدائرتين، ليحصل مثلث متساوي الأضلاع لكونها أنصاف أقطار الدائرتين المتساويتين، هذا ولكن لا سبيل إلى إثبات الدائرة على القائلين بالجزء على ما ستعرفه
That is, among these methods are those based on geometric principles that cannot be established except on the assumption of the non-existence of the part, as is apparent to the observer of the proofs mentioned in the book of Euclid. Hence these methods are very many, and let us mention several of them: arguments such as the first: that we can construct on every line an equilateral triangle, but this is inconceivable in a line composed of two parts unless a part falls on the junction of the two parts, and you know that necessitates the division of the three. The second: that every angle can be bisected, necessitating the division of the part that is the meeting point of the two lines of the angle. The third: that every line can be bisected; so in a line composed of parts, the bisector necessitates the division of the part in the middle. That has been shown in geometry by constructing on that line an equilateral triangle, then bisecting the angle that the line makes with a line connecting to it, so it is at its midpoint. The construction of the equilateral triangle on a line is done by drawing two circles with the two endpoints as centers, so they necessarily intersect, then drawing from each center a line to the intersection point, yielding an equilateral triangle because they are radii of two equal circles. This, however, there is no way to prove the circle to those who affirm the part, as you will learn.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage serves as an introduction to a new set of geometric proofs against atomism, followed by three specific arguments. The commentator (Taftazani) first explains that these methods rely on geometric principles from Euclid that cannot be proven without assuming the non-existence of indivisible parts (intifa' al-juz'). He then lists three arguments:

1. The first argument: An equilateral triangle can be constructed on any line. But if the line is composed of two indivisible parts, the apex of the triangle would have to fall on the junction of the two parts, which would require dividing one of the parts (or the junction itself). This leads to the division of the 'three' – referring to the indivisible units.

2. The second argument: Every angle can be bisected. This requires that the bisector passes through the vertex, which is the meeting point of the two lines. If the vertex were an indivisible part, bisection would necessitate splitting that part, which is impossible.

3. The third argument: Every line can be bisected. For a line composed of indivisible parts, the midpoint would fall on an indivisible part, requiring its division. Taftazani provides a detailed geometric construction for bisecting a line using an equilateral triangle and circles, emphasizing that this construction is valid only if circles exist continuously – but he notes that those who affirm indivisible parts cannot even prove the existence of a circle, as will be discussed later.

Key Terms
أصول هندسية
إقليدس
الجزء
مثلث متساوي الأضلاع
تنصيف الزاوية
تنصيف الخط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These arguments assume the existence of indivisible parts to show a contradiction.
Correction: These arguments do not assume existence of indivisible parts; they show that geometric operations (which are possible) lead to contradictions if parts existed.

Checkpoint
In the second argument, why does bisecting an angle refute the existence of indivisible parts?
A) The angle vertex must be divisible
B) The bisector requires the vertex to be a point
C) The bisector would split the vertex part
D) The angle cannot be bisected

Key Takeaway
Taftazani uses three geometric arguments to show that the existence of indivisible parts is incompatible with basic geometric constructions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that seemed reasonable until you subjected it to logical or geometric scrutiny? What did you do when you encountered a contradiction?
?
The atomists rejected the geometric foundations because they conflicted with their doctrine. Is there an area of your life where you dismiss evidence simply because it challenges your worldview? What would it take for you to honestly reconsider?
?
Taftazani acknowledges that his arguments cannot be proved to someone who rejects the very premises of geometry. When you encounter someone who denies your starting assumptions, how far are you willing to follow the argument? Are you more interested in being right or in finding truth?

The Circle and Sphere Arguments Against Atomism
Taftazani defines circle and sphere and argues they are incompatible with indivisible parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the geometric definitions of circles and spheres are used to argue against the possibility of indivisible parts (atomism) in kalam cosmology.

Core Idea
Circle and sphere geometry exposes contradictions in atomism.

Taftazani presents a fourth argument that uses basic geometry to challenge atomism. He first defines the circle and sphere in terms of rotation, then shows that their properties cannot be explained if bodies are composed of indivisible atoms. This passage demonstrates the depth of kalam reasoning using mathematical examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of circle and sphere
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Taftazani defines the circle as the surface generated by rotating a line segment around a fixed endpoint, and the sphere by rotating a semicircle around its diameter.
These definitions are constructive and rely on geometric operations.
2
Understand the dilemma posed for the circle
Taftazani sets up a dilemma: if a circle is composed of indivisible parts, either the outer parts are contiguous with the inner parts or not. Explore both branches and see how each leads to a contradiction.
Pay attention to the sub-case where inner and outer parts are equal in size.
3
Grasp the proof from concentric circles
Taftazani provides a proof: if the circle is made of indivisible parts, then all concentric circles (including those near the center and the outermost) would have the same circumference. This is obviously false.
The argument is cut off but the conclusion is clear: atomism leads to absurdity.
4
Reflect on the completeness of the argument
Note that the passage ends abruptly at 'واللزوم' (the implication). The intended conclusion is that the necessary consequences are impossible, thus the premise of atomism must be false.
The reasoning is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن كلاً من الدائرة والكرة ممكن بل متحقق . أما الدائرة فلأنا نتخيل على السطح المستوي خطاً مستقيماً متناهياً نثبت أحد طرفيه، ونديره حول طرفه الثابت إلى أن يعود إلى موضعه الأول، فيحصل سطح يحيط به مستدير حاصل من حركة الطرف المتحرك وفي باطنه نقطة الطرف الثابت ، جميع الخطوط الخارجة من تلك النقطة إلى ذلك المحيط متساوية ، لكون كل منها بقدر ذلك الخط الذي أدرناه، ولا نعني بالدائرة إلا ذلك السطح أو الخط المحيط به، وأما الكرة فلأنا إذا أثبتنا، قطر الدائرة، أعني الخط الخارج من المركز إلى المحيط في الجهتين، وأدرنا نصف الدائرة على ذلك الخط إلى أن يعود إلى وضعه الأول حصل سطح مستدير محيط بجسم في باطنه نقطة جميع الخطوط الخارجة منها إلى ذلك السطح متساوية، ولا نعني بالكرة إلا ذلك الجسم المحاط أو السطح المحيط، ثم إن كلاً من الدائرة والكرة ينافي كون الأجسام والخطوط والسطوح من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، أما الدائرة فلأنها لو كانت من أجزاء لا تتجزأ ، فإما أن تكون ظواهر الأجزاء متلاقية كبواطنها أولا، فعلى الأول إما أن تكون بواطنها أصغر من الظواهر فينقسم الجزء ، أو لا، فيساوي في المساحة باطن الدائرة، أعني المقعر ظاهرها . أعني المحدب وهو باطل بالضرورة، وإن شئت فالبرهان، وذلك أنه يستلزم تساوي جميع الدوائر المحاطة بها حتى التي بقرب المركز، وكذا جميع الدوائر المحيطة بها حتى المحيط بجميع الأجسام وبطلانه ضروري ، واللزوم
Fourth: Both the circle and sphere are possible, indeed real. As for the circle: we imagine on a plane surface a finite straight line, fix one of its ends, and rotate it around its fixed end until it returns to its original position. A round surface results bounded by the motion of the moving end, and inside it is the point of the fixed end. All lines extending from that point to the circumference are equal, because each is as long as the line we rotated. By 'circle' we mean only that surface or the line surrounding it. As for the sphere: if we fix the diameter of the circle – meaning the line from the center to the circumference in both directions – and rotate the semicircle around that line until it returns to its original position, a round surface results surrounding a body inside which is a point from which all lines to that surface are equal. By 'sphere' we mean only that enclosed body or the surrounding surface. Then, both the circle and sphere are incompatible with bodies, lines, and surfaces being composed of indivisible parts. As for the circle: if it were composed of indivisible parts, then either the outer parts are contiguous like the inner parts or not. In the first case, either the inner parts are smaller than the outer parts – then the part divides – or not, meaning the interior of the circle is equal in area to the exterior, i.e., the concave (interior) equals the convex (exterior), which is necessarily false. If you wish, the proof: that entails the equality of all circles enclosed by it, even those near the center, and likewise all circles surrounding it, even the one encompassing all bodies. Its falsity is necessary, and the implication...
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument, which uses the definitions of the circle and sphere to refute atomism. Taftazani first defines the circle as the surface generated by rotating a line segment around one fixed endpoint; all radii are equal. The sphere is defined by rotating a semicircle around its diameter. He then argues that both the circle and sphere are incompatible with the idea that bodies, lines, and surfaces are composed of indivisible parts (ajza' la tatajazza').

For the circle, he sets up a dilemma: if the circle is composed of indivisible parts, the parts on the circumference (outer parts) and those inside (inner parts) could be contiguous or not. If they are contiguous, two subcases arise: either the inner parts are smaller than the outer parts (which would mean a part is divided) or they are equal. If equal, then the interior area (concave) would equal the exterior area (convex) – an obvious impossibility. Alternatively, if not contiguous, a gap would exist, also leading to contradiction. He then gives a proof that this would force all concentric circles to have the same circumference, which is absurd. The argument is cut off, but the conclusion is that indivisible parts cannot account for the properties of circles and spheres.

Key Terms
الدائرة
الكرة
الجزء
المحيط
المركز
المقعر
المحدب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The circle argument assumes the actual existence of perfect circles in the physical world.
Correction: Taftazani uses the geometric possibility of circles and spheres, not their actual existence. He argues that if atoms existed, the necessary geometric properties of these shapes would be contradicted, so atomism cannot be true.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani's argument, what would happen if a circle were composed of indivisible parts and its inner and outer parts are equal?
A) The circle would have no center
B) The concave area would equal the convex area
C) The parts would not be contiguous
D) The radius would be zero

Key Takeaway
The geometric properties of circles and spheres provide a powerful argument against atomism by showing that indivisible parts cannot account for continuous curved shapes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Taftazani uses precise geometric reasoning to expose an inconsistency in atomism. In your own life, are there areas where you avoid examining the logical coherence of your beliefs? What would it take for you to follow the argument wherever it leads, even if it unsettles you?
?
This argument about atoms may seem abstract, but it teaches that reality is consistent and logical. Do you live as if truth matters, or do you compartmentalize your faith and your reasoning? What would change if you committed to letting logic and revelation guide your entire worldview?

Sixth and Seventh Arguments Against Atomism
Two mathematical proofs against atomism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two geometric proofs from Euclid are used to refute the idea that a line is composed of indivisible parts.

Core Idea
Geometry proves that atoms cannot compose a line without contradiction.

In this passage, the author presents two mathematical arguments to refute atomism. The first uses a right triangle to show that the hypotenuse cannot be a whole number of parts. The second constructs a square and shows that the diagonal must either equal the side or require fractions, which is impossible if parts are indivisible. These arguments demonstrate the logical necessity of continuous magnitude.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The arguments aim to disprove atomism—the view that a line is made of indivisible parts. The author uses geometry to show contradictions.
2
Examine the sixth argument
We assume a right triangle with legs of two parts and one part. According to Euclid, the hypotenuse must be less than three and greater than two, but not a whole number of parts.
This shows that the hypotenuse length cannot be expressed as an integer multiple of the assumed minimal part.
3
Grasp the seventh argument
We construct a square with sides of four parts each. The diagonal is formed by connecting corresponding parts. Either the diagonal equals the side (refuted by geometry) or there are gaps; if gaps, then either each gap accommodates a part (diagonal=seven parts, also false) or a part must divide—contradicting indivisibility.
The 'shape of the bride' is a known geometric figure that shows diagonal and side cannot be equal.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Both arguments show that assuming indivisible parts leads to geometric impossibility, thus refuting atomism.
This supports the Ash'ari view of continuous matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : نفرض خطاً من جزءين، فنضع فوق أحدهما جزءاً، فتحصل زاوية قائمة، فوترها يجب أن يكون أقل من الثلاثة وأكثر من الاثنين، لما بين إقليدس أن وتر القائمة أقل من مجموع ضلعيها، وأكثر من كل منهما. السابع : نفرض مربعاً من أربعة خطوط مستقيمة، مضمومة بعضها إلى البعض، على غاية ما يمكن كل منها من أربعة أجزاء، فقطره خط يحصل من الجزء الأول من الخط الأول والثاني من الثاني والثالث من الثالث والرابع من الرابع . فإن كانت متلاقية كان القطر مساوياً للضلع ، ويبطله شكل العروس، وإن كان بينها فرج ولا تكون إلا ثلاثاً، فإما أن يسع كل جزء جزءاً فيكون القطر كالضلعين سبعة أجزاء، وهو باطل بالشكل الجاري، أو أقل فينقسم الجزء، وبما ذكرنا من استقامة الخطوط وتضامها على غاية ما يمكن يظهر امتناع أن تقع الفرج فيما بين بعض الأجزاء دون البعض .
The sixth: We assume a line of two parts, then we place a part over one of them, so a right angle is obtained, and its hypotenuse must be less than three and greater than two, because Euclid showed that the hypotenuse of a right triangle is less than the sum of its two legs and greater than each of them. The seventh: We assume a square of four straight lines, joined to one another, each of which is at most four parts, and its diagonal is a line obtained from the first part of the first line, the second of the second, the third of the third, and the fourth of the fourth. Then if they meet, the diagonal equals the side, and the 'shape of the bride' refutes it. If there are gaps, and there can only be three, then either each part accommodates a part, so the diagonal is like the two sides: seven parts, which is false by the usual figure, or less, so the part divides. And by what we mentioned of the straightness of the lines and their contiguity to the utmost extent, it becomes evident that it is impossible for gaps to occur among some parts without others.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents two mathematical arguments (السادس and السابع) aimed at refuting atomism by showing contradictions that would arise if a line were composed of indivisible parts. The first argument considers a right triangle with legs of two units and one unit; the hypotenuse would have to be an irrational length, not a whole number, contradicting the assumption that all lengths are multiples of a minimum unit. The second argument constructs a square from four lines each composed of four parts; the diagonal must also be a multiple of parts, but geometry shows it cannot be equal to the side or product of parts without gaps or division. Both arguments demonstrate the impossibility of a continuous line being composed of discrete, indivisible parts.

Key Terms
جزء
وتر
قطر
ضلع
زاوية قائمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These arguments are merely mathematical puzzles without theological significance.
Correction: These geometric proofs are used by the author to refute atomism, a key metaphysical doctrine in kalam, showing that matter must be continuous.

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of the sixth and seventh arguments in this passage?
A) To prove the existence of indivisible parts
B) To show contradictions in assuming lines are composed of indivisible parts
C) To establish Euclidean geometry as a foundation for kalam
D) To argue that the atom is spherical

Key Takeaway
Geometric proofs demonstrate that assuming lines are composed of indivisible parts leads to contradictions, refuting atomism.

First Objection: Are Parts Essential Attributes?
Objection that the part must be essential, and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that parts are self-evidently essential only applies to conceptual parts, not physical components.

Core Idea
Essentiality of parts depends on whether they are rational or external.

The first objection against indivisible parts argues that if a body has such parts, they must be essential and self-evident. The refutation clarifies that this is only true for rational parts like genus and species, not for physical parts like hyle and form, which require demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is presented. Notice the claim that if a body is composed of indivisible parts, the part must be essential to it and self-evident.
The objection assumes that 'part' is straightforwardly essential.
2
Identify the refutation's key distinction
The refutation responds by distinguishing between rational parts (like genus and differentia) and external parts (like hyle and form). Understand that only rational parts are self-evident when the essence is conceived.
3
Examine the examples given
Look at the examples: rational parts (genus, differentia) are intelligible; external parts (hyle, form) need proof. Also note the point about the intellect and the soul's substantiality.
This illustrates why the objection fails.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this distinction saves the theory of indivisible parts from the objection. The part's essential nature is not conceded for physical components.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : لو كان الجسم من أجزاء لا تتجزأ لكان الجزء ذاتياً له، متعقلاً قيل تعقله بين الثبوت له غير مفتقر إلى البيان، ولا منكراً عند كثير من العقلاء. ذلك ورد بأن ذلك إنما هو في الأجزاء العقلية كالأجناس والفصول، ومع فيشترط تعقل الماهية بحقيقتها، وأما الجزء الخارجي فقد يفتقر إلى البيان ، كالهيولي والصورة عندكم ، وكذا العقل إذا لم تتصور الماهية بحقيقتها، لجوهرية النفس وتجردها.
The first: If the body were composed of indivisible parts, then the part would be essential to it, intelligible, it is said its intelligibility is evident for it, not in need of demonstration, and not denied by many rational people. That was responded to by saying that this is only with regard to rational parts like genera and differentiae, and here it is conditional upon conceiving the essence in its reality. As for the external part, it may need demonstration, like hyle and form according to you, and likewise the intellect if one does not conceive the essence in its reality, due to the substantiality of the soul and its immateriality.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection (الأول) against the theory that the body is composed of indivisible parts, along with its refutation. The objection claims: if a body consists of indivisible parts, then the part must be an essential (ذاتي) attribute of the body, and this is self-evident and not denied. The refutation (ورد) distinguishes between rational parts (أجزاء عقلية), such as genus and differentia, which are indeed essential and intelligible once the essence is known, and external parts (أجزاء خارجية), which are not self-evident and require demonstration—like hyle and form in Aristotelian physics. The refutation also notes that even the intellect, due to the soul's substantiality, may not grasp essences directly. Thus the part's essential nature is not conceded.

Key Terms

ذاتي
أجزاء عقلية
أجزاء خارجية
هيولي
صورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All parts, whether conceptual or physical, are self-evidently essential to the whole.
Correction: Only rational parts (like genus and differentia) are self-evident once the essence is known; physical parts (like hyle and form) require demonstration.

Checkpoint

A) By denying that the part exists at all
B) By distinguishing between rational and external parts
C) By asserting that all parts are self-evident
D) By appealing to consensus of rational people

Key Takeaway
The essential nature of a part depends on whether it is a rational concept or an external physical component; only rational parts are self-evident.

Second Objection: Finite Part Must Be Shaped and Its Refutation
Objection that a finite part must have a shape, implying division, and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the indivisible part—that a finite part must be shaped and therefore divisible—and how the refutation limits the argument to composite bodies.

Core Idea
Shape implies division only for composites, not atoms.

The second objection argues that if an indivisible part exists, it must be finite and thus have a shape—either spherical or polygonal. Both shapes lead to further division, either obviously or through gaps that arise when spheres are joined. The refutation, however, concedes the shape but points out that the gap problem only applies to large spherical bodies, not to the atoms themselves. This lesson unpacks the logic and the counter.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and the literal translation. Notice the tight argument structure: objection then refutation.
The objection uses two cases: polygon and sphere.
2
Identify the Core Claim
The objection claims that a finite part must be shaped (sphere or polygon) and that both shapes imply further division. Understand why the polygon case is 'obvious' and why the sphere case relies on gaps.
3
Examine the Refutation
The refutation accepts that the part is shaped but denies that the gap argument applies to indivisible parts. It says the gap reasoning holds only for spherical bodies (الأجسام الكرية), not for parts (الأجزاء).
This is a key distinction between atoms and composite bodies.
4
Reflect on the Broader Context
Consider how this objection and refutation fit into the larger debate on atomism. The defenders of atoms are blocking an argument that tries to prove infinite divisibility.
This lesson is part of a series on the kalam atom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : لو وجد الجزء لكان متناهياً ضرورة، وكان متشكلاً كرة أو مضلعاً لأن المحيطة به إما حد واحد أو أكثر، وكل منهما يستلزم الانقسام، أما المضلع فظاهر وأما الكرة فلأنه لا بد عند انضمام الكرات من تخلل فرج يكون منها أقل من الكرة . ورد بعد تسليم تشكل الجزء بأن ذلك إنما هو في الأجسام الكرية دون الأجزاء.
The second: If the part existed, it would be finite necessarily, and it would be shaped as a sphere or a polygon, because what surrounds it is either one boundary or more, and each entails division. As for the polygon, it is obvious; as for the sphere, because when spheres are joined, gaps necessarily occur that are smaller than the sphere. It was responded, after conceding the shaping of the part, that this is only with regard to spherical bodies, not parts.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage gives the second objection (الثاني) against the indivisible part. The objection argues: if a part exists, it must be finite (متناهي) and therefore must have a shape—either spherical or polygonal—because whatever encloses it has either one boundary or multiple boundaries. Both shapes imply possible further division: polygons obviously divide, and spheres when joined create gaps smaller than the sphere itself, implying a smaller unit. The refutation (ورد) concedes that parts have shape but restricts the gap argument to large spherical bodies (الأجسام الكرية), not to the indivisible parts themselves. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms

متشكل
كرة
مضلع
فرج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an atom has a shape, it must be divisible like any shaped object.
Correction: The refutation shows that the argument about gaps and division only applies to composite bodies, not to the indivisible atoms themselves. Atoms can have a shape without being divisible.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why does the second objection fail?
A) The part is not finite
B) The part has no shape
C) The argument about gaps only applies to spherical bodies, not to parts
D) The part is infinite

Key Takeaway
The second objection fails because the refutation restricts the claim about gaps to spherical bodies, not to the indivisible atoms themselves.
