Shadows and Indivisible Parts: Third Objection Refuted
Objection using shadow proportions to claim part division and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the shadow argument against atomism is refuted by denying its universal applicability.

Core Idea
Shadows only prove division for bodies that have a half.

This lesson examines the third objection to atomism, which uses shadow lengths to argue that even indivisible parts must be divisible. The refutation reveals a crucial flaw: the objection assumes a property that only applies to divisible bodies. Understanding this refutation sharpens the kalam defense of the indivisible part.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how the objection claims that every body has a shadow twice its length at some time, leading to half of the shadow being the shadow of half the body.
The phrasing uses 'لا شك' to assert certainty.
2
Identify the refutation's move
The refutation responds with 'منع الكلية'—denying the universal premise. It argues that the shadow relationship only holds for bodies that actually have a half.
This restricts the claim to divisible bodies, not indivisible parts.
3
Connect to atomism
Understand why this refutation is important: if the part were divisible, atomism would be false. The refutation protects the doctrine by showing the objection assumes what it tries to prove.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: لا شك أن كل جسم يصير ظله مثليه في وقت ما وحينئذ يكون بالضرورة نصف ظله ظل النصف ففي المركب من الأجزاء وتر يكون شفعاً له نصف هو ظل نصف ذلك الجسم فينتصف الجسم وينقسم الجزء . ورد بمنع الكلية، وإنما ذلك فيما يكون له نصف.
The third: There is no doubt that every body's shadow becomes twice its length at some time, and then necessarily half of its shadow is the shadow of its half. So in the composite of parts, a chord? is an even number? for it half is the shadow of half that body, so the body is halved and the part divides. It was responded by denying the generality, and that this applies only to that which has a half.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection (الثالث) against atomism, based on the behavior of shadows. The objection claims: for any body, at a certain time its shadow becomes twice its length; then necessarily half of the shadow equals the shadow of half the body. This would imply that a body composed of parts has a half, thus the part could be halved, leading to division of the indivisible part. The refutation (ورد) denies the universal premise (منع الكلية): this property only holds for bodies that actually have a half—i.e., that are divisible. It does not apply to indivisible parts, which by definition have no half. Thus the argument is circular.

Key Terms
ظل
نصف
منع الكلية
جزء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The shadow argument proves that every body, including indivisible parts, must have a half and thus be divisible.
Correction: The shadow relationship only applies to bodies that already have a half; it does not apply to indivisible parts, which by definition have no half.

Checkpoint
Why does the refutation reject the shadow argument?
A) Because shadows are not real
B) Because the relationship only applies to bodies that actually have a half
C) Because the part is too small to cast a shadow
D) Because the argument conflates mathematical and physical objects

Key Takeaway
The shadow argument fails because it assumes all bodies have halves, which the atomist denies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The objection failed because it assumed atoms have halves. Are there any beliefs you hold that you have not critically examined for circular reasoning? Commit to honestly questioning one such assumption this week.

The First Argument: Essential Parts and Intellectual vs. External Parts
Sanusi presents and refutes the first argument that indivisible parts must be essential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against indivisible parts and why the refutation distinguishes between intellectual and external parts.

Core Idea
The part being 'essential' depends on whether it is intellectual or external—a key distinction in Islamic ontology.

Sanusi begins with an argument that if bodies have indivisible parts, those parts would be self-evidently essential to the body. However, this reasoning only holds for intellectual parts like genera and species, not for external parts like hyle and form. The refutation clarifies this distinction, preserving the doctrine of indivisible parts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the argument
Read the first sentence: 'If the body were composed of indivisible parts, then the part would be essential to it...' Note what the argument claims about conceivability and denial by rational people.
2
Examine the refutation
The refutation begins with 'that is refuted by...' It distinguishes intellectual parts (genera, differentiae) from external parts (hyle, form). Understand why this distinction matters.
External parts may require explanation unlike self-evident intellectual parts.
3
Apply the distinction
Consider how the refutation protects the doctrine of indivisible parts. The argument's premise about self-evidence only applies to a different category of parts.
This shows the importance of precise metaphysical vocabulary in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي من تلك الطرق ما يبتنى على مقدمات هي بصدد المنع وهي وجوه : الأول : لو كان الجسم من أجزاء لا تتجزأ لكان الجزء ذاتياً له، متعقلاً قيل تعقله بين الثبوت له غير مفتقر إلى البيان، ولا منكراً عند كثير من العقلاء. ذلك ورد بأن ذلك إنما هو في الأجزاء العقلية كالأجناس والفصول، ومع فيشترط تعقل الماهية بحقيقتها، وأما الجزء الخارجي فقد يفتقر إلى البيان ، كالهيولي والصورة عندكم ، وكذا العقل إذا لم تتصور الماهية بحقيقتها، لجوهرية النفس وتجردها . الثاني : لو وجد الجزء لكان متناهياً ضرورة، وكان متشكلاً كرة أو مضلعاً لأن المحيطة به إما حد واحد أو أكثر، وكل منهما يستلزم الانقسام، أما المضلع فظاهر وأما الكرة فلأنه لا بد عند انضمام الكرات من تخلل فرج يكون منها أقل من الكرة . ورد بعد تسليم تشكل الجزء بأن ذلك إنما هو في الأجسام الكرية دون الأجزاء. الثالث: لا شك أن كل جسم يصير ظله مثليه في وقت ما وحينئذ يكون بالضرورة نصف ظله ظل النصف ففي المركب (ظن ظل نصفه، فظل الجسم الذي طوله أجزاء وتر تكون شفعاً له نصف هو ظل نصف ذلك الجسم فينتصف الجسم وينقسم الجزء . ورد بمنع الكلية، وإنما ذلك فيما يكون له نصف.
From those methods, what is built on premises that are subject to objection, and they are aspects: First: If the body were composed of indivisible parts, then the part would be essential to it, rationally conceivable, it is said its conception is clear and does not need explanation, and it is not denied by many rational people. That is refuted by that it is only in intellectual parts like genera and differentiae, and there it requires conceiving the quiddity by its reality. As for the external part, it may need explanation, like hyle and form according to you, and likewise the intellect if the quiddity is not conceived by its reality, due to the substance of the soul and its abstraction. Second: If the part existed, it would necessarily be finite, and would be shaped as a sphere or polygon, because what surrounds it is either one boundary or more, and each entails division. As for the polygon it is obvious, and as for the sphere, because when spheres are joined, there must be gaps that are less than the sphere. It is refuted after conceding the shaping of the part by that it is only in spherical bodies, not in parts. Third: There is no doubt that every body's shadow becomes double at some time, and then necessarily half of its shadow is the shadow of half, so in the composite (suppose the shadow of its half), then the shadow of a body whose length is an odd number of parts is the shadow of half of that body, so the body is halved and the part divides. It is refuted by denying the universality, and that only occurs for what has a half.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct arguments (وجوه) against the doctrine that bodies are composed of indivisible parts (جزء لا يتجزأ), each followed by a refutation. The first argument claims that if bodies have indivisible parts, those parts would be self-evidently essential to the body, which is not universally accepted. The refutation distinguishes between intellectual parts (like genera and differentiae) and external parts; only intellectual parts are self-evident, while external parts like hyle and form require explanation. The second argument asserts that an indivisible part would have a shape (sphere or polygon), which leads to division. The refutation concedes the part would be shaped but insists that division only applies to spherical bodies, not to the parts themselves. The third argument uses the ratio of shadows: at a certain time, a body's shadow doubles, implying that half the shadow corresponds to half the body, leading to division of the part. The refutation denies the universal claim, noting that only bodies with a half (i.e., that are not minimal) undergo such division. Thus each refutation targets a specific assumption, preserving the logical coherence of the doctrine of indivisible parts against these objections.

Correction: Only intellectual parts are self-evidently essential; external parts may require explanation and are not self-evident.

A) Because indivisible parts do not exist.
B) Because it only applies to intellectual parts, not external parts.
C) Because the body is not composed of parts.
D) Because self-evidence is impossible for any part.

Key Takeaway
The first argument against indivisible parts fails because it confuses intellectual parts with external parts; only the former are self-evident.

The Second Argument: Shape and Finitude, and the Refutation
Sanusi presents and refutes the second argument about shape and finitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that indivisible parts must have a shape leading to division, and why the refutation limits this to spherical bodies.

Core Idea
Even if an indivisible part has a shape, division only follows for whole bodies, not for the part itself.

The second objection argues that an indivisible part must be finite and hence have a shape—either spherical or polygonal—which implies it can be divided. Sanusi's refutation concedes the part has a shape but insists that the inference of division only applies to spherical bodies, not to the parts themselves. This subtle distinction preserves the indivisibility of the part.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument
Read the second argument: if a part exists, it must be finite and have a shape. Why does that lead to division? Because a polygonal shape has sides, and a sphere when joined creates gaps smaller than the sphere.
The argument relies on the idea that any shape implies composition and thus division.
2
Examine the refutation
The refutation concedes the part has a shape but says the division from shape only applies to whole spherical bodies, not to the parts themselves. Why? Because the logic of gaps and multiple sides does not hold for a minimal part.
This illustrates the principle that what is true for the whole may not be true for the part.
3
Appreciate the precision
Note that the refutation does not deny the part is finite or shaped; it just denies that this entails divisibility. This shows the importance of careful reasoning in kalam.

Source Text

The Third Argument: Shadows and Division, and the Refutation
Sanusi presents and refutes the third argument from shadow ratios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from shadow ratios that supposedly forces division of indivisible parts, and why the refutation denies its universality.

Core Idea
The shadow argument only applies to bodies that have a half, not to minimal indivisible parts.

The third argument uses the fact that at a certain time a body's shadow doubles, implying that half the shadow corresponds to half the body. For a body with an odd number of parts, this would force the body to be halved, leading to division of an indivisible part. The refutation denies the universal claim, pointing out that this reasoning only works for bodies that actually have a half—i.e., that are divisible. Thus the argument begs the question.

Guided Path
1
Understand the shadow argument
Read: 'every body's shadow becomes double at some time' – this means the shadow length doubles. Therefore half the shadow corresponds to half the body. For a body with an odd number of parts, halving would split a part.
The argument assumes that any body can be halved.
2
Identify the problem
The argument implicitly assumes the body has a half. But if the body is composed of minimal parts, it may not have a half (e.g., odd number of parts). The refutation points out that the shadow ratio only applies to bodies that have a half.
This is a logical critique: the argument's premise is only valid for divisible bodies.
3
Examine the refutation
The refutation says 'by denying the universality' – the principle is not universal. It only holds for bodies that have a half, i.e., that are not composed of an odd number of indivisible parts.
Thus the argument does not prove that indivisible parts must divide.
4
Reflect on the strategy
Sanusi shows that the argument relies on a hidden assumption that the body is divisible. This is a classic kalam technique: exposing question-begging premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Definitions of Qisma: Language, Mathematics, and Logic
Al-Taftazani defines qisma linguistically, mathematically, and logically, with two logical types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct meanings of the term 'qisma' (division) in Arabic language, mathematics, and logic, and how logicians classify it into two types.

Core Idea
Division is not just arithmetic; in logic, it is the art of classifying concepts.

Al-Taftazani begins by clarifying the term 'qisma' which appears throughout kalam and logic texts. He provides its linguistic root, its mathematical operation, and its specialized logical sense where it becomes a tool for analysis and classification. This terminological grounding is essential before encountering arguments that rely on division.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three domains: language, mathematics, logic.
2
Distinguish the two logical aspects
The logicians identify two types of division: reduction of a composite to its parts (tajzi'a/taḥlīl) and reduction of a universal to its particulars (by adding restrictions).
The first is analysis, the second is classification into subspecies or individuals.
3
Examine the dichotomous division example
Study the example of politics being divided by successive dichotomies until its meaning is determined. This illustrates the Platonic ideal of division.
The chain ends when the concept is sufficiently delimited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسمة في اللغة اسم من انقسام الشيء، وعند الرياضيين تجزئة الشيء، فإذا أردت ان تقسم عدداً على آخر جزان الأول بقدر العدد الثاني، ويسمى الأول بالمقسوم والثاني بالمقسوم عليه، والناتج خارج القسمة . أما عند المنطقيين فالقسمة مرادفة للتقسيم، وهو ارجاع التصور الى أقسامه ولها عندهم وجهان الأول: ارجاع المركب الى أجزائه أو عناصره، ويسمى هذا الارجاع تجزئة أو تحليلا والثاني ارجاع الكلي الى جزئياته أو انقسام الكلي بحسب الماصدق الى أصناف أو أفراد تندرج وسبيل ذلك أن يضاف الى ذلك الكلي قيد يخصه فينشأ عن هذه الإضافة مفهوم جديد يسمى والقسمة الثنائية : هي المثل الأعلى عند أفلاطون مثال ذلك قولنا السياسة علم، والعلم نظري وعملي، والسياسة تدخل في النظري والعلم النظري علم يأمر وعلم يقرر والسياسة تدخل في العلم الذي يأمر ، وهكذا دواليك حتى يتحدد معنى السياسة .
Division (qisma) in language is a noun from the division of a thing. According to mathematicians, it is the partitioning of a thing: when you want to divide a number by another, you partition the first by the measure of the second, and the first is called the dividend and the second the divisor, and the result is the quotient. As for the logicians, division (qisma) is synonymous with classification (taqsīm), which is the reduction of a concept to its divisions. It has two aspects: first, the reduction of a composite to its parts or elements, and this reduction is called partitioning or analysis; second, the reduction of a universal to its particulars, or the division of a universal by instantiations into types or individuals that fall under it, and the way of that is to add to that universal a specific restriction, and from this addition a new concept arises called... And dichotomous division is the ideal according to Plato, for example our saying: politics is a science, and science is theoretical and practical, and politics falls under the theoretical, and theoretical science is a science that commands and a science that determines, and politics falls under the science that commands, and so on until the meaning of politics is determined.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a terminological definition and classification of the word qisma (division). It begins with the linguistic meaning: a noun derived from 'division of a thing'. Then it gives the mathematical sense: partitioning a number by another, with terms dividend, divisor, and quotient. For logicians, qisma is equivalent to taqsīm (classification), defined as the reduction of a concept to its divisions. There are two types: (1) reduction of a composite to its parts or elements, called tajzi'a (partitioning) or taḥlīl (analysis); (2) reduction of a universal to its particulars, i.e., dividing the universal by its instantiations into types or individuals, done by adding a specific restriction to the universal, generating a new concept (the text appears to cut off before naming it). The passage then gives an example of dichotomous division (al-qisma al-thunā'iyya), the ideal according to Plato: starting with 'politics is a science', then dividing science into theoretical and practical, placing politics under theoretical, then dividing theoretical science into that which commands and that which determines, and so on until the meaning of politics is delimited.

Key Terms
قسمة
تقسيم
تجزئة
تحليل
القسمة الثنائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think qisma refers only to mathematical division or only to splitting physical objects.
Correction: In logic, qisma (taqsīm) is a method of classifying concepts, either by analysis (breaking composites) or by division of universals into particulars via added restrictions.

Checkpoint
According to the logicians, what are the two aspects of qisma (division)?
A) Division into numbers and division into concepts.
B) Reduction of a composite to its parts and reduction of a universal to its particulars.
C) Division by cutting and division by imagination.
D) Division into stable and non-stable accidents.

Key Takeaway
Qisma in logic means classification, which can be either analysis of composites or division of universals into particulars, with dichotomous division as the ideal method.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to understand a new religious concept, do you instinctively break it into parts (analysis) or categorize it as an example of a broader universal (classification)? Which approach from this lesson do you neglect, and what might you discover if you used the other?
?
The dichotomous division example starts with 'politics is a science' and splits until the meaning is determined. Pick a belief you hold about Islam—like 'prayer is important'—and attempt to apply this dichotomous method. What do you learn about the layers of your own conviction?
?
The passage distinguishes between mathematical division and logical classification. In your daily thinking, do you ever confuse a process of dividing (like arithmetic) with a process of classifying (like categorizing people or ideas)? How might that confusion affect your understanding of your own faith?

Objection to Bodies' Equality and Its Refutation
The author responds to an objection and clarifies its weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that bodies may differ in essence is considered far‑fetched in the context of proving hyle.

Core Idea
The singular body leaves no room for essential difference; the objection misses the point.

Sanusi responds to an objection that denies the equality of bodies in essence, which would undermine the proof for hyle. He argues that the objection is based on a remote possibility because the discussion concerns the singular body, where difference in nature is inconceivable. Let's read his response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Identify the objection that denies equality of bodies and Sanusi's initial refutation.
2
Understand the remote possibility
Note that the objection is considered far‑fetched because the discussion is about the singular body.
3
Interpret the claim about division
See how a statement about division producing duality must be understood as applying to the singular body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما اعتراضه بمنع تساوي الأجسام في الماهية ، فلا يندفع بأن مبنى الكلام على اعترافهم يكون تلك البسائط متساوية في الطبع ، لأن مراده على ما صرح به في المباحث المشرقية هو أنه لو ادعى مدع أنها متخالفة بالماهية ، وأنه لا يوجد جزآن متحدان في الماهية لم يثبت أن كل جسم قابل للقسمة والانفكاكية، فلم يتم دليل على إثبات الهيولي. لكن لا خفاء في أنه احتمال بعيد لأن الكلام في الجسم المفرد الذي لا يعقل فيه اختلاف طبيعة وعلى هذا ينبغي أن يحمل قول من قال إن القسمة بأنواعها تحدث في المقسوم أثنينية تساوي طباع كل واحد طباع المجموع على القسمة الواردة على الجسم المفرد، وإلا ففساده واضح، وفسر الطباع بمصدر الصفة الذاتية الأولية للشيء حركة أو سكوناً ، كان أو غيرهما، فيكون أعم من الطبيعة، وفسر أنواع القسمة بما يكون بحسب الفتك والقطع ، أو بحسب الوهم والفرض، أو بحسب اختلاف عرضين ،قارين، أي ما هو للموضوع في نفسه كالسواد والبياض، أو غير قارين أي ما هو له بالقياس إلى الغير كالتماس والتحاذي، وذلك لأن الانقسام إن تأدى إلى الافتراق فالأول، وإلا فإن كان في مجرد الوهم ،فالثاني وإلا فالثالث وبما ذكرنا من اعتبار مجرد الوهم صار هذا قسماً ثالثاً، وإلا فهو من قبيل الانقسام الوهمي والفرضي بدليل قولهم : إن الجزء ما لا ينقسم لا كسراً ولا قطعاً، ولا وهماً ولا فرضاً، من غير تعرض لما يكون باختلاف عرضين، وذلك للقطع بأن الجسم الذي يتسخن بعضه، أو وقع الضوء على بعضه، أو لاقى ببعضه جسماً آخر، لم يحصل فيه الانفصال بالفعل، وبحسب الخارج ولم يصر جسمين ، ثم إذا زال التسخن أو الضوء أو الملاقاة عاد جسماً واحداً . ولو كان كذلك لكانت المسافة تصير أقساماً غير متناهية في الخارج بحسب موافاة المتحرك حدوداً، ثم تعود متصلة في نفسها واحدة في ذاتها عند انقطاع الحركة، وما يقال إنا قاطعون بأن محل البياض من الجسم غير محل السواد منه مسلم ، لكن باعتبار اختلاف العرضين لا بالنظر إلى ذات الجسم بحيث يعرض له انفصال وتميز في الخارج، بل بالفرض العقلي، ولهذا قال في الشفاء، ومن الذي بالفرض اختصاص العرض ببعض دون بعض، حتى إذا زال ذلك العرض زال ذلك الاختصاص، مثل جسم تبيض لا كله فبفرض له بالبياض جزء، إذا زال ذلك البياض زال افتراضه، فما ذكر في شرح الإشارات من أن الانفصال بحسب اختلاف العرضين انفصال في الخارج من غير تأد إلى الافتراق يحمل على أنه لأمر في الخارج، وما ذكر في منطق الشفاء من أنه انفصال بالفعل يحمل على فعل الأذهان دون الأعيان
As for his objection by denying the equality of bodies in essence, it is not refuted by [saying] that the basis of the discourse is on their acknowledgment that those simple [bodies] are equal in nature, because his intent — as he stated in al-Mabaḥith al-Mashriqiyya — is that if someone claimed that they are different in essence and that no two parts exist that are united in essence, it would not be established that every body is divisible and separable, so the proof for hyle would not be complete. But it is not hidden that this is a remote possibility, because the discussion is about the singular body in which difference of nature is not conceivable. On this basis, one should interpret the saying of whoever said that division in its kinds produces in the divided [object] a duality equalizing the nature of each one with the nature of the whole — [this refers] to division occurring on the singular body; otherwise its corruption is clear. And he interpreted 'nature' as the source of the primary essential attribute of a thing, whether movement or rest or something else, so it is more general than 'natural constitution'. And he interpreted the kinds of division as [that which is] according to tearing and cutting, or according to estimation and assumption, or according to difference of two accidents: permanent, i.e., what belongs to the subject in itself like blackness and whiteness; or non-permanent, i.e., what belongs to it in relation to another like contact and adjacency. That is because division: if it leads to separation, it is the first; otherwise, if it is in mere estimation, it is the second; otherwise, it is the third. And by our mentioning consideration of mere estimation, this became a third kind; otherwise it belongs to the category of estimative and hypothetical division, as evidenced by their saying: 'The indivisible part is that which is not divisible by breaking or cutting, nor by estimation or assumption,' without mentioning what is by difference of accidents. That is because of the certainty that a body some of which becomes hot, or light falls on some of it, or it touches another body with some of it, does not undergo actual separation externally and does not become two bodies; then when the heat, light, or contact ceases, it becomes one body. And if it were otherwise, distance would become infinite divisions externally according to the moving body's meeting boundaries, then become continuous in itself as one in its essence when motion ceases. And what is said — 'We are certain that the location of whiteness in the body is not the location of blackness' — is granted, but in consideration of the difference of accidents, not with regard to the essence of the body such that separation and distinction occur externally; rather, by intellectual assumption. For this reason, it is said in al-Shifā': 'And who [holds] that by assumption the accident is specified to some [parts] without others, such that when that accident ceases, that specification ceases, like a body that whitens not entirely, so by assumption it has a part for whiteness; when that whiteness ceases, its assumption ceases.' So what is mentioned in the Commentary on al-Ishārāt — that separation according to difference of accidents is an external separation without leading to detachment — is interpreted as [being] due to something external. And what is mentioned in the Logic of al-Shifā' — that it is an actual separation — is interpreted as [being] an act of minds, not external things.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage begins by responding to an objection against the proof for hyle. The objection denies that all bodies are equal in essence, which would undermine the argument that bodies are composed of matter and form. The author explains that this objection is far-fetched because the discussion concerns the singular body, where no difference in nature is conceivable. He then clarifies a statement about division producing duality, insisting it applies only to the singular body. Next, he defines 'nature' as the source of the primary essential attribute, broader than 'natural constitution'. He then classifies three types of division: 1) by tearing/cutting leading to actual separation, 2) by estimation/assumption without actual separation, and 3) by difference of accidents (permanent or relational). He explains that the third type is considered a separate kind when based on mere estimation; otherwise it falls under estimative division. He supports this with the definition of the indivisible part, which excludes division by accidents. He argues that accidental differentiation does not cause actual external separation, as seen when a body partially heats or touches another — it does not become two bodies. He then discusses the case of whiteness and blackness, showing that their different locations are distinguished by intellectual assumption, not actual division. Finally, he reconciles statements from al-Shifā' and its commentary: separation by accident difference is external in a sense but not actual physical separation; mental acts are the basis for calling it actual.

ماهية
طبع
هيولي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If bodies differ in essence, the proof for hyle collapses.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the proof concerns the singular body, where no essential difference can be assumed, so the objection is far‑fetched.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi consider the objection that bodies differ in essence to be far‑fetched?
A) Because the proof for hyle does not rely on bodies' essences.
B) Because the discussion concerns the singular body, where difference is not conceivable.
C) Because the objector failed to provide evidence.
D) Because all bodies are known to be equal in essence.

Key Takeaway
The objection to the proof for hyle based on essential difference is dismissed as a remote possibility because the discussion focuses on the singular body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author dismisses the objection as a 'remote possibility.' Are there objections to your own faith that you dismiss without serious thought because they seem remote? What would it cost you to honestly examine one of them today?
?
The lesson insists on focusing on the 'singular body'—the core of the argument. What is the 'singular body' of your faith that you keep avoiding, the simple truth that demands a response you are not yet ready to give?
?
The proof for hyle stands even if other bodies differ. Is your faith built on proofs that are independent of others' opinions, or does it rely on the surrounding consensus? What would remain of your belief if that consensus disappeared tomorrow?

Definition of Nature and Classification of Division Types
The author defines 'nature' and enumerates three types of division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's definition of 'nature' (طبع) and his classification of three kinds of division.

Core Idea
Division is categorized by how it affects the body: tearing, estimation, or accident difference.

Sanusi now defines the term 'nature' broadly and then classifies division into three types based on whether it involves actual separation, mental estimation, or difference of accidents. This classification is crucial for understanding how bodies can be considered divisible in different senses.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of 'nature'
Note that 'nature' (طبع) is defined as the source of the primary essential attribute, broader than 'natural constitution' (طبيعة).
2
Learn the three types of division
Identify: 1) by tearing/cutting (actual separation), 2) by estimation/assumption (mental), 3) by difference of accidents (permanent or relational).
3
See the rule for classification
Observe the conditional: if division leads to separation, it's the first; if in mere estimation, the second; otherwise, the third.

?

Which of your mental categories are based on real differences, and which might be mere estimations that create false separations? Will you reexamine one such division today?
?

?

Division by Accidents is Intellectual, Not Actual
The author explains why division by accidents is mental, with examples and reconciliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why division by difference of accidents does not imply actual physical separation and how it is distinct from estimative division.

Core Idea
Accidental differentiation does not split the body; it is only a mental assumption.

Sanusi continues by clarifying that the third type of division, based on difference of accidents, is not a real physical division. He argues that a body that partially heats or touches another does not become two bodies; the division is only in the mind. He then addresses an example of whiteness and blackness and reconciles statements from authoritative works.

Guided Path
1
Understand the third type as a separate kind
Notice that when based on mere estimation, it becomes a distinct third kind; otherwise it falls under estimative division.
2
See the example of partial heating
A body that heats partially does not become two bodies; actual separation does not occur.
3
Consider the case of whiteness and blackness
Their locations are distinguished by intellectual assumption, not actual division.
4
Reconcile statements from al-Shifā' and its commentary
Learn that 'external' separation in one source means due to an external reason, and 'actual' separation in another means mental act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما اعتراضه بمنع تساوي الأجسام في الماهية ، فلا يندفع بأن مبنى الكلام على اعترافهم يكون تلك البسائط متساوية في الطبع ، لأن مراده على ما صرح به في المباحث المشرقية هو أنه لو ادعى مدع أنها متخالفة بالماهية ، وأنه لا يوجد جزآن متحدان في الماهية لم يثبت أن كل جسم قابل للقسمة والانفكاكية، فلم يتم دليل على إثبات الهيولي. لكن لا خفاء في أنه احتمال بعيد لأن الكلام في الجسم المفرد الذي لا يعقل فيه اختلاف طبيعة وعلى هذا ينبغي أن يحمل قول من قال إن القسمة بأنواعها تحدث في المقسوم أثنينية تساوي طباع كل واحد طباع المجموع على القسمة الواردة على الجسم المفرد، وإلا ففساده واضح، وفسر الطباع بمصدر الصفة الذاتية الأولية للشيء حركة أو سكوناً ، كان أو غيرهما، فيكون أعم من الطبيعة، وفسر أنواع القسمة بما يكون بحسب الفتك والقطع ، أو بحسب الوهم والفرض، أو بحسب اختلاف عرضين ،قارين، أي ما هو للموضوع في نفسه كالسواد والبياض، أو غير قارين أي ما هو له بالقياس إلى الغير كالتماس والتحاذي، وذلك لأن الانقسام إن تأدى إلى الافتراق فالأول، وإلا فإن كان في مجرد الوهم ،فالثاني وإلا فالثالث وبما ذكرنا من اعتبار مجرد الوهم صار هذا قسماً ثالثاً، وإلا فهو من قبيل الانقسام الوهمي والفرضي بدليل قولهم : إن الجزء ما لا ينقسم لا كسراً ولا قطعاً، ولا وهماً ولا فرضاً، من غير تعرض لما يكون باختلاف عرضين، وذلك للقطع بأن الجسم الذي يتسخن بعضه، أو وقع الضوء على بعضه، أو لاقى ببعضه جسماً آخر، لم يحصل فيه الانفصال بالفعل، وبحسب الخارج ولم يصر جسمين ، ثم إذا زال التسخن أو الضوء أو الملاقاة عاد جسماً واحداً . ولو كان كذلك لكانت المسافة تصير أقساماً غير متناهية في الخارج بحسب موافاة المتحرك حدوداً، ثم تعود متصلة في نفسها واحدة في ذاتها عند انقطاع الحركة، وما يقال إنا قاطعون بأن محل البياض من الجسم غير محل السواد منه مسلم ، لكن باعتبار اختلاف العرضين لا بالنظر إلى ذات الجسم بحيث يعرض له انفصال وتميز في الخارج، بل بالفرض العقلي، ولهذا قال في الشفاء، ومن الذي بالفرض اختصاص العرض ببعض دون بعض، حتى إذا زال ذلك العرض زال ذلك الاختصاص، مثل جسم تبيض لا كله فبفرض له بالبياض جزء، إذا زال ذلك البياض زال افتراضه، فما ذكر في شرح الإشارات من أن الانفصال بحسب اختلاف العرضين انفصال في الخارج من غير تأد إلى الافتراق يحمل على أنه لأمر في الخارج، وما ذكر في منطق الشفاء من أنه انفصال بالفعل يحمل على فعل الأذهان دون الأعيان
As for his objection by denying the equality of bodies in essence, it is not refuted by [saying] that the basis of the discourse is on their acknowledgment that those simple [bodies] are equal in nature, because his intent — as he stated in al-Mabaḥith al-Mashriqiyya — is that if someone claimed that they are different in essence and that no two parts exist that are united in essence, it would not be established that every body is divisible and separable, so the proof for hyle would not be complete. But it is not hidden that this is a remote possibility, because the discussion is about the singular body in which difference of nature is not conceivable. On this basis, one should interpret the saying of whoever said that division in its kinds produces in the divided [object] a duality equalizing the nature of each one with the nature of the whole — [this refers] to division occurring on the singular body; otherwise its corruption is clear. And he interpreted 'nature' as the source of the primary essential attribute of a thing, whether movement or rest or something else, so it is more general than 'natural constitution'. And he interpreted the kinds of division as [that which is] according to tearing and cutting, or according to estimation and assumption, or according to difference of two accidents: permanent, i.e., what belongs to the subject in itself like blackness and whiteness; or non-permanent, i.e., what belongs to it in relation to another like contact and adjacency. That is because division: if it leads to separation, it is the first; otherwise, if it is in mere estimation, it is the second; otherwise, it is the third. And by our mentioning consideration of mere estimation, this became a third kind; otherwise it belongs to the category of estimative and hypothetical division, as evidenced by their saying: 'The indivisible part is that which is not divisible by breaking or cutting, nor by estimation or assumption,' without mentioning what is by difference of accidents. That is because of the certainty that a body some of which becomes hot, or light falls on some of it, or it touches another body with some of it, does not undergo actual separation externally and does not become two bodies; then when the heat, light, or contact ceases, it becomes one body. And if it were otherwise, distance would become infinite divisions externally according to the moving body's meeting boundaries, then become continuous in itself as one in its essence when motion ceases. And what is said — 'We are certain that the location of whiteness in the body is not the location of blackness' — is granted, but in consideration of the difference of accidents, not with regard to the essence of the body such that separation and distinction occur externally; rather, by intellectual assumption. For this reason, it is said in al-Shifā': 'And who [holds] that by assumption the accident is specified to some [parts] without others, such that when that accident ceases, that specification ceases, like a body that whitens not entirely, so by assumption it has a part for whiteness; when that whiteness ceases, its assumption ceases.' So what is mentioned in the Commentary on al-Ishārāt — that separation according to difference of accidents is an external separation without leading to detachment — is interpreted as [being] due to something external. And what is mentioned in the Logic of al-Shifā' — that it is an actual separation — is interpreted as [being] an act of minds, not external things.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage begins by responding to an objection against the proof for hyle. The objection denies that all bodies are equal in essence, which would undermine the argument that bodies are composed of matter and form. The author explains that this objection is far-fetched because the discussion concerns the singular body, where no difference in nature is conceivable. He then clarifies a statement about division producing duality, insisting it applies only to the singular body. Next, he defines 'nature' as the source of the primary essential attribute, broader than 'natural constitution'. He then classifies three types of division: 1) by tearing/cutting leading to actual separation, 2) by estimation/assumption without actual separation, and 3) by difference of accidents (permanent or relational). He explains that the third type is considered a separate kind when based on mere estimation; otherwise it falls under estimative division. He supports this with the definition of the indivisible part, which excludes division by accidents. He argues that accidental differentiation does not cause actual external separation, as seen when a body partially heats or touches another — it does not become two bodies. He then discusses the case of whiteness and blackness, showing that their different locations are distinguished by intellectual assumption, not actual division. Finally, he reconciles statements from al-Shifā' and its commentary: separation by accident difference is external in a sense but not actual physical separation; mental acts are the basis for calling it actual.

Key Terms
وهم
فرض
عرض
انفصال
اتصال
هيولي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Division by difference of accidents implies actual physical separation.
Correction: Sanusi shows it is only by intellectual assumption; the body does not actually split.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what happens when a body is partially heated?
A) It becomes two bodies due to heat.
B) It undergoes actual separation externally.
C) It does not become two bodies; the division is only mental.
D) It undergoes separation by estimation.

Key Takeaway
Division by accidents is a mental concept, not actual physical separation; it is based on intellectual assumption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have a body that changes—you gain weight, lose a limb, get scars. According to this lesson, those accidents do not split you into multiple selves. But do you live as if your changing accidents define who you are? In what area of your life today must you reject that lie and cling to your unchanging identity before Allah?
?
The passage shows that when heat or contact passes, the body returns to being one. Think of a time when a temporary emotion (anger, fear, joy) made you feel divided from yourself or others. After it passed, what did you realize was still united? How can that truth help you trust Allah's oneness when your feelings fluctuate?
?
If division by accidents is only in the mind, then the lines you draw between yourself and 'others' (race, status, personality) are ultimately mental assumptions. How does this lesson call you to break down one such assumption today—and what real act of unity will you perform as a result?

The Peripatetic Proof for Hyle
The Peripatetics prove hyle as the substrate for connection and separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Peripatetics argue for the existence of hyle as the persistent substrate underlying both connection and separation in bodies.

Core Idea
Hyle is the unchanging substrate that remains when bodies connect or separate.

The Peripatetics, heirs to Aristotle, developed a subtle argument to prove that all material bodies require a primordial matter, or hyle, that persists through change. Their reasoning begins by examining what it means for a body to be connectable or separable. This lesson walks through that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage silently, then follow the literal translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
Pay attention to the key terms: هيولي, صورة, امتداد.
2
Understand the problem: connection not by assembly
The argument starts by rejecting the idea that a body's connection comes from assembling parts. Instead, the body is inherently connectable.
This sets up the need for a substrate that is not form.
3
Distinguish substantial form from accidental extensions
The body has a substantial extension (form) that underlies accidental changes, like wax taking different shapes. This form is not what receives separation.
Form changes with separation, so it cannot be the persistent substrate.
4
Identify the need for hyle as substrate
Since form does not remain with separation, there must be a distinct recipient of both connection and separation that persists—that is hyle.
The proof uses the necessary distinction between vanishing entirely and simply dividing.
5
Reflect on the verification from perception
The first thing we perceive in a body is an extensional identity that is not negated by quantity changes. This identity does not survive separation, yet something must persist—that something is hyle.
This clinches the argument for hyle's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ثم احتج المشاؤون) منهم على ثبوت الهيولي بأنه لما لم يكن اتصال الجسم باجتماع الأجزاء، وانفصاله بافتراقها، بل كان في ذاته متصلاً للانفصال، فله امتداد جوهري يتبدل عليه الامتدادات العرضية كما في الشمعة وهو المسمى بالصورة، ويمتنع يكون هو القابل للانفصال، لأنه لا يبقى معه، بل لا بد معه من قابل للاتصال والانفصال يبقى معها ويتبدل عليه الهويات الاتصالية المختلفة بالشخص وهو المسمى بالهيولي، وتحقيقه أن أول ما يدرك من جوهرية الجسم هوية امتدادية، لا تنتفى بتبدل المقادير، ولا تعقل الجسم دونها، يسمونه اتصالاً بل متصلاً بمعنى الجوهر الذي من شأنه الاتصال بمعنى كونه بحيث يفرض فيه الأبعاد، ولا خفاء في أنها بعينها لا تبقى مع الانفصال بل تزول إلى هويتين اتصاليتين مع بقاء أمر في الحالين هو القابل بالذات للاتصال والانفصال للفرق الضروري، بين أن ينعدم بالكلية، ويحدث جسمان، أو بالعكس، وبين أن ينفصل إلى جسمين
He said: 'Then the Peripatetics argued for the establishment of hyle [thus]: Since the connection of the body is not by the assembly of parts, nor its separation by their disjunction, but rather it is in itself connectable for separation, it has a substantial extension upon which accidental extensions change — as in the candle — and that is called form. And it is impossible that it [the substantial extension] be the recipient of separation, because it does not remain with it. Rather, there must be with it a recipient of connection and separation that remains with them, and upon which the different individual connective identities change, and that is called hyle. Its verification is that the first thing perceived of the substantially of a body is an extensional identity that is not negated by change of quantities, and the body is not conceived without it. They call it 'connection' or rather 'connectable', meaning the substance whose property is connection in the sense of being such that dimensions are assumed in it. And it is not hidden that these very [identities] do not remain with separation but pass away into two connective identities, while something remains in both states that is the recipient by essence of connection and separation — due to the necessary distinction between [the case where] it disappears entirely and two bodies come to be (or the reverse) and [the case where] it separates into two bodies.'
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents the Peripatetic proof for the existence of hyle (matter). The argument begins by noting that the body's connection and separation are not due to the assembly or disassembly of pre-existing parts; rather, the body itself is inherently connectable and separable. It possesses a substantial extension — called form — that underlies accidental extensions (like the wax in a candle that takes different shapes). This substantial extension cannot itself be the recipient of separation, because it does not remain when separation occurs. Therefore, there must be a distinct substrate that persists through both connection and separation, receiving different individual connective identities; this is hyle. The verification elaborates: the first thing perceived in a body's substantially is an extensional identity that remains despite changes in quantity and is indispensable for conceiving the body. They call this 'connection' or 'connectable,' meaning the substance that can have dimensions assumed in it. Clearly, this extensional identity does not survive separation but is replaced by two such identities; yet something must persist across both states to be the recipient of connection and separation. This necessary distinction explains why we do not say the original body vanishes entirely when it divides; rather, it separates into two bodies, implying a persistent substrate.

Key Terms
هيولي
صورة
امتداد جوهري
امتداد عرضي
اتصال
انفصال
هوية امتدادية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The substantial extension (form) itself persists through separation.
Correction: The proof shows that form does not survive separation; it is replaced by new forms. Hyle is the underlying substrate that remains.

Checkpoint
According to the Peripatetic proof, what is the primary reason that hyle must exist?
A) Because bodies are made of parts that assemble and disassemble
B) Because form (substantial extension) does not remain with separation
C) Because accidental extensions are more fundamental than substantial extension
D) Because the body's connection is due to the assembly of parts

Key Takeaway
Hyle is the necessary substrate that persists through connection and separation, distinct from form which changes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You learned that beneath the changing forms and accidents of your body lies a substrate (hyle) that remains through connection and separation. But what about your soul? Are you living as if your true self is only the ever-changing surface, or are you anchoring yourself in the deeper reality that persists even beyond death? How will you remind yourself daily that what is permanent is not what you see?
?
The Peripatetics used reason to prove an invisible substrate from the observable behavior of bodies. When was the last time you reasoned from the visible world to the unseen truths of your faith (like the Hereafter)? This lesson models a method of inference—can you apply it to strengthen your conviction in what your eyes cannot see? What step will you take today to train your mind to see beyond appearances?
?
The passage says that when a body separates, something must remain—otherwise it would vanish entirely. Reflect on a time you felt your world 'separate' (lost a relationship, a role, a health). What remained of you? Did you discover that your essence is not tied to those accidents? If not, why? What would it mean to live as if the only thing that cannot be taken from you is your relationship with Allah—the One who alone is permanent? Are you willing to let go of what is transient and cling to what endures?

Separation and Distinction as Mental Assumptions
Taftāzānī defines separation and distinction as mental assumptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the separation and distinction of parts in a body are not actual external realities but are constructed by mental assumption.

Core Idea
Separation of body parts is a mental construct, not external fact.

In kalam, we often speak of parts of a body as if they are separate, but is that separation real? Taftāzānī clarifies that separation (infiṣāl) and distinction (tamayyuz) are not actual in the external world; they are products of mental assumption. This lesson unpacks that claim through an example and references to Ibn Sīnā.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without stopping. Then read the translation. Notice the references to al-Shifā' and the commentary on al-Ishārāt.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the central claim
Find the sentence that states separation and distinction are 'by mental assumption' (bil-farḍ al-ʿaqlī). This is the core teaching.
3
Understand the example
The example of a body that is partially white shows how the whiteness is assumed to belong to a part. When whiteness ceases, the assumed part vanishes. This illustrates that the part only exists by assumption.
The whiteness is an accident (ʿaraḍ) that gives rise to the assumed part.
4
Resolve the apparent contradictions
Taftāzānī addresses two other texts that seem to say separation is external or actual. He explains that 'external separation' refers to the accident's existence, not physical separation, and 'actual separation' is an act of the mind, not of external entities.
This harmonizes conflicting statements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انفصال وتميز في الخارج، بل بالفرض العقلي، ولهذا قال في الشفاء، ومن الذي بالفرض اختصاص العرض ببعض دون بعض، حتى إذا زال ذلك العرض زال ذلك الاختصاص، مثل جسم تبيض لا كله فبفرض له بالبياض جزء، إذا زال ذلك البياض زال افتراضه، فما ذكر في شرح (1) الإشارات من أن الانفصال أن الانفصال بحسب اختلاف العرضين انفصال في الخارج من غير تأد إلى الافتراق يحمل على أنه لأمر في الخارج، وما ذكر في منطق الشفاء من أنه انفصال بالفعل يحمل على فعل الأذهان دون الأعيان
Separation and distinction are in the external, but rather by mental assumption. And for this reason he said in al-Shifa': And it is that which, by assumption, the accident is specific to some without some, such that when that accident ceases, that specificity ceases, like a body that whitens not all of it, so by assumption it has a part of whiteness; when that whiteness ceases, its assumption ceases. So what is mentioned in the commentary on al-Isharat, that separation based on difference of two accidents is a separation in the external without leading to disjunction, is interpreted as being due to something in the external; and what is mentioned in the Logic of al-Shifa', that it is separation in act, is interpreted as being the action of minds, not of entities.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage defines the concepts of separation (infisal) and distinction (tamayyuz) in the context of body. The author states that these are not actual in external reality but occur by mental assumption (al-fardh al-aqli). He supports this by referencing Ibn Sina's al-Shifa' and the commentary on al-Isharat. The example given: a body that is partially white – the whiteness is assumed to be in a part, and when the whiteness ceases, the assumed part ceases. Thus, separation between the white and non-white parts is only by mental assumption. The commentary on al-Isharat mentions that separation according to difference of two accidents is an external separation that does not lead to actual disjunction, but this is interpreted as being due to something external (i.e., the accident's existence), not actual physical separation. Meanwhile, the Logic of al-Shifa' mentions that separation is 'in act' (bil-fi'l), but this is interpreted as an act of the mind (fa'l al-adhhan), not of external entities (duna al-a'yan).

Key Terms
انفصال (infisal)
تميز (tamayyuz)
فرض عقلي (mental assumption)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that when we speak of parts of a body (e.g., the white part), those parts are actually separate in the external world, like pieces of a puzzle.
Correction: The passage clarifies that such parts are not externally separate; they are only distinguished by mental assumption based on accidents like whiteness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, is the separation of parts in a partially white body an actual external separation?
A) Yes, it is actual in the external
B) No, it is by mental assumption
C) It is actual only when the whiteness ceases
D) It is actual only in the case of complete disjunction

Key Takeaway
Separation and distinction of parts in a body are not real external divisions; they are constructs of mental assumption based on accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you mistakenly assumed a permanent division between yourself and someone else based on a temporary trait (like a mistake or a difference in opinion), only to realize it was a mental construct? How does this lesson call you to tear down that assumption today?
?
The whiteness on a part of the body disappears, and the assumed part vanishes with it. What 'whiteness'—what temporary attribute—are you using to define yourself or others, and what would be left if that attribute ceased? Will you let go of that mental construct?
?
Taftāzānī tells us that separation is an act of the mind, not of entities. In your journey of faith, where are you mistaking your mental constructs about Islam (e.g., cultural habits, narrow interpretations) for the true reality of the religion? What one step can you take today to align your practice with the external truth of revelation, not your assumptions?

Five Objections to the Existence of Matter and Their Refutation
Author enumerates five objections to the proof of matter and declares them repelled.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five standard objections raised against the proof of matter in kalam cosmology and how the proof of a permanent substrate (al-qabil al-baqi) refutes them.

Core Idea
Five objections to matter are repelled by the proof of a permanent receiver.

The preceding argument established the necessity of a permanent substrate underlying the alternation of connection and separation in bodies. Here, the author counters five objections that might be raised against that conclusion. Each objection attempts to show that the concept of matter is unnecessary or leads to absurdity. Understanding these objections deepens your grasp of the kalam argument for the existence of a material substratum.

Guided Path
1
Read the five objections
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Let the author's enumeration come through.
Notice how each objection is a concise philosophical argument.
2
Understand the refutation context
The passage states 'on this basis they are repelled.' Recall the earlier proof for a permanent receiver that provides the basis.
The objections assume that connection is an accident, not a substance.
3
Analyze each objection
Go through each of the five objections. Identify the core claim and why it challenges the existence of matter.
Use the commentary for clarification.
4
Connect to the permanent substrate
After each objection, ask: How does the proof of a permanent receiver (qabil baqi) address this?
The objections are presumed refuted; try to infer the rebuttal.
5
Take the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to test your recognition of the objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا يندفع إشكالات : الأول: أن كون الاتصال جوهراً وجزءاً من الجسم ضروري البطلان، بل الاتصال والانفصال عرضيان يتعاقبان على الجسم، وبالتحقيق عبارتان عن وحدته وكثرته . الثاني : أن لا معنى للانفصال إلا انعدام هوية اتصالية إلى هويتين، فلا حاجة إلى قابل باق . الثالث : لو افتقر الانفصال إلى مادة لتسلسلت المواد . الرابع : إن الزائل عند الانفصال إن كان هو الاتصال الجوهري الذاتي فقد انعدم الجسم، فلم يكن قابلاً، أو العرضي فلم يفد المطلوب . الخامس : أن الجسم إذا انفصل إلى جسمين، فإن كانت مادة هذا مادة ذاك كان الواحد بالتشخص موجوداً في حيزين موصوفاً بجسميتين، وإن كانت غير يا (1) فعند الاتصال إن كانتا موجودتين لم يكن الجسم متصلاً بالذات، بل من أجزاء بالفعل، وإلا كان الانفصال إعداماً للجسم بالكلية لا بمجرد الصورة الاتصالية ]
And on this basis are repelled the objections: First: that connection as a substance and part of body is necessarily false; rather, connection and separation are accidents that alternate upon the body, and in truth are two expressions for its unity and multiplicity. Second: that there is no meaning to separation except the annihilation of one connectional identity into two identities; so there is no need for a permanent receiver. Third: if separation were in need of a matter, matters would be sequential (tasalsul). Fourth: that what ceases at separation, if it is the essential substantial connection, then the body has ceased to exist, so it was not a receiver; or if it is the accidental connection, then it did not achieve the desired. Fifth: that when the body is separated into two bodies, if the matter of this is the matter of that, then an individual thing would exist in two places described with two corporealities; and if it is different (1), then during connection, if they both existed, the body would not be essentially connected, but rather composed of actual parts; otherwise, the separation would be a complete annihilation of the body, not merely of the connectional form. ]
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage lists five objections (ishkalat) that are repelled by the preceding proof for matter. The author states 'on this basis they are repelled' (wa 'ala hadha yandafi'). The objections are: First: that connection as a substance and part of body is false; rather, connection and separation are accidents that alternate on the body and are just expressions of unity and multiplicity. Second: separation is simply the cessation of one connectional identity into two, so there is no need for a permanent substrate (qabil baqi). Third: if separation required a matter, there would be an infinite regress of matters (tasalsul). Fourth: what ceases at separation: if it is the essential substantial connection, then the body itself ceases, so it was not a receiver; if it is accidental connection, then the desired conclusion (a permanent substrate) is not obtained. Fifth: when a body separates into two bodies, if the matter of both is the same, then one individual would be in two places with two corporealities; if the matters are different, then before separation either both were present (so the body was not one continuous body but composed of actual parts) or not, in which case separation would be annihilation of the body entirely, not just the connectional form. These objections are presumed refuted by the earlier argument for a permanent substrate.

Key Terms
إشكالات (ishkalat)
اتصال جوهرى (essential connection)
اتصال عرضى (accidental connection)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
قابل باق (permanent receiver)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The existence of matter is unnecessary because bodies can be explained solely by accidents of connection and separation.
Correction: The alternation of connection and separation itself requires a permanent receiver (matter) that persists through these changes, as proven earlier.

Checkpoint
Which objection claims that if separation required a matter, there would be an infinite regress?
A) First
B) Second
C) Third
D) Fifth

Key Takeaway
All five objections against a material substrate are repelled by the proof that a permanent receiver is necessary for the alternation of connection and separation in bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear objections to your beliefs that sound convincing at first, do you have a deep foundational truth that repels them, or do you feel shaken? What is your 'permanent receiver' that anchors you?
?
The objections here misunderstand the relationship between connection and separation. In your own spiritual life, are you confusing superficial changes with your core identity? Are you attached to transient 'accidents' rather than your permanent substance?
?
The refutation shows that a single principle can dissolve multiple objections. What is one foundational truth in your life that, if you truly held it, would dissolve many of your doubts or struggles? Will you commit to strengthening that foundation today?

The Essential Extension of Body: A Conclusion from Refuting Atomism
Author concludes that body is essentially continuous with an essential extension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rejection of atomism leads to affirming an essential continuous extension in body.

Core Idea
Body is essentially continuous; extension is its inseparable reality.

After demonstrating the impossibility of atomism, the author draws the positive conclusion: body is not a composite of indivisible parts but is essentially continuous, possessing an inherent extension that underlies all quantitative change. This passage establishes the 'mathematical body' as a real substance.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
This passage concludes the argument against atomism. Recall that atomism holds body is made of indivisible parts, which has been refuted.
The author uses 'لما بطل كون الجسم متألفاً من أجزاء لا تتجزأ' to signal the refutation.
2
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the logical flow: refutation leads to affirmation of essential continuity.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the conclusion may be completed in the following lines.
3
Identify the main claim
Find the statement: 'ثبت أنه متصل في نفسه ... فله جوهري، تتبدل عليه المقادير المختلفة' – this affirms an essential continuous substance that underlies varying magnitudes.
This 'johari' is the essential extension.
4
Consider the example of the candle
The candle can be shaped round, cubic, or thin – showing the same substance takes different forms, implying a continuous subject that is extended.
This illustrates that body is not a mere aggregate but a continuous thing.
5
Reflect on the incomplete ending
The passage cuts off mid-sentence. Based on context, the author likely continues to contrast this essential extension with accidental extensions.
The subsequent discussion likely clarifies the distinction between essential and accidental extension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما بطل كون الجسم متألفاً من أجزاء لا تتجزأ أصلاً، أو تتجزأ وهماً لا فعلاً، متناهية أو غير متناهية، يكون اتصاله باجتماعها وانفصاله بافتراقها، ثبت أنه متصل في نفسه كما هو عند الحس، قابل للانفصال نظراً إلى ذاته على ما مر، فله جوهري، تتبدل عليه المقادير المختلفة. أعني الجسم التعليمي الذي هو من قبيل الكميات، كالشمعة التي تجعل تارة مدوراً وتارة مكعباً، وتارة صفحة رقيقة إلى غير ذلك وزعموا أن حقيقة الجسم لا تعقل بدون تعقله، بل تدرك في بادىء النظر من الجسم غيره، أعني الجوهر الذي له الامتدادات العرضية الآخذة امتداد
When it is false that the body is composed of parts that are indivisible at all, or divisible in imagination not in act, finite or infinite, such that its connection is by their joining and its separation by their parting, it is established that it is connected in itself as it is perceived, capable of separation with respect to its essence as previously, so it has a substantial thing (johari) upon which various magnitudes change. I mean the mathematical body (al-jism al-ta'limi), which is of the category of quantities (kamiyyat), like a candle that is made sometimes round, sometimes cubic, sometimes a thin sheet, etc. And they claimed that the reality of body is not intelligible without its being intelligible, rather it is perceived at first glance from the body other than it, I mean the substance that has the accidental extensions that take extension...
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage concludes from the refutation of atomism. Since it has been proven false that body is composed of indivisible parts (whether actually indivisible, or only mentally divisible, finite or infinite), such that its connection is by joining of parts and separation by their parting, it is established that body is connected in itself (muttasil fi nafsihi) as perceived by the senses, and capable of separation in its essence. Therefore, it has a substantial thing (johari) upon which different magnitudes change. This is the 'mathematical body' (al-jism al-ta'limi), which belongs to the category of quantities. An example is a candle that can be shaped round, cubic, or thin. The author notes that the reality of body is not intelligible without this extension, and at first glance one perceives from the body something other than the body itself, i.e., the substance that has the accidental extensions that take extension (the last word is incomplete).

Key Terms
جسم تعليمي (mathematical body)
كميات (quantities)
امتداد جوهري (essential extension)
أجزاء لا تتجزأ (indivisible parts)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Body is composed of indivisible parts (atoms) that are truly separate and only joined accidentally.
Correction: The passage refutes atomism and establishes that body is essentially continuous, not a composite of discrete parts. Its connection is in itself, not from assembling parts.

Checkpoint

A) It is composed of indivisible parts
B) It is essentially continuous and has an essential extension
C) It is only an accident
D) It has no extension at all

Key Takeaway
The rejection of atomism reveals that body is essentially continuous, possessing an inherent extension called the mathematical body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author says the reality of body is not intelligible without extension. What constant 'extension' or substance do you rely on beneath the changing forms of your daily life? Is it your soul, your identity, or something else?
?
The candle takes different shapes but remains the same substance. In your life, what are the shapes that change, and what is the substance that remains? Are you too attached to the shapes, neglecting the essence?

Refuting Five Objections on Continuity and Discontinuity
Analogy and refutation of five objections on continuity and discontinuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the analogy of water as pure potentiality to refute five objections about continuity and discontinuity, and why continuity and discontinuity are accidental rather than substantial.

Core Idea
The same substance can be continuous or discontinuous because it is pure potentiality, not fixed essence.

The author presents an analogy of water in a jar and pitchers to illustrate that a substance can receive opposites without changing its underlying nature. This analogy then serves as the foundation for refuting five objections that arise from misunderstanding the accidental nature of continuity and discontinuity. In this passage, you will see the objections and their concise answers.

Guided Path
1
Understand the analogy
Read the analogy of water in a jar and pitchers. Notice that water is pure potentiality (استعداد محض) and becomes one or many depending on the form. This shows that the same substance can receive both continuity and discontinuity.
The analogy is key to understanding the refutations.
2
Identify the five objections
The passage lists five objections (الأول, الثاني, etc.) against the idea that the same substance can be both continuous and discontinuous. As you read each one, note the core claim.
Each objection is numbered from first to fifth.
3
Examine the first two objections
The first objection claims continuity is a substance and part of the body. The author says continuity is accidental. The second says discontinuity needs no substrate, but the author says it is just loss of one identity into two.
These two are about the nature of continuity and discontinuity.
4
Examine the remaining objections
The third objection involves infinite regress, the fourth distinguishes essential vs. accidental continuity, and the fifth deals with division into two bodies. See how the author's principle resolves each.
All objections are refuted by the idea that the substance is pure potentiality.
5
Synthesize the core principle
After studying all objections, restate the core principle: continuity and discontinuity are accidents (عرضان) that alternate upon the body, which is pure potentiality. This is the main takeaway.
Link back to the water analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالعكس، كماء الجرة يجعل في الكيزان وعكسه ولا يمتنع توارد المتقابلين عليه لكونه في نفسه استعداداً محضاً يصير واحداً بوحدة الصورة، ومتعدداً بتعددها، مع بقائها في الحالين، وعلى هذا يندفع إشكالات :
الأول: أن كون الاتصال جوهراً وجزءاً من الجسم ضروري البطلان، بل الاتصال والانفصال عرضيان يتعاقبان على الجسم، وبالتحقيق عبارتان عن وحدته وكثرته .
الثاني : أن لا معنى للانفصال إلا انعدام هوية اتصالية إلى هويتين، فلا حاجة إلى قابل باق .
الثالث : لو افتقر الانفصال إلى مادة لتسلسلت المواد .
الرابع : إن الزائل عند الانفصال إن كان هو الاتصال الجوهري الذاتي فقد انعدم الجسم، فلم يكن قابلاً، أو العرضي فلم يفد المطلوب .
الخامس : أن الجسم إذا انفصل إلى جسمين، فإن كانت مادة هذا مادة ذاك كان الواحد بالتشخص موجوداً في حيزين موصوفاً بجسميتين، وإن كانت غير يا (١) فعند الاتصال إن كانتا موجودتين لم يكن الجسم متصلاً بالذات، بل من أجزاء بالفعل، وإلا كان الانفصال إعداماً للجسم بالكلية لا بمجرد الصورة الاتصالية ] .
On the contrary, like the water of a jar placed into pitchers and its opposite, and it is not impossible for opposites to occur upon it because it is in itself a pure potentiality, becoming one by the unity of form, and multiple by its multiplicity, while remaining in both states. And upon this, objections are repelled:
First: That continuity is a substance and a part of the body is necessarily false; rather, continuity and discontinuity are accidents that alternate upon the body, and in reality they are expressions of its unity and multiplicity.
Second: That there is no meaning of discontinuity except the annihilation of one continuous identity into two identities, so there is no need for a remaining substrate.
Third: If discontinuity required a material, then materials would regress infinitely.
Fourth: That what ceases at discontinuity, if it is the essential substantial continuity, then the body has ceased to exist, so it would not be a substrate; or if it is accidental continuity, then it does not achieve the desired.
Fifth: That when a body is divided into two bodies, if the matter of this is the matter of that, then the one in person would exist in two loci described by two corporealities; and if it is different (ghayrī), then at the time of connection, if both exist, the body would not be connected in essence but rather from actual parts; otherwise, the discontinuity would be an annihilation of the body entirely, not merely of the connective form.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage refutes five objections against the idea that the same substance can be both continuous and discontinuous. The author begins with an analogy: water is the same substance whether in a jar or in pitchers, and opposites can occur to it because it is pure potentiality (استعداد محض) that becomes one by the unity of form and many by the multiplicity of form. Then he lists five objections and shows how they are repelled. The first objection claims continuity is a substance; the author says continuity is accidental. The second says discontinuity needs no substrate; the author says it is just a loss of one identity into two. The third is an infinite regress argument. The fourth distinguishes essential vs accidental continuity. The fifth deals with division into two bodies and whether the matter is the same or different.

Key Terms
اتصال
انفصال
جوهر
صورة
استعداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Continuity and discontinuity are intrinsic substances that the body inherently possesses.
Correction: The passage shows that continuity and discontinuity are accidental (عرضيان) and the body is pure potentiality that receives them externally.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can one substance be both continuous and discontinuous?
A) Because it is composed of parts.
B) Because it is pure potentiality that becomes one by the unity of form and many by its multiplicity.
C) Because continuity and discontinuity are separate substances.
D) Because the substance is destroyed in the process.

Key Takeaway
Continuity and discontinuity are accidental attributes, not essential substances; the same substance can receive both because it is pure potentiality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If continuity and discontinuity are merely accidents, not essential to your own existence, what does that imply about your attachment to a particular state of being? Will you cling to a 'continuous' identity even when Allah brings change?

The water remains the same whether in a jar or divided into pitchers. What is the 'water' in your life that remains constant through all your changes? Are you living in accordance with that constant, or are you defined by your forms?
?
The passage refutes the idea that the substance is annihilated in division. How does this challenge your fear of losing yourself—whether through aging, hardship, or spiritual transformation? Are you willing to let go of your current form to allow a new one to arise from the same substance?

Refuting Atomism and Defining the Continuous Body
The author refutes atomism and defines the body as continuous, with a substance receiving different measures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the body is considered continuous in itself and how it relates to the concept of the educational body (jism ta'limi) as a substance receiving different measures.

Core Idea
The body is not made of atoms; it is continuous and has a substance that takes different shapes.

This passage tackles a foundational question in Islamic theology: what is the nature of the physical body? The author first refutes the atomistic view that bodies are composed of indivisible parts. He then establishes that the body is continuous in itself, as we perceive it, and introduces the idea of a substance (jawhar) that can receive different magnitudes—what he calls the 'educational body.' This sets the stage for understanding how physical objects can change shape while retaining their core substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the original Arabic passage
Start by reading the Arabic text slowly. Notice the key terms and the structure of the argument. Then read the English translation to get a clear sense of the meaning.
Pay attention to the word 'جوهري' (jawharī) and its role in the passage.
2
Understand the refutation of atomism
Identify the different positions being refuted: absolute indivisibility, indivisibility in estimation, finite or infinite parts. The passage invalidates all these, showing that the body is not composed of atoms.
3
Grasp the positive claim: continuity and substance
After the refutation, the author asserts that the body is continuous in itself, capable of separation in essence. It has a substance (jawhar) that can take different measures. This substance is the 'educational body' (jism ta'limi), a type of quantity.
4
Reflect on the candle example
The example of wax (candle) made into different shapes illustrates how the same substance can receive varying measures. Think about how this supports the idea of a continuous body that is not atomic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

When it is invalidated that the body is composed of parts indivisible absolutely, or divisible in estimation not actually, finite or infinite, and that its continuity is by their aggregation and its discontinuity by their separation, it is established that it is continuous in itself as it is according to sense, capable of discontinuity with respect to its essence as previously mentioned, so it has a substance (jawharī) upon which different measures alternate. I mean the educational body (al-jism al-taʻlīmī) which is of the type of quantities (kammiyyāt), like the candle which is made sometimes round, sometimes cubic, and sometimes a thin sheet, etc.

The example of wax shows how the same substance can take different shapes.
