Plato vs Aristotle on Body: Substance or Composite?
Contrasting Platonic and Aristotelian views on body essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contrasting views of Plato and Aristotle on the essence of body and how they define its relationship to substance, matter, and form.

Core Idea
Plato identifies body with substance; Aristotle distinguishes matter and form within it.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid presents two classical philosophical positions on what constitutes a body. Both schools agree that what we perceive first is a substance with extensions, but they differ fundamentally on whether that substance is the body itself or a part that underlies change. The text sets up a foundational distinction for later kalam discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument about body, substance, and form.
Notice the technical terms: جوهر, هيولي, صورة جسمية.
2
Identify the common premise
Both Plato and Aristotle start from the same observation: what is first perceived from a body is a substance with accidental extensions. This shared starting point is essential.
3
Spot the key difference
Plato identifies that substance with the body itself, making it capable of discontinuity. Aristotle says it is only a part (the form) inhering in another part (matter) that receives discontinuity.
This difference has major implications for how change and persistence are explained.
4
Understand the roles of hayula and bodily form
The passage explains that for Aristotle, the substrate (hayula) receives continuity and discontinuity and remains while forms change. The inhering substance is the bodily form. Grasp these two components.
The text has a garbled phrase, but the intended meaning is that matter must be capable of both continuity and discontinuity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعموا أن حقيقة الجسم لا تعقل بدون تعقله، بل تدرك في بادىء النظر من الجسم غيره، أعني الجوهر الذي له الامتدادات العرضية الآخذة في الجهات، فليس هو خارجاً عن حقيقة الجسم بل عند أفلاطون وأشياعه نفس الجسم، ويقبل الانفصال لذاته، وعند أرسطو وأتباعه جزء منه حال في جزء آخر هو القابل للانفصال، لأن القابل يجب اجتماعه مع المقبول، والاتصال الانفصال، فلا بد من جوهر قابل للاتصال، والانفصال يبقى يمتنع أن يبقى مع منهما، ويتبدل عليه الهويات الاتصالية المختلفة بالشخص، وهو المسمى الهيولي. والجوهر الحال بالصورة الجسمية.
And they claimed that the reality of body is not intellected without intellection of it, but rather in the first glance something else is perceived from the body, namely the substance which has the accidental extensions (imtidādāt) extending in directions. So it is not outside the reality of the body; rather, according to Plato and his followers, it is the body itself and it is capable of discontinuity essentially. And according to Aristotle and his followers, it is a part of it inhering in another part which is the recipient of discontinuity, because the recipient must be united with the received, and (the) continuity (and) discontinuity, so there must be a substance capable of continuity and discontinuity - it remains impossible that it remain with both of them - and the connective identities different in person alternate upon it, and it is called hyle (hayūlā). And the substance inhering in the bodily form.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage explains two views on the nature of body. Both agree that what is first perceived is a substance with accidental extensions, but Plato identifies this substance with the body itself, capable of separation essentially. Aristotle says it is a part (matter) that receives form; the matter (hayula) is the substrate that receives continuity and discontinuity and remains while forms change. The inhering substance is the bodily form.

أفلاطون
أرسطو
انفصال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Plato and Aristotle had the same view on the essence of body.
Correction: Plato considered the perceived substance as the body itself, while Aristotle considered it as a part (form) inhering in matter (hayula).

Checkpoint
How does Aristotle's view of the substance differ from Plato's according to the passage?
A) Aristotle says it is the body itself, Plato says it is matter.
B) Plato says it is the body itself, Aristotle says it is a part inhering in another part (matter).
C) Both agree it is the body itself.
D) Aristotle says it is form only.

Key Takeaway
In Islamic kalam, the essence of body is understood through the philosophical debate between Plato and Aristotle on whether it is pure substance or a composite of matter and form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that philosophers like Plato and Aristotle debated whether your body is a simple substance or a composite, what does that imply about the dignity of your body? Are you treating your body as something merely physical or as a bearer of form that gives it meaning?
?
If your body is a composite of matter and form, and the form is what makes you who you are, then what happens to your identity after death? Does your hope in the resurrection rest on the permanence of form or the recreation of matter? How does that change the way you live today?
?
The passage says that for Plato, body is essentially divisible. For Aristotle, it is united by form. Which view makes you more aware of your body as a gift from God? And what one action can you take this week to honor your body as a trust (amānah) rather than just a collection of parts?

First Perception of Body and Meanings of Continuity
The author analyzes the first perception of body and defines continuity with its various meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the first thing perceived from a body is its continuous extended identity, and that 'continuity' (ittiṣāl) has multiple meanings: relational, non-relational, and as a differentia of quantity.

Core Idea
The first perception of body is an extended identity; 'continuity' has many shades of meaning.

In this passage, the author unpacks what is first perceived from a body—an extended identity that persists regardless of magnitude. He then defines the technical term 'continuity' (ittiṣāl) and distinguishes its various accidental meanings, from relational to non-relational, and finally explains how 'continuous' serves as a differentia for quantity. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for grasping the nature of body in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first perception of body
Read the opening line: the first thing perceived is an extended identity (هوية امتدادية). Notice that it does not depend on any particular magnitude.
2
Understand the primary meaning of 'continuity'
Find where the author defines 'continuity' as the property of a substance in which three dimensions can be imagined. This is the core technical meaning.
3
Distinguish relational and non-relational meanings
The passage lists two relational meanings (moving with another, sharing an endpoint) and one non-relational meaning (common boundary upon division). Note the examples: surface, line, point.
4
See how 'continuous' applies to types of bodies
The continuous is a differentia of quantity (distinguishing magnitude from number). It applies to the educational body, the bodily form, and the natural body. Understand that each has continuity in a specific sense.

وتحقيق ذلك أن أول ما يدرك من الجسم هوية امتدادية لا تنعدم بانعدام مقدار عنها، وحدوث آخر، ولا تعقل حقيقة الجسم دون تعقلها، بل ربما لا يعقل في بادي النظر من الجسم سواها، وهم يسمونها بالاتصال والمتصل بمعنى الجوهر الذي شأنه الاتصال، ويعنون بالاتصال الذي هو شأن ذلك الجوهر كونه بحيث تفرض فيه الأبعاد الثلاثة المتقاطعة الآخذة في الجهات، وإن كان لفظ الاتصال يطلق على معان أخر عرضية إضافية ككون الجسم بحيث يتحرك بحركة جسم آخر، وككون المقدار متحد النهاية بمقدار آخر، كضلعي الزاوية أو غير إضافية، ككون الشيء بحيث إذا فرض انقسامه حدث حد مشترك هو بذاته لأحد قسميه ونهاية للآخر كالسطح لقسمي الجسم والخط لقسمي السطح. والنقطة لقسمي الخط، والمتصل بهذا المعنى فصل للكم يميز أحد نوعيه وهو المقدار عن الآخر، وهو العدد، ويقع على الجسم التعليمي لأنه ذو اتصال بهذا المعنى، وعلى الصورة الجسمية، لأنها ذات اتصال بمعنى الجسم التعليمي، وعلى الجسم الطبيعي بمعنى الصورة الجسمية
And the verification of that is that the first thing perceived from the body is an extended identity (huwiyya imtidādiyya) that does not cease by the cessation of a magnitude from it nor by the occurrence of another, and the reality of the body is not intellected without its intellection; rather, perhaps in the first glance nothing else is intellected from the body besides it. They call it continuity (ittiṣāl) and the continuous (al-muttaṣil) in the sense of the substance whose property is continuity. And they mean by continuity which is the property of that substance that it is such that the three intersecting dimensions extending in directions can be imagined in it, although the term continuity is used for other accidental meanings, relational—like the body being such that it moves with the motion of another body, and like the magnitude being united in endpoint with another magnitude, like the two sides of an angle—or non-relational, like a thing being such that if its division is assumed, a common limit occurs which is by itself one of its two parts and an endpoint for the other, like the surface for the two parts of the body, the line for the two parts of the surface, and the point for the two parts of the line. And the continuous in this sense is a differentia (faṣl) of quantity (al-kamm) distinguishing one of its two types, namely magnitude (al-miqdār), from the other, which is number (al-ʿadad). And it applies to the educational body (al-jism al-taʿlīmī) because it possesses continuity in this sense, and to the bodily form (al-ṣūra al-jismiyya) because it possesses continuity in the sense of the educational body, and to the natural body (al-jism al-ṭabīʿī) in the sense of the bodily form.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage explains that the first thing perceived from a body is an extended identity that does not depend on any particular magnitude. This is called 'continuity' and 'the continuous' in the sense of a substance that can have three dimensions. Then the author distinguishes different meanings of continuity: relational (like moving with another) and non-relational (like having a common boundary). Then he defines the continuous as a differentia of quantity, distinguishing magnitude from number, and shows how it applies to the educational body, bodily form, and natural body.

The First Perceived Identity of Body and Hyle
Al-Sanusi defines the first perceived identity of body and introduces hyle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the first thing perceived of a body is an extending identity (huwiyya imtidadiyya), and that a substance called hyle underlies the reception of continuity and separation.

Core Idea
The body's primary identity is an extending dimension, not an accident.

This passage from al-Sanusi's metaphysical work discusses the foundational concept of body. It presents competing views of Plato and Aristotle regarding the nature of body and introduces the concept of hyle as the substance that receives continuity and separation. The passage then identifies the first perceived identity of body as an extending identity, which is the basis for understanding body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins mid-sentence, referring to 'directions'.
2
Identify the views of Plato and Aristotle
The passage contrasts two views on body: for Plato it is the body itself accepting separation; for Aristotle it is a part residing in another part receptive of separation.
This leads to the need for a substance that receives both continuity and separation.
3
Understand the need for hyle
The argument shows that there must be a substance that remains when continuous identities change; this is called hyle.
Hyle is the underlying matter.
4
Grasp the concept of the first perceived identity
The passage then states that the first thing perceived of a body is an extending identity that is not annihilated by change of magnitude.
This identity is essential for understanding body.
5
Note that this identity is called 'ittisal'
They call this identity 'ittisal' (continuity) and 'mutassil' (continuous) in the sense of a substance whose property is to have three dimensions.
This is the primary meaning of ittisal.

(۱) الكم في الرياضيات هو المقدار، وهو ما يقبل القياس، وقيل إنه الذي يمكن أن يوجد فيه شيء يكون واحداً عادا له سواء كان موجوداً بالفعل أو بالقوة وقيل : إنه عرض يقبل لذاته القسمة والمساواة واللامساواة والزيادة والنقصان . والكم: إما متصل وإما منفصل فالمتصل هو الذي يوجد لأجزائه بالقوة حد مشترك تتلاقى عنده وتتحد به كالنقطة للخط راجع) ابن سينا النجاة ص ١٢٦) امتداداً . فان كانت جميع أجزائه قارة ومجتمعة في الوجود سمي وان كانت غير مجتمعة زماناً. والمنفصل هو الذي لا يوجد لأجزائه بالقوة ولا بالفعل حد مشترك كالعدد. وكمية العــدد في المنطق ما صدقه والكم في علم ما بعد الطبيعة مقابل للكيف، وهو من مقولات العقل الأساسية، والكمي هو المنسوب الى الكم تقول : مذهب اللذات الكمي.

سمي
In the directions, it is not outside the reality of body, but according to Plato and his followers it is the body itself, and it accepts separation by itself; according to Aristotle and his followers it is a part of it residing in another part which is the receptive of separation, because the receptive must be together with the received, and continuity [and] separation, so there must be a substance receptive of continuity and separation [and] remains. It is impossible that it remains with both of them, and the varying continuous identities by individual change upon it, and it is called hyle. And the substance residing in the bodily form, and the verification of that is that the first thing perceived of the body is an extending identity (huwiyya imtidadiyya) that is not annihilated by the annihilation of a magnitude from it, nor by the occurrence of another, and the reality of the body is not understood without understanding it; rather, perhaps nothing else is understood in the initial view of the body besides it. And they call it 'ittisal' and 'mutassil' (continuous) meaning the substance whose property is continuity, and they mean by continuity which is the property of that substance that it is such that the three intersecting dimensions extending in the directions are assumed in it. However, the word 'ittisal' is applied to other accidental relational meanings, such as the body being such that it moves with the movement of another body, and such as the magnitude sharing an endpoint with another magnitude, like the two sides of an angle, or non-relational, such that when a thing is assumed divided, a common boundary occurs that is itself one of its two parts and an endpoint of the other, like the surface for the two parts of the body and the line for the two parts of the surface, and the point for the two parts of the line. And the continuous in this sense is a differentia for quantity (1) distinguishing one of its two types, which is magnitude, from the other, which is number. And it applies to the mathematical body because it has continuity in this sense, and to the corporeal form because it has continuity in the sense of the mathematical body, and to the natural body in the sense of the corporeal form. (2) * (1) Quantity in mathematics is magnitude, which accepts measurement; and it is said that it is that in which a thing can exist that is one counting it, whether it exists actually or potentially; and it is said that it is an accident that accepts by itself division, equality, inequality, increase and decrease. Quantity is either continuous or discrete. The continuous is that for whose parts there exists potentially a common boundary at which they meet and unite, like the point for the line (see Ibn Sina, al-Najat p. 126) as an extension. If all its parts are stationary and gathered in existence, it is called ; if they are not gathered in time. The discrete is that for whose parts there does not exist, potentially or actually, a common boundary, like number. The quantity of number in logic is the extension of its concept, and quantity in metaphysics is opposite to quality, and it is one of the fundamental categories of the intellect, and the quantitive is attributed to quantity: you say 'the doctrine of quantitive pleasures'. (2) What is in brackets is missing from (b). sumi
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage defines the first perceived identity of body and distinguishes various meanings of 'ittisal' (continuity). It begins by discussing the nature of body in relation to directions, presenting the views of Plato and Aristotle: for Plato and his followers, body is the same as its receptive continuity; for Aristotle and his followers, body has a part that is receptive of separation. This leads to the necessity of a substance receptive of continuity and separation, which remains when continuity changes. This substance is called hyle (hayula). The passage then states that the first thing perceived of a body is an extending identity (huwiyya imtidadiyya) that is not annihilated by the loss or gain of magnitude, and without which the body's reality is not understood. This identity is called 'ittisal' and 'mutassil' in the sense of a substance whose property is continuity, meaning that three intersecting dimensions can be assumed in it. However, the word 'ittisal' has other accidental meanings—relational (e.g., a body moving with another) and non-relational (e.g., a common boundary like surface, line, point). In its primary sense as a differentia of quantity, 'ittisal' distinguishes magnitude from number. It applies to the mathematical body, the corporeal form, and the natural body in terms of the corporeal form. The footnote clarifies the definition of quantity and its types.

Correction: The passage argues that the first perceived identity is an extending identity (huwiyya imtidadiyya), which is not an accident but a primary attribute of body without which its reality is not understood.

Could it be that you are mistaking an accident for the essence? What would it mean to live from your true substance as a servant of Allah rather than from transient attributes?
?
Plato and Aristotle both sought the fundamental nature of physical reality. How earnestly do you seek the fundamental nature of your own soul and your relationship with your Creator? Are you satisfied with surface-level explanations, or do you push deeper until you find the truth that remains when all else changes? Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate

The Various Meanings of 'Ittisal' and Its Logical Role
Al-Sanusi classifies the accidental meanings of 'ittisal' and defines its logical role

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different meanings of 'ittisal' (continuity) and its role as a differentia distinguishing magnitude from number in the category of quantity.

Core Idea
'Ittisal' is a key term with multiple meanings, primarily distinguishing continuous quantity from discrete number.

After defining the primary sense of 'ittisal' as the property of a substance that can receive three dimensions, al-Sanusi notes that the term has other accidental meanings. He classifies them into relational and non-relational, and then explains that in its primary sense, 'ittisal' serves as a differentia for quantity, dividing it into continuous (magnitude) and discrete (number). This lesson unpacks these distinctions and their applications.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of meanings
Read the passage that lists various meanings of 'ittisal'. Notice the distinction between relational (idadiyya) and non-relational (ghayr idafiyya) meanings.
Relational meanings involve another body or magnitude; non-relational involve a common boundary.
2
Distinguish relational and non-relational meanings
Relational: a body moving with another, or magnitudes sharing an endpoint. Non-relational: a common boundary resulting from division (surface, line, point).
These are accidental meanings, not the primary sense.
3
Focus on the meaning as a common boundary
The non-relational meaning is crucial: when a thing is divided, there is a common boundary that is part of both divisions. This defines continuous quantity.
Examples: surface for body, line for surface, point for line.
4
Understand 'ittisal' as a differentia of quantity
In this sense, 'ittisal' distinguishes magnitude (continuous quantity) from number (discrete quantity). Magnitude has common boundaries; number does not.
This is the logical definition of continuous quantity.
5
Note the applications to bodies
This meaning applies to the mathematical body (jism ta'limi), the corporeal form (sura jismiyya), and the natural body (jism tabi'i) in terms of the corporeal form.
The mathematical body has continuity in this sense; the corporeal form has continuity as mathematical body.

(2) * (1) Quantity in mathematics is magnitude, which accepts measurement; and it is said that it is that in which a thing can exist that is one counting it, whether it exists actually or potentially; and it is said that it is an accident that accepts by itself division, equality, inequality, increase and decrease. The continuous is that for whose parts there exists potentially a common boundary at which they meet and unite, like the point for the line (see Ibn Sina, al-Najat p. 126) as an extension. If all its parts are stationary and gathered in existence, it is called ; if they are not gathered in time. The discrete is that for whose parts there does not exist, potentially or actually, a common boundary, like number. The quantity of number in logic is the extension of its concept, and quantity in metaphysics is opposite to quality, and it is one of the fundamental categories of the intellect, and the quantitive is attributed to quantity: you say 'the doctrine of quantitive pleasures'. (2) What is in brackets is missing from (b). sumi
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage defines the first perceived identity of body and distinguishes various meanings of 'ittisal' (continuity). It begins by discussing the nature of body in relation to directions, presenting the views of Plato and Aristotle: for Plato and his followers, body is the same as its receptive continuity; for Aristotle and his followers, body has a part that is receptive of separation. This leads to the necessity of a substance receptive of continuity and separation, which remains when continuity changes. This substance is called hyle (hayula). The passage then states that the first thing perceived of a body is an extending identity (huwiyya imtidadiyya) that is not annihilated by the loss or gain of magnitude, and without which the body's reality is not understood. This identity is called 'ittisal' and 'mutassil' in the sense of a substance whose property is continuity, meaning that three intersecting dimensions can be assumed in it. However, the word 'ittisal' has other accidental meanings—relational (e.g., a body moving with another) and non-relational (e.g., a common boundary like surface, line, point). In its primary sense as a differentia of quantity, 'ittisal' distinguishes magnitude from number. It applies to the mathematical body, the corporeal form, and the natural body in terms of the corporeal form. The footnote clarifies the definition of quantity and its types.

Key Terms
الاتصال
الانفصال
الكم
المقدار
العدد
الجسم التعليمي
الجسم الطبيعي
الصورة الجسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'ittisal' as a differentia of quantity distinguish?
A) Body from soul
B) Magnitude from number
C) Substance from accident
D) Surface from line

Key Takeaway
The primary technical sense of 'ittisal' as a common boundary defines continuous quantity and distinguishes magnitude from number.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you, do you tend to perceive things as continuous wholes (like a smooth surface) or as collections of discrete parts (like a number of separate objects)? How does Al-Sanusi’s distinction between magnitude and number challenge or refine your natural tendency? Take a moment today to observe something you normally treat as continuous—like time, space, or even your own stream of thoughts—and ask yourself: is it truly continuous, or is it composed of discrete units? Commit to examining one assumption you have about continuity before the end of this week.
?
The concept of 'common boundary' arises when something is divided—you cannot have a boundary without dividing what was once whole. In your own life, where have you drawn boundaries (e.g., between work and rest, between your past and present self) that you now see as artificial? How might acknowledging the original continuity change how you relate to those divisions? Write down one boundary you will consciously try to soften today and observe what happens.
?
Al-Sanusi uses 'ittisal' as a precise technical term that separates magnitude from number. But in your daily speech, you likely use the word 'continuous' loosely. Identify one situation today where your casual use of 'continuous' might actually hide a deeper truth (e.g., 'continuous effort' might be made of discrete moments). How can being more precise in your language bring you closer to the reality of God’s creation? Make a commitment to define one term more carefully in your next conversation.

Definition of Hyle as the Persisting Subject
Definition of hyle as the persisting subject in both continuity and discontinuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic philosophical concept of hyle as the pure potentiality that persists through changes of unity and multiplicity.

Core Idea
Hyle is a pure potentiality, not a substance, that remains when bodies connect or separate.

In this passage, the author clarifies a key concept in natural philosophy: what underlies the change when a body splits or merges. He argues that there is a persistent substratum called hyle, which is pure potentiality. This lesson unpacks his argument and the analogy of water.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the author's use of the water analogy.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the problem
The author states that the continuous identity does not remain when discontinuity occurs; it perishes. Yet we know something persists. Identify this problem.
This sets up the need for hyle.
3
Examine the water analogy
The example of water in a jar vs. in multiple pitchers illustrates how the same matter underlies different configurations.
The water itself does not change, only its shape and container.
4
Grasp hyle as pure potentiality
The author defines the persisting thing as hyle, which is pure potentiality, not inherently one or many.
This is a key metaphysical point.
5
Note the role of inherence
Unity and continuity come from the inherence of the continuous form; separation and multiplicity come from discontinuity affecting hyle.
Hyle itself is passive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لاخفاء في أن تلك الهوية الاتصالية لا تبقى بنفسها عند طريان الانفصال، بل تنعدم ويحدث هويتان أخريان مع القطع بأنه يبقى في حالتي الاتصال والانفصال أمر واحد وهو القابل لهما بالذات للفرق الضروري بين أن ينعدم جسم بكليته، ويحدث جسمان آخران، أو ينعدم جسمان ويحدث جسم ثالث، وبين أن ينفصل جسم فيصير جسمين، أو يتصل جسمان فيصير جسماً واحداً، كماء الجرة يجعل في كيزان أو ماء الكيزان يجعل في جرة، فذلك أن الأمر الباقي في الحالين هو المراد بالهيولي، وهو استعداد محض ليس في نفسه بواحد، ومتصل ليمتنع طريان الكثرة والانفصال عليه، مع بقائه بحاله ولا كثير ومنفصل ليمتنع طريان الاتصال عليه، بل وحدته واتصاله بحلول الصورة الاتصالية فيه، وانفصاله وكثرته بطريان الانفصال عليه.
Then there is no concealment that that continuous identity does not remain by itself when discontinuity occurs, but perishes and two other identities arise, while it is certain that there remains in both states of continuity and discontinuity one thing which is the receptive subject for both by nature, due to the necessary distinction between a body perishing entirely and two other bodies arising, or two bodies perishing and a third body arising, and between a body separating and becoming two bodies, or two bodies connecting and becoming one body, like water from a jar put into pitchers or water from pitchers put into a jar. So that remaining thing in both states is what is meant by hyle (الهيولي), and it is a pure potentiality (استعداد محض), not in itself one and continuous to prevent the occurrence of multiplicity and discontinuity upon it while remaining as it is, nor multiple and separate to prevent the occurrence of continuity upon it. Rather, its unity and continuity are by the inherence (حلول) of the continuous form (الصورة الاتصالية) in it, and its separation and multiplicity by the occurrence of discontinuity upon it.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defines hyle (الهيولي) as the underlying subject that persists when a body undergoes continuity or discontinuity. The author argues that when a body separates, the continuous identity perishes and two new ones arise, yet there is a common substratum that remains, analogous to water poured from one container into multiple ones. This substratum is pure potentiality, devoid of actual unity or multiplicity. It is not inherently one or many; rather, unity and continuity result from the inherence of the continuous form, while separation and multiplicity result from the occurrence of discontinuity. The passage emphasizes that hyle is not a substance with its own attributes but a receptive potentiality that takes on the properties of either connection or disconnection depending on the form that inheres in it.

Key Terms
الهيولي (hyle)
الهوية الاتصالية (continuous identity)
الصورة الاتصالية (continuous form)
استعداد محض (pure potentiality)
حلول (inherence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hyle is a substance with its own identity and attributes.
Correction: The author clarifies that hyle is pure potentiality (استعداد محض), not in itself one or many, and its properties come from the inherence of forms.

Checkpoint
What is the 'thing' that remains in both states of continuity and discontinuity according to the author?
A) The continuous identity itself
B) Hyle (الهيولي)
C) The bodily form
D) The volume of the body

Key Takeaway
The persistence of a common subject (hyle) in both connection and disconnection shows that hyle is pure potentiality, not a substance with fixed properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have been through many changes in your life—different moods, beliefs, relationships. Is there a constant 'you' that remains, or are you just the sum of your changes? This lesson says hyle is pure potentiality, not a fixed thing. If your identity is not tied to any particular form, what form are you most afraid to let go of? Identify one attachment to a 'form' in your life that you need to surrender to Allah, and commit to one action that detaches you from it today.
?
The water analogy shows that the same water takes the shape of any container it is poured into. What 'container'—such as a role, reputation, or habit—are you allowing to define who you are? How would your relationship with Allah change if you saw yourself as pure receptivity to His commands, rather than a fixed personality? Write down one specific way you can be more malleable to Allah's guidance this week.

Objection on Continuous Identity as First Perceived and Its Answer
Objection and answer on continuous identity as first perceived.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that continuous identity is denied by mutakallimun and the author's response distinguishing agreed and disputed points.

Core Idea
The dispute is not about the existence of extended substance, but about its unity and role in body composition.

This passage addresses a critical objection to the claim that continuous identity (substantial extension) is the first perceived aspect of corporeality. The objector notes that mutakallimun and many philosophers deny it. The author clarifies what is actually denied and what is universally accepted, providing a precise theological distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the objection as presented: 'For you say...' Understand why the objector thinks continuous identity cannot be the first perceived.
The objector equates continuous identity with substantial extension.
2
Identify the author's distinction
In the answer, notice the author first states what is agreed upon: the existence of a substance characterized by extension. Then he identifies what is actually disputed.
The agreed point is the existence of extended substance, perceived via accidents.
3
Understand the two points of dispute
The author lists two disputed issues: (1) whether the substance is one continuous entity as perceived, and (2) whether it constitutes the entire body. Also note the clarification about accidental quantities.
Accidental quantities are denied by mutakallimun as non-existent ends.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: الهوية الاتصالية بمعنى الامتداد الجوهري مما أنكره المتكلمون وكثير من الفلاسفة، فكيف يصح دعوى كونها أول ما يدرك من جوهرية الجسم؟ وإنما ذلك هو المقادير والامتدادات العرضية. قلنا: لا نزاع في ثبوت جوهر شأنه الامتداد والاتصال، وفي كونه مدركاً بالحس ولو بواسطة ما يقوم به من الأعراض وإنما النزاع في أنه هل هو في نفس الأمر متصل واحد كما هو عند الحس أم لا؟ وعلى الأول هل هو تمام الجسم أم لا؟ بل يفتقر إلى جزء آخر يتوارد عليه الاتصال والانفصال وأما الامتدادات العرضية، أعني المقادير فهي التي أنكرها المتكلمون وكثير من الفلاسفة . أعني القائلين بأنها أمور عدمية لكونها نهايات وانقطاعات، فالسطح للجسم، والخط للسطح، والنقطة للخط.
For you say: The continuous identity in the sense of substantial extension (الامتداد الجوهري) is something the mutakallimun (المتكلمون) and many philosophers have denied, so how can it be claimed that it is the first thing perceived of corporeality? Rather, that is the accidental quantities and extensions (المقادير والامتدادات العرضية). We say: There is no dispute about the existence of a substance whose property is extension and continuity, and that it is perceived by sense, even if through the accidents that subsist in it. Rather, the dispute is whether it is in itself one continuous as it is perceived by sense or not? And on the first view, is it the entire body or not? Rather, it requires another part upon which continuity and discontinuity alternate. As for accidental extensions, i.e., quantities (المقادير), these are what the mutakallimun and many philosophers denied, i.e., those who say they are non-existent things because they are ends and discontinuities: the surface (السطح) for the body, the line (الخط) for the surface, and the point (النقطة) for the line.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector claims that the continuous identity (substantial extension) is denied by the mutakallimun and many philosophers, so it cannot be what is first perceived of corporeality; rather, accidental quantities and extensions are what is perceived. The author answers by distinguishing what is agreed upon from what is disputed. He states that there is no doubt about the existence of a substance characterized by extension and continuity, and that it is perceived by the senses, even if mediated by accidents. The real dispute is twofold: (1) whether this substance is in itself one continuous entity as sense perception suggests, or not; (2) if it is one, whether it constitutes the entire body or requires another part that undergoes continuity and discontinuity. The author then clarifies that the accidental extensions (quantities) are what the mutakallimun and many philosophers deny, as they consider them nonexistent things (umur 'adamiyya) because they are merely ends and discontinuities, such as surface, line, and point.

Key Terms
الهوية الاتصالية (continuous identity)
الامتداد الجوهري (substantial extension)
المقادير العرضية (accidental quantities)
جوهر (substance)
جوهرية الجسم (corporeality)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author endorses the denial of substantial extension, or that the mutakallimun deny extension entirely.
Correction: The author affirms the existence of a substance with extension, noting that the dispute is about its unity and whether it constitutes the whole body, not about its existence. The denial by mutakallimun concerns accidental quantities, not substantial extension.

Checkpoint
According to the author's answer, what is the real point of dispute regarding the continuous identity?
A) Whether it is perceived by sense
B) Whether it is a substance at all
C) Whether it is one in itself and whether it is the whole body
D) Whether it is accidental or substantial

Key Takeaway
The author affirms the existence of substance with extension while clarifying that the real disputes concern its unity and whether it constitutes the whole body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own body, do you naturally perceive it as one continuous whole or as a collection of parts? How does your immediate perception align with the philosophical dispute described in this lesson? What does that dissonance teach you about the reliability of your senses without rational scrutiny?
?
The author distinguishes between what is agreed upon (existence of substance with extension) and what is disputed (unity and composition). In your life, are there arguments you engage in where the real point of disagreement is different from what you initially think? Take a moment to identify one such situation and ask yourself: Have I properly separated what is agreed from what is actually disputed?
?
The objector assumes that if some deny continuous identity, it cannot be first perceived. How often do you reject a truth simply because others deny it, without examining the nuances? Can you recall a time when you dismissed something prematurely based on opposition? How could this lesson's careful distinction prevent that mistake in the future?
